# Glass Podium

# Chapter 1

The shuttle from Meiringen climbed the switchbacks in low gear, and Kai sat in the back with his bag between his knees and watched the road fold away behind each turn. Snow packed both shoulders of the road, and the guardrails were crusted white. The driver hadn't spoken since the valley floor, just worked the wheel through the hairpins with loose shoulders and bored accuracy, each turn arriving in his hands before the shuttle reached it. A small mercy. Kai had used up his supply of pleasant talk on the Zurich connection, chatting with a woman who'd recognized him from a Meridian promo spot and wanted to know what it felt like. Running enhanced. Like running, he'd told her. But faster. She'd laughed as if he'd made a joke.

The altitude registered at the second hairpin. Not dramatic, just a tightness behind his sternum, breath becoming labor instead of habit at two thousand and change meters. His lungs would catch up in a few days. The spinal neural bridge had already caught up. If he paid attention, he could feel a faint warmth running along his back, the hardware recalibrating his oxygen distribution before his body had finished registering the deficit. The bridge was always ahead of him. That was the point.

Snow-covered firs lined the road, bowed under weight, thinning as the altitude climbed. The light was going. Late January in the Bernese Alps meant the sun started quitting around four, and the sky above the ridgeline was already the pale, exhausted grey of a winter afternoon giving up early. Kai pressed his forehead against the cold window and watched the tree line drop away.

The Kirchberg Institute appeared around the final curve. A campus of concrete and glass set into the mountainside: training halls, residential blocks, a medical wing with windows catching the last of the daylight. Not beautiful, but functional, the kind of place that assumed you were there to work and had designed every surface accordingly. Floodlights had already come on along the approach road, and the facility's glass facade reflected them back in flat panels of white that made the building look like it was generating its own weather.

The shuttle stopped at the main entrance. Kai stepped out and the cold hit his face, the dry metallic cold of high altitude, thinner than Indiana winter, sharper, with none of the damp heaviness that meant snow was actually coming. This cold was just the air being honest about its temperature. Negative twelve, maybe. His breath came out solid.

Inside, the lobby was warm, carpeted, aggressively neutral. A receptionist scanned his credentials and handed him a room key and an orientation packet in a folder embossed with the Meridian BioSystems logo. The folder was thick enough to suggest corporate believed paperwork could do the work of conversation.

"Dinner's at seven in the cafeteria. Dr. Qadir will see you for your initial calibration at eight."

"Great. Thanks."

"Welcome to Kirchberg, Mr. Brennan."

Corridor signs led him to the residential wing. The corridor was fluorescent-lit, with doors on both sides, each fitted with a small digital display showing room status. Most were blank, dark rooms, empty, or athletes who'd disabled their readouts. A few showed scrolling biometric data visible through half-open doors. Heart rate, integration scores, neural latency. The casual broadcast of what would have been private medical information anywhere else. Here it was like leaving your shoes outside your door.

In one doorway, a woman was stretching, legs in a split that ran the full width of the frame with the mechanical precision of a compass opening. She nodded at him without interrupting, her calves showing the same engineered definition his did, bilateral symmetry too clean to be the product of genetics and training alone. Her eyes followed him without flicker, too smooth by half. He had the brief, stupid sense that she was seeing him in more frames than he was seeing her. Kai nodded back and kept walking.

The corridor cut through a connecting wing between residential and medical. Through a glass partition he could see a corner of what had to be the calibration bay: reclining chairs, monitor banks, the blue standby glow of diagnostic screens in a room that looked unoccupied. The hum was louder here, a bass throb pushing up through the floor so that by the time he heard it, it was already in his sternum and the arches of his feet. Further on, a second glass wall overlooked the pool deck: fifty meters of chlorinated water under flat white light, the surface so still it looked solid. A swimmer in a green cap sat on the far edge with her feet in the water, not moving, not preparing, just sitting there and letting the delay happen.

The room was spartan and adequate. It had a bed, a desk, a bathroom, and a window facing the eastern ridge. The wall-mounted diagnostic display was already active, scrolling data from his enhancement system: heart rate, core temperature, synthetic muscle integration, neural bridge latency. All green, everything functioning. His bag landed on the bed, and he went to the window. Snow fell in the floodlights' periphery. The mountains beyond were shapes now, losing detail to the dark. Warm air kept hissing through the vent over the door, a dry mechanical breath that made the room feel occupied even with nobody else in it.

He'd been to training facilities before, Colorado Springs, the Meridian campus outside Portland, altitude work in Flagstaff. They'd all had the same basic architecture of purpose: track, gym, medical, cafeteria, sleep. But those places had natural athletes mixed in with the enhanced, and the mix created a visible gradient you could use to locate yourself. You knew where you stood. At Kirchberg, there was no gradient. Every body in the building was rebuilt. Enhancement wasn't the variable here. It was the baseline. The woman doing splits in her doorway hadn't been showing off. She'd been stretching before dinner.

Kai sat on the bed and ran his thumb along the inside of his left thigh, pressing until he found the seam. The synthetic muscle graft sat under the skin without a scar to mark it, but the texture changed where engineered fiber met natural tissue. A faint ridge ran from mid-thigh to just above the knee. The right leg had the same seam in the same place. Below the knee, the calf grafts had their own. Bilateral symmetry. His legs looked better than they ever had when they were entirely his, the proportions a little too balanced, the muscle definition a little too even, legs a sculptor would give an athlete who existed only in reference photos.

Three years since the reconstruction. Two ACLs and a spinal compression fracture at twenty-seven, the kind of injury that ended natural careers because natural bodies didn't come back from it, not fully, not to world-record form. Meridian had offered the rebuild six weeks after the surgery. Full reconstruction: synthetic grafts in both legs, spinal neural bridge to replace the compromised nerve pathways, visual processing enhancement for reaction time. He'd signed in a hospital bed in Indianapolis with his mother sitting in the corner not saying anything and his agent on speaker saying everything.

Now he was faster than he'd been at twenty-five, when the 200-meter record had been his and the body had been too.

***

The indoor track was a 200-meter oval under high ceilings with acoustic dampening that didn't quite work. Footfalls carried, each one a clean report off the rubber surface followed by a fast, shallow echo. Kai walked in at nine the next morning and the sound of other athletes warming up was like listening to rain on a drum. Four runners were on the infield stretching and jogging short bursts, and a coach stood by the finish with a tablet and a stopwatch. The air was altitude-controlled and warmer than outside, and it smelled of rubber and the particular chemical cleanliness of a facility that was maintained rather than lived in.

In the locker room, he changed and worked through his warm-up sequence: hip flexors, hamstrings, calves. The rebuilt sections stretched differently than natural tissue. There was compliance but no complaint, a willingness in the synthetic fibers that felt cooperative rather than resistant. His real quads still ached when he pushed too deep. The engineered sections just extended, obliging, as though the muscle had already read the script.

The qualifying heat was a formality: five runners, posted times, a gate officer with a starter pistol and an unhurried manner that said he'd still be standing there long after Kai was done. Kai set his blocks at the line and sank into the crouch, hands on the rubber, weight forward on his fingertips. His visual processing enhancement activated without being asked. The lane ahead sharpened, depth of field narrowing, peripheral detail dimming to irrelevance. It had triggered before the conscious decision to trigger it. The system had read the posture, the setting, the context, and done what it always did at a start line.

The gun fired.

Out of the blocks, his legs drove him into the curve. The synthetic muscle fired in a sequenced cascade, calf, quad, hip, and the power was clean and immediate and felt like his own effort in the same way that driving a car fast felt like running: the speed was real, the direction was yours, and somewhere in the middle was an engine doing the parts you couldn't. Through the curve, leaning, the centrifugal force was managed by the hardware low in his hips and lower back, anticipating the angle before he thought about banking. The rubber passed beneath him in a blur of red-brown. Into the straight now, full speed, and this was where the quiet lived. There was no burn in his legs, no oxygen debt building in his chest, no negotiation between what the lungs could supply and what the muscles demanded. Just his body moving at a speed that should have felt like violence, feet barely touching the surface, arms pumping with a rhythm he wasn't setting, and felt instead like coasting. Like being carried very fast in a direction he'd agreed to.

A stride later he was through the line. His body decelerated through the curve past the finish, and the transition from sprint to jog was seamless: no shock, no buckled stride, no sudden announcement of oxygen debt. The rebuilt legs downshifted smoothly, and his breathing barely changed. The natural version of him used to finish the 200 and fold over, hands on knees, lungs screaming, vision spotted at the edges. That was the natural version. Now he ran the fastest 200 meters of his life and his body's first response was to inform him, politely, that it could go again.

The clock said 19.02.

Personal best by three hundredths. The number held on the overhead board long enough for him to think he'd read it wrong, then an amber box lit around lane four. REVIEW appeared under the time in German, French, and English. Closed qualifiers at Kirchberg didn't touch any record book; the system kept them internal until it verified the start blocks, lane pickup, and graft telemetry against the athlete's calibration file. Too much Meridian money sat in the walls here for a number like that to pass as just a number.

Now the gate officer looked up properly. So did the coach by the finish.

"Hold there, Brennan," the coach called. "Don't disappear yet."

Kai stopped at the edge of the track.

The gate officer's thumb moved over the tablet. "Start was clean. Block pressure normal. Lane sensors clean."

"Telemetry sync?" the coach asked.

"Pending medical cross-check."

The coach glanced at Kai, then at the board again. It wasn't admiration exactly. More like the sharp attention people gave an expensive machine when it started behaving in a new way.

"Fine," he said. "We'll log it provisional till medical clears the pull. Qadir will get the split. You're bumped into seeded section tomorrow."

"Okay."

One of the runners still coming off the bend gave a short laugh that sounded more startled than amused. The hurdler from Houston jogged past a few seconds later, breathing harder than Kai was, looked up at the board, and said, "Jesus. Nice run."

"Thanks."

The coach was already speaking into his headset by then, walking toward the finish table. Two staff on the infield had turned to their screens. The board kept the amber box around lane four while his breathing sat there, ordinary, giving him nothing.

Kai stood at the edge of the track with his hands on his hips and tried to locate the race in his body. The usual markers weren't there. No residual burn in the quads, no trembling in the hamstrings, no gratitude from his lungs for the air they were now getting. His heart rate was settling into a rhythm that felt more like waking from a nap than recovering from a flat-out sprint. He'd been in the blocks ten seconds ago. Now he was standing here, breathing normally, and the distance between those two facts felt like someone had edited out the middle of a film and left him watching the credits.

On the cool-down curve, his heart rate was already settling. The spinal bridge managed recovery the way a thermostat managed temperature: smooth, automatic, without consulting him. Sweat cooled on the back of his neck. The rubber under his shoes had a faint chemical bite to it, and he focused on that smell because it was just his senses doing their own work, his nose being a nose, and even that felt thin, the sensation arriving and departing with less weight than it should have carried.

Nineteen-oh-two. Faster than anything he'd run in his natural career. Faster than the version of him that had set world records on legs made of what he'd been born with. The number was correct and the clock was correct and his legs had done the work and his lungs had done the work and his spine had done the work and all of it was functioning as designed and he had been present for the entire ten-point-odd seconds of it.

What he couldn't tell was which parts had been his idea.

After a shower and a change of clothes, he ate lunch in the cafeteria. Institutional food: grilled chicken, rice, vegetables steamed past the point of having personality. The windows showed mountains in midday winter light, the sun a flat white presence behind a veil of cloud that hadn't committed to being overcast. The upper ridges held the light for a moment longer than the valleys, white against grey. Athletes ate in clusters. The conversations Kai overheard were ordinary: sleep quality, supplement protocols, the facility's ongoing hot-water problem. A woman two tables over was arguing quietly on her phone about a missed delivery. A young guy eating alone, twenty-one maybe, had a decathlete's broad shoulders and the slightly unfinished proportions of a body still settling around the rebuild. He was reading his tablet with the dutiful concentration of pre-meet homework. An energy bar lay half-eaten beside his plate, forgotten in favor of whatever he was reading.

Kai's phone buzzed. His mother, texting from Lafayette. A photo of snow on the front porch, the specific yellow-grey Indiana snow that clung to everything, covering the porch railing and the mailbox and the edge of the welcome mat she'd had since he was in high school.

*Probably colder here than where you are*

He typed: *Doubt it. 2100 meters up here*

*Is that high? I don't know what that is*

*About 7000 feet. Cold*

*How was the run?*

*Good. New personal best*

*That's wonderful honey. Call me this week ok?*

He set the phone down. His right hand was resting on the table and then it wasn't. His fingers cinched around the water glass while he was still looking at his mother's last text. By the time the wanting caught up, his wrist had already turned. The bottom of the glass dragged a wet crescent through the condensation ring and clipped one of his front teeth on the way up. Cold water spilled across his lower lip. He swallowed too fast, felt it hit the back of his throat wrong, and set the glass down harder than he'd meant to. A few drops jumped onto his knuckles.

He looked at his hand.

Nothing anybody else would have seen. A fraction of a second, maybe less. But his pulse had kicked once, hard enough that he felt it in his throat, and his palm stayed braced on the table as if the hand might try again. Six months ago, or three months ago, the motion would have slipped past him. He'd started noticing it in Portland, and then in Flagstaff, and now here, at a table in a cafeteria in the Swiss Alps, the motion arriving a fraction ahead of the wanting.

***

The calibration bay was in the medical wing, a clean room with fluorescent tubes and the permanent low hum of processing equipment, a sound that lived in the walls, below the pitch of conversation but too present to be called silence. Kai had been told you stopped hearing it after a week. A day in, he still heard every cycle.

Yusuf Qadir was setting up when Kai arrived at eight. Short, dark-haired, in real prescription glasses, he was one of the few people at the facility who'd declined enhancement for himself. Around the calibration rig, Yusuf moved without looking for anything, adjusting monitor angles, tapping commands at a console, a grey diagnostic cable looped over one shoulder that he hadn't found a use for yet.

"Brennan. Come in, sit down." He gestured at the rig, a reclining chair boxed in by screens and connector ports, a setup that could have passed for a dentist's chair if dentists needed six monitors and a firmware interface. "How was the trip in?"

"Long."

"Always is. Zurich connection?"

"Yeah."

"God, yeah, that layover. Right, so." He patted the chair's headrest. "First calibration since Portland, which means I need a full baseline. About forty-five minutes, maybe an hour if your graft firmware's been running updates I don't know about. You good with that?"

"I'm good."

Kai settled into the chair. The surface was medical-grade vinyl, body-temperature, shaped to distribute his weight evenly across his back and legs. Yusuf attached sensor pads to his legs, inner thigh, outer calf, just above both ankles, his hands moving without hesitation, each pad finding its place before Kai had finished registering the last touch. Then he pressed a flat contact pad against the small of Kai's back so the rig could sync with the bridge through the skin. The pad caught with a soft click, and Kai felt a brief, clean pressure at the base of his spine, a sensation that would have been uncomfortable if it lasted longer than a second.

"You'll feel a handshake. That's me saying hi to your bridge."

"Felt it."

"Brilliant. Right." Yusuf dropped into his chair and scrolled through the incoming data, his face lit by the console's blue-white glow. In that light, he had the focused, slightly rumpled look of an engineer at the end of a long day who still had three things left to do and was prioritizing by interest rather than urgency. "Any changes since Portland? Pain, numbness, anything with range of motion?"

"No. Everything's been fine."

"Good. Graft integration's solid, both legs. Your calf firmware pushed an update last week. Did you catch that?"

"I felt a warmth in my lower legs for about ten minutes. Wednesday, I think."

"Wednesday, yeah, that tracks. Routine optimization pass. Nothing to worry about, it just recalibrated your calf response to some updated baseline data. Happens automatically." He scrolled further. "Bridge latency's within spec. Visual processing nominal. How's the visual been? Any artifacts?"

"Like what?"

"Halos around light sources, depth-of-field lag, colour banding. Some athletes get this thing where the world goes slightly flat for a second. Hard to describe. Like someone compressed the depth out of it."

"No, nothing like that."

"Good." Yusuf tapped a command and the sensor pads on Kai's legs came alive with a low vibration. "I'm going to map your graft response times. This bit's automatic. Just try to stay relaxed."

He stayed still. A calibration pulse moved through his left calf, a contraction he hadn't initiated, the muscle tightening and releasing in a wave that ran from ankle to knee. Then the right calf. Then both quads, one after the other. Then the hamstrings. His body twitched and responded and twitched again, and Kai lay there looking at the ceiling tiles and let it happen. Each pulse was a small theft, his muscle obeying someone else's instruction, and each time the muscle released, the control returned to him. Each time control returned he noticed the seam of the handover. The system giving him back what was already his.

This was the part that never made the interviews. The lying still. The being adjusted. The specific intimacy of letting another person tune the parts of your body you couldn't reach yourself, while you stared at the ceiling and felt your legs twitch on a schedule you didn't set.

Yusuf worked in focused silence for a while, scrolling data, occasionally leaning over to reposition a sensor pad. Under his breath he made small sounds of acknowledgment, not words, just the audible equivalent of nodding at the data on a screen. When he moved a sensor on Kai's thigh, his fingers found the seam by touch, without looking.

"Right, so," Yusuf said after about twenty minutes. "One more series. Voluntary this time. Left ankle. Don't rush it. Just do it when you mean to."

Kai kept his eyes on the ceiling. For a second nothing happened. Then the back of his calf went tight and his heel dragged a fraction against the chair before the internal yes had finished arriving. The foot flexed. He let it fall back.

"You feel that?" Yusuf asked.

"Yeah."

"Again."

Kai waited longer, stubborn now, trying to catch the exact start. The muscle loaded under the skin with the same clean eagerness, and his foot was already moving by the time the decision turned into language in his head.

Yusuf angled the console screen toward him. Numbers, graphs, a timeline. Two trend lines, one blue, one orange, that had been tracking close together six months ago and were now separating. The gap between them was small but consistent, and it widened at the right edge of the graph.

His tone had shifted. Still easy, still conversational, but there was a weight under it now, the way a voice changes when the data confirms bad news.

"Your proprioception scores are drifting."

"That's what this is."

"Yeah." He pointed at the gap. "Blue line is conscious motor intent. That's the signal your brain generates when you decide to move. Orange is execution. In a healthy integrated system, they should overlap. Over the last three months, orange has started edging ahead. Forty to fifty milliseconds."

"So my body starts before I do."

"Not exactly. The predictive layer is supposed to lean forward. That's how it strips latency out. It sees the beginning of a pattern and primes the rest. Usually the lead disappears inside the motion. What you just felt in your ankle is that lead getting big enough to notice. In a sprint, that's where your start times come from. Most people never feel it."

"And mine isn't invisible anymore."

"The window's widening. At ten milliseconds, nobody feels it. At fifty, some people start to notice. You're at fifty and trending up." He sat back, took off his glasses, rubbed the bridge of his nose with his thumb and forefinger. Put them back on. "Have you noticed anything?"

"Yeah."

"When?"

"Small transitions. Getting out of a chair. Rolling over in bed. First step when I've only just decided to stand. Nothing dramatic, just enough to make me stop and check whether I actually told myself to do it."

"How long?"

"Couple months. Maybe longer." Kai looked at the graph on the screen, the two lines diverging. "It's hard to say when it started because, I mean, how do you time a decision? You don't. You decide and you move and they feel like the same thing. So when the move starts happening first, you don't notice right away. You just feel like you're very decisive."

Yusuf almost smiled at that. "Yeah. That's the design problem, isn't it. When the system predicts cleanly, it just reads as movement. You only catch it once there's room between the thought and the muscle. And from your data, that room's getting wider."

Kai looked at the two lines on the screen. The blue one was his intent, his decisions, the electrical fact of wanting to move. The orange was his body already on its way. And the orange was winning. On the chair he'd felt fifty milliseconds as a clean tightening in his calf, the foot beginning to lift before the internal yes had fully taken shape. In a race the gap would hide inside speed. Here it sat on a screen in two separate colours.

"Is it getting worse?"

"The trend is up. Consistent over three months. I'm going to log it as within normal drift parameters, because technically it is. Meridian's spec allows up to eighty milliseconds of predictive lead before they flag it. But I'm setting a tracker so we can compare session to session." He began disconnecting the sensor pads, peeling each one off with a small, precise pull. "If the window keeps opening, I'll talk to Meridian's firmware team about dialing the predictive threshold down."

"And if they don't want to dial it down."

"Then we have a different conversation." Yusuf wound the diagnostic cable around his hand in a neat loop and hung it on its hook by the console. "Look. Your system is working. It's working well. You ran a nineteen-oh-two today on an unfamiliar track at altitude after a transatlantic flight. The system is doing what it was built to do."

"I know."

"But it doesn't feel like yours."

"I didn't say that."

"Nah, you didn't." Yusuf tapped a final command on the console and the screens went to standby, the blue light dimming to a low pulse. "Same time Thursday for a follow-up. Get some sleep. Altitude takes about three days to stop messing with your deep cycles."

"Thanks."

"Yeah. Night, Brennan."

Kai walked back to his room through the fluorescent corridor. The facility was quiet now, nine-thirty, athletes in their quarters, the common areas emptied, the cafeteria dark. His footsteps were the only sound except the building's ventilation, thinner here than it had been by the calibration bay but steady enough to sand the edges off each step. His own gait on the hard floor came back at him: left foot, right foot, even and smooth, the rebuilt legs managing his stride with the consistency his natural legs had never quite achieved. He'd always been slightly dominant on the left side. Now he wasn't. The reconstruction had corrected the asymmetry without asking.

His room was dark except for the wall display's glow. Green readouts, all systems nominal, the system reporting on itself in the same quiet room where the person it was installed in was trying to fall asleep. In the darkened room, he undressed, brushed his teeth, stood at the window. The mountains were invisible, just darkness and the faint reflection of the diagnostic display behind him, green numbers floating in the glass where the Bernese Alps should have been.

The graph stayed with him. Two lines diverging. Forty to fifty milliseconds. A gap he hadn't been able to name until Yusuf drew it on a screen, and now it had a shape and a slope and the slope was up.

His hand found the light switch. The plastic met his fingertips while the decision was still forming, and then the room went dark.


# Chapter 2

The smell was already in the back of Lena Morozova's throat before her feet touched the deck. At five-thirty the overhead lights were still climbing toward full brightness. The facility brought them up on a schedule: dim at five, half-bright by five-twenty, full fluorescent by six. In that first stretch the water looked darker than it was, lane ropes soft in the gloom, pace clock a red blur at the far end, ceiling lost above in shadow. Pools had looked like this for as long as she could remember. Kirchberg's was newer than the one in Zug where she'd grown up, cleaner, better regulated, but the fundamentals were the same. She had a lane assigned on the rotation schedule. Under the surface, her own breathing got loud enough to fill the world.

In the women's room, the cubicle sensors logged weight, hydration level, and baseline heart rate the moment she stepped onto the scale. The data went straight to the coaching system. After eighteen years inside programs like this, the transfer barely registered.

The pool deck felt cool under her bare feet. At lane four, her lane that week, she set down her water bottle and pull buoy. Dara Lund was already at the wall of lane five, adjusting her goggles with small precise movements. She always made three adjustments: left strap, nose bridge, right strap. Then she pulled the cap over her ears and rolled her right shoulder twice, working loose whatever had been recalibrated over winter break. At Christmas a card from Bergen had arrived for the whole floor, not for Lena specifically, with a photograph of Dara's mother's garden under snow. It was still pinned to the hallway bulletin board. No one had taken it down.

"Morning," Dara said.

"Morning."

They got in.

Warm-up was four hundred freestyle at moderate pace, building through the second half. Off the wall, the familiar compression closed around Lena: water against skin, pressure from the surface above, the lane rope on her left, the tiled floor below, her body settling into the stroke the neural link had been shaping since childhood. Her shoulder rotated, her hand caught and pulled, and the kick came cleanly off the hip. The coaching files called it mechanically optimal. Since fifteen she'd read those files the way other people read medical charts or school reports, clinical pages about someone who happened to be her. Usually the gap between what the files described and what she felt in the water was small enough not to matter.

The main set was eight two-hundreds at race pace, forty seconds rest. On the first repeat the link disappeared into usefulness, handling pacing, stroke timing, muscle recruitment, all the quiet labor below decision. Inside that work she could still mark what was hers: push harder here, hold the streamline longer off the wall, breathe every three instead of two because the altitude at Kirchberg thinned her oxygen uptake and the link kept trying to stretch the breath cycle. She preferred three. Everything else still arrived in the sequence she knew: catch, pull, hip turn, and her mouth turning clear on the third stroke.

The times came in tight. 2:08.3, 2:08.1, 2:07.9, 2:08.0. Slight fade on the fourth, nothing unusual for this point in the block. At the wall she checked the clock, pulled in air, and pushed off for the fifth.

The first hundred meters were fine. She pulled, kicked, breathed, and turned. Lactate sat heavy in her legs, but it was familiar heaviness, something the link factored into pace. Fifty meters remained, then the wall.

At the wall she came in, tucked, rotated.

It was wrong.

Not wrong like a mistake. Wrong like a different answer to the same question. Her body folded tighter than she'd asked it to, came off the wall deeper, lengthened the streamline, drove into a four-beat dolphin instead of the three-beat pattern she'd been using since January. The push-off angle was a fraction steeper, and the water was colder that far down. It was the November firmware variant they'd tried for three weeks and then rolled back because the deeper push-off cost too much oxygen late in the eight-hundred. She'd trained that version and watched the coaches remove it, or thought she had. By the time she knew what the turn was, the turn had already chosen itself.

She broke the surface too hard and got pool water up her nose. For two strokes she went nowhere, sculling in place. Immediately she flexed the left ankle, then the right, then kicked once behind her as hard as she could. The calf took the load cleanly. There was no catch and no knot in it, nothing except the remembered pressure of the tighter fold and the colder water she should not have been touching.

Mid-lane, with the set unfinished and the wall clock still running, she floated while water kept moving around her: Dara's wake in the next lane, recirculation pushing at her calves, lane rope ticking softly against the chop. Above the surface the pool turned back into a room. Her breath echoed, the gutter slapped, and Dara's stroke came muffled from two lanes over. Chlorine came bitter at the back of her tongue.

From the far wall Dara surfaced, pushed her goggles to her forehead, and looked down the lane. When Lena didn't start again, she swam back.

"You okay?"

"Yes," Lena said. "Cramp."

"Calf or foot?"

"Calf. Left side. It's already loosening."

Dara nodded and hung on the lane rope with one arm. "I had one two weeks ago during threshold work. Left calf. Yusuf said it was a hydration flag. The system sent me a fluid reminder every forty minutes for the rest of the day."

Nothing in her face suggested that this was strange.

"Do you want me to get someone?"

"No, it's fine. Thank you."

"I'm going to do the last three."

Dara pulled her goggles down, made one last adjustment to the left strap, and pushed off.

Her stroke was long and clean, the kind of efficient that looked effortless because the effort had been engineered out of sight. Bergen used a different calibration philosophy from Meridian, but the result in the water was close enough.

Lena stayed at the rope a moment longer, heel drawn up behind her under the surface, thumb pressed into the left calf until it hurt. The muscle felt warm and ordinary. If there had been a cramp, it had chosen impossible manners.

Water bottle in one hand, pull buoy under the other arm, she went to the cool-down lane at the far end of the deck. The observation window stayed dark. Margot did not come poolside before eight unless there was a live feed session, and Lena still had nearly two hours before the standard review slot. On the first fifty she open-turned at both walls, palms flat to tile, refusing the flip entirely. On the second she flipped at half speed and counted each part. She tucked. She planted. She pushed. She took three kicks and surfaced. On the third she let the wall come a little faster. The body held to the count because she was watching it, or felt like she was. By the time she settled into actual easy swimming, her shoulders were tight from paying attention in places where attention usually wasn't needed.

The pace clock read 6:11 when she climbed out for good.

In the women's room the post-session traffic moved quietly: lockers opening, shower valves squeaking, wet sandals slapping tile. Lena sat on the bench in a towel and pressed both thumbs down the length of her left calf from knee to Achilles. It was sore from work, no more than that. No spasm sat waiting under the skin. She could not even identify the exact place she was supposed to have lied about.

Under the shower she reached for the shampoo dispenser, found her hair already slick with soap, and stood still for a second with her hand on the button. She could not remember pressing it the first time. That was ordinary enough to dismiss. It was one distracted second, steam in her face, too little sleep. She rinsed, counted to ten while the water went over the back of her neck, and did not count it as anything.

Breakfast service had just opened. By the time she carried her tray into the cafeteria the first tables were filling with track athletes coming off lift, coaches with coffee, two gymnasts in matching thermal tops still pink from the cold corridor. She took oatmeal, two boiled eggs, yogurt, tea. The room smelled like toasted bread and wet synthetic fabric and the industrial coffee the staff called coffee because there was no reason not to.

She sat alone near the windows. Outside, the snow beyond the floodlights looked blue at the edges. Inside, the room's glass reflected her back at her more strongly than the mountain did.

Dara came through the line ten minutes later with a tray that looked measured to the gram: eggs, rye bread, cut orange, one bottle of electrolyte mix already open. She stopped beside Lena's table without sitting.

"Calf better?"

"Yes. It was brief."

"Good." Dara peeled the label from her bottle in one clean strip. "Mine last month wasn't even painful. The system kept flagging sodium and I ignored it. Then Yusuf changed the ratio and the alerts stopped."

"You didn't feel it before the alerts?"

Dara considered that as if it were a technical question, not a personal one. "Not exactly. I felt normal. The numbers were wrong."

She said it matter-of-factly, not proudly and not with resentment either. Then she nodded once and went to the Bergen table.

Lena finished the eggs and half the oatmeal. At 6:54 she looked at the wall clock. At 7:07 she looked again and found herself still holding the spoon in midair, oatmeal cold and thickening in the bowl. She put the spoon down, drank the rest of the tea in three swallows, and told herself this counted as waiting, not losing time. She could account for the minutes. She had watched them pass. That was different.

At 7:42 she left the cafeteria and walked the corridor to the coaching suite. The building had warmed fully by then. Ventilation pushed dry heat out through the floor grilles. Somewhere above her, a medicine ball hit rubber in slow regular beats. The neural coaching suite door was shut. A cleaning cart stood against the wall opposite. She sat on the bench outside, damp hair cooling through the back of her sweatshirt, and watched the clock at the end of the corridor advance from 7:48 to 7:59.

Margot arrived at 8:03 with a paper coffee cup in one hand and her tablet tucked under the other arm.

"You beat me here," she said.

"I wanted to see the session log."

Margot looked at her for a second, then pushed the door open with her shoulder. "Fine. Come in."

---

Next to the pool sat the neural coaching suite, small and windowless, with two reclining stations, a wall of displays, and the faint chlorine residue everyone carried in from the deck. The fluorescent lights were fully up. A clock hung on one wall. The coat rack held Margot's dark parka and nobody else's. Processing hardware hummed behind the panels.

Margot Voss set down her coffee, shrugged out of the parka, and woke the main display with a tap. Her feed interface, the flat silver wrist band she used during live sessions, rested dark against her right wrist. Reviews used recorded data, not live feed. One shoe was already off by the time the system finished loading, her stockinged foot hooked around the chair rung in the posture of someone who spent most mornings in this room.

"Sit," she said. "Show me where it felt wrong."

The athlete's station reclined for bodies that needed to lie still while the system read them. For a review Lena stayed unplugged; the data was already in the log.

Margot pulled up the fifth repeat. At the wall the two traces split: Lena's intended turn on the left, the November variant on the right. Standard tuck and shallow push-off against the deeper angle, tighter fold, four-beat dolphin. A small marker sat over the breach and read 1.3 seconds.

"There," Lena said.

"Yeah." Margot zoomed in. "Stored variant. November rotation. The link thought it was the faster answer and took it."

"I didn't."

"I know. I'm telling you what it did."

Margot widened the view. Three smaller markers appeared earlier in the set, pale enough that Lena hadn't noticed them at first.

"What are those?"

"Minor corrections. Hand entry here. Kick timing off the wall here. This one on repeat four." Margot tapped each in turn. "All under a second."

"It made those too."

"Looks like it."

Lena kept looking at the screen. The earlier markers sat there with the same blunt certainty as the big one. Three moments from the morning when the link had altered hand entry or kick timing before she felt it clearly enough to stop.

"Today's one-point-three," Margot said. "You've been between point-eight and one-point-five all block. This isn't a cliff."

"It felt different."

Margot turned in her chair. "Different how?"

Precision mattered. "Like I was late to it. The turn had already started."

Margot held her gaze another second, then looked back at the screen. "That part I believe. Usually you don't feel the small corrections. This one got big enough to register."

"That's not the same as saying it's fine."

"No." Margot took a sip of coffee. "It's not the same as saying it's catastrophic either."

The chart was clean. The explanation held together. Margot was good at this part of the job. She read data clearly, didn't hedge, didn't patronize. None of that made the split traces easier to look at.

"If it happens in a race?" Lena asked.

"Then the system thinks it's helping you in a race." Margot set down the cup. "The link makes tiny calls all the time. Stroke timing, load distribution, angle correction. That's the arrangement. What matters is whether the calls stay inside tolerances and whether they start costing you anything."

"This cost me the rest of the repeat."

"Yes." Margot clicked to the physiological overlay: heart rate, oxygen uptake, turn depth, recovery curve. "But not because it hurt performance. Because you stopped."

There was no good answer to that. If she had kept swimming the numbers might have treated the moment as a success.

Margot leaned back. "You told Dara it was a cramp."

"Yes."

"Why?"

Lena looked at the screen instead of at her. "Because I didn't want to say what it was in the lane."

"Fine." Margot did not push. "Here are your options. I can flag it to Yusuf as an engine issue and he looks at the stored pattern tree. Or I can kick it to Petra and make it medical. If I do that, you'll lose training time while they run a full work-up. With today's numbers she's likely to hand you straight back, but she'll still want the work-up."

The sentence sat between them for a moment. It was the first thing that morning that sounded like a choice and not like a description.

"Yusuf first," Lena said.

"That's what I figured." Margot made the note. "If it happens again, you tell me before you tell the next lane it's a cramp."

"All right."

Margot closed the turn trace but left the broader drift chart open. "Anything else feel off this morning? Not emotionally. In the system."

Lena thought about the shower dispenser under her hand, the second she could not account for. A second was nothing. Everyone forgot ordinary things. Everyone stood in the shower and drifted. If she said it aloud it would become a symptom the way naming weather on a training report made it part of the record.

"No," she said.

"Good. Your pace structure's where we want it. I'd hold volume this week, maybe add threshold Friday if recovery stays clean." Another tap on the screen. "And eat more. Your protein numbers are low."

"I ate breakfast."

"Then eat breakfast harder."

It was flat enough to almost count as a joke. Almost.

Margot sent the note to Yusuf and swivelled the screen far enough for Lena to see the confirmation tag appear.

"Done. If he wants you in the bay, he'll message. Anything else?"

"No."

"Then go recover."

Chlorine still clung to Lena's hair and the creases of her hands when she left the room. Eighteen years in the water had made the smell invisible most of the time. In moments like this it returned all at once, lifting off skin as though she were carrying the pool out with her.

---

By evening the room had shrunk to what she'd left in it: damp towel cooling on the chair back, radiator ticking under the sill, the novel Anne had recommended lying open on the blanket where Lena had left it after dinner, a finger's width of spine giving way under its own weight. The wall display above the desk sat dimmed to a narrow strip, still cycling through neural link connectivity, system load, calibration schedule, the slow ticker of biomarkers that meant nothing unless Yusuf pointed at one and said this is running hot, we'll adjust it Tuesday.

She had checked for Yusuf's name on the display at 16:10, 17:02, and 18:31. Nothing. The flagged turn existed now as a note in somebody else's queue, something that had happened inside her body and then been translated into text waiting for review. No answer should have made the morning smaller. Instead it left the shape of it entirely to her. By dinner she was looking up every time footsteps slowed outside her door. Once, after recovery, she had tried closing her eyes for ten minutes and found she could not tell whether she had slept or only lain there with her jaw clenched against the pillow.

Under the shower the water felt hotter and softer than the pool, with none of the chlorine bite. She stayed there longer than she needed to, then dressed and went down to the cafeteria. Dinner was pasta with chicken, a salad she moved around without finishing, and a glass of water. Around her, a table of sprinters compared session notes, some gymnasts traded complaints, two decathletes from the briefing sheets sat shoulder to shoulder over their trays. A two-person table near the windows stayed empty long enough for her to claim it. No one joined her. Eighteen years of institutional dining had taught her that this too was ordinary.

By seven the room had gone dark around the window. Late January in the Bernese Alps: sun gone behind the peaks by four-thirty, exterior floods flattening the nearest slope into white, the rest of the range reduced to shape and rumor.

For two weeks she'd been reading the German novel Anne had mailed from Bern, the one about a woman sorting through her dead aunt's papers. It moved through letters, postcards, train tickets, whole decades of somebody else's filed-away life. Nothing about it was difficult. The pace just asked more patience than most things in Lena's day.

On page 147 the woman in the book sat at her aunt's desk sorting envelopes while afternoon light made dust visible in the air. The German nouns arrived one by one: Schreibtisch, Umschlaege, Licht, Staub.

Lena read the paragraph.

Then she read it again.

The nouns stayed separate on the page. She could identify desk, envelopes, light, and dust, and still not hold them together long enough to understand the paragraph.

She set the heel of her hand against the page and started from the first line again. The result was the same. Her eyes tracked properly. The words did not blur. She could translate any of them in isolation. By the time she reached the end of the sentence, the first words had already gone.

Book open on her lap, she sat still while the paper warmed and cooled under her thumbs. The radiator under the sill ticked once. Her shoulders held the same angle as if the next motion would be to turn the page.

It didn't happen.

When she looked up, the display clock read 19:44.

It had read 19:23 when she'd opened the book.

The left leg had gone numb under her. The page edge was damp where her thumb had held it. One shoulder had stiffened from keeping the same angle. Twenty-one minutes had gone by with her sitting upright, eyes open, on the same page. She had not fallen asleep. She had not drifted into a chain of thought and come back from it. There was nothing to return from. The clock had moved and her body had been here when it did.

She put the book down on the blanket, face open, the spine cracking softly. On the back of the cafeteria receipt still in her pocket she wrote, in small block letters, 19:23-19:44 AWAKE. Then she looked at the words to make sure she believed them.

Her hand went up behind her head to the neural link port at the base of her skull. The disc was small and smooth, two centimeters across and warm from the skin around it. It was always warm. She pressed her fingertip against it gently, the way you'd test a bruise. The port had been installed in the Zug facility when she was four, one medical appointment among a hundred that year, and it had been part of her skull longer than most of the memories she carried inside it.

In the bathroom, cold water ran over her hands before she splashed her face once, then again. In the mirror there was only herself: brown eyes, the faint mark on her left temple from the fall on the pool deck when she was twelve, jaw set in the expression she wore when deciding whether something counted as a problem. She looked fine. Tired in the ordinary way, not altered.

Back on the bed, the book reopened to page 147. The woman at the desk returned, along with the envelopes, the light, the dust.

This time the words connected. She found a letter from a man named Aurel, train schedules, the 7:14 from Delemont to Basel, a delay at Laufen, and an apology for arriving late to something the letter never bothered to specify. The dead aunt had kept it for reasons the novel hadn't explained yet. Not understanding that felt ordinary again, almost pleasant.

Three more pages went by without trouble. Comprehension returned so completely that it became invisible. Room, blanket, book, page, all of it joined up the way it ordinarily did.

At lights-out the bedside lamp clicked off and the wall display folded itself down to a single muted status line above the desk. Lena rolled onto one elbow and woke the screen again.

A symptom menu opened. Dizziness. Nausea. Muscular pain. Loss of consciousness.

She read the list twice. Nothing on it matched twenty-one minutes upright in bed with her eyes open and no memory attached. If she flagged loss of consciousness, the overnight log would show otherwise. If she left a note, it would be only her saying it had happened.

She opened the note field anyway.

For a few seconds the cursor blinked in the empty box. Then she typed: Awake. Reading. Twenty-one minutes missing.

She looked at the sentence. It seemed flatter than the thing itself, smaller and less reliable. By the time someone read it, Yusuf maybe, or Margot, or a nurse who did not know her, the sentence would already have started sounding like poor sleep or nerves or language fatigue. She deleted it word by word until the box was empty again.

Yusuf was already going to inspect the turn. Margot already had the morning anomaly in the system. She was not going to report this. Not yet. Not without a trace to point at that wasn't only her own account.

She set the receipt on the bedside table, closed the menu, and tapped the brightness back up until the desk chair, the hanging towel, and the book on the blanket sharpened out of the dim room. Then she lay flat on her back and watched the seconds in the top corner turn over one by one, needing to see each change happen.


# Chapter 3

The calibration bay ran at a constant seventeen degrees Celsius, two below facility standard. The lower temperature wasn't for the athletes. It was for the equipment. Spectral analyzers drifted warm, and Yusuf had spent three months arguing with Building Services until they gave the room its own climate loop. Since October he'd been wearing a fleece under the lab coat and pretending not to notice.

At six forty-five the main station was already awake. The schedule sat on the left. The queue sat on the right. Across the top, an orange Meridian banner he'd dismissed twice this week still waited for him.

`RETENTION MIGRATION: 31 JAN 23:30. Local raw-channel cache access disabled after switchover. Full-resolution export thereafter requires Compliance ticket and Meridian research authorization.`

Security upgrade, the memo called it. It promised better stewardship and a cleaner chain of custody. Tomorrow night the machines would keep seeing everything they already saw; Yusuf just wouldn't. The full streams, the hormone traces, the tiny pre-movement corrections before a limb committed, all of it would route to Meridian's Zurich mirror and come back to him only as licensed research material. Farah didn't have licensed-research time.

He set his coffee on the workstation ledge. He had six athletes before noon and four after lunch. Then he'd have data review and reporting. At least the reporting would be official.

His engineer credentials opened the service pane under the same login he used every day. There was nothing illicit about that part. Calibration staff needed access because bad graft days went bad fast, and if an adjustment misfired the only way to roll it back cleanly was to watch the uncompressed maintenance stream while the athlete was still in the chair. The loophole sat inside legitimate access: during an active session the rig staged ninety seconds of raw physiology in local cache before collapsing it into the compliant summary. Compliance cared about what got filed, not what passed through RAM. If a raw stream touched synced storage, or if the service pane remained open after the session token closed, the workstation generated a retention exception for the morning audit. If he mistimed it once, somebody from Compliance would appear in the doorway with a neutral face and too many questions.

The first athlete at seven was Anya Petrov, a javelin specialist from the Russian program. Her appointment was a monthly recalibration. Her right-shoulder graft had been stable for two seasons, but the altitude altered synthetic-muscle oxygen transfer and the numbers needed nudging every few weeks. She came in already stretching, one earbud still in, then pulled it free and dropped it in her lap as she settled into the rig.

"Morning, Anya. How's the shoulder?"

"Good. A little tight after yesterday's throws."

"Scale of one to ten."

"Three. Maybe four overhead."

Her integration profile opened across the display, the graft's performance envelope running in green and amber bands.

"Right. Tension sweep first. You'll feel the deltoid cycle pressure for about ninety seconds. Try to stay loose."

The sweep passed through the graft. Synthetic fibers adjusted resting tension in soft increments. Work like this always felt more intimate than Yusuf wanted it to. The athletes sat there answering simple questions, and on his screen their bodies opened in layers they couldn't feel themselves: heat drift, local blood flow, nerve firing across the seam where flesh yielded to engineered muscle. His hands stayed steady on the interface. They always did.

Anya's shoulder behaved. Oxygen transfer was down a fraction, and resting tension was back inside range. Yusuf filed the notes and released the restraints.

"Roll it once for me."

She did.

"Feels better," she said. "Looser."

"Should hold four weeks. If the tightness comes back before then, flag it."

Anya slid the earbud back in and headed out. The official chart kept calibration values and approved adjustments. Behind it, while the session token still lived, the service pane held everything else in a temporary block of local memory: raw nerve activity, inflammatory markers at the graft line, stress chemistry, the autonomous correction channel the summary screen rounded into nothing. Yusuf dragged the full stream into the unsynced analysis buffer and watched the progress ring fill. There was no network write, no ticket number, and no audit trail yet. Ninety seconds later the cache would zero itself and leave a clean institutional story behind.

Tomás Reyes arrived at seven thirty, twenty-one, Ecuadorian, in his first Apex cycle. He sat in the rig and looked up at the sensor plates the way newer athletes still did, as if the equipment might reveal what it thought of them.

"How's the integration feeling?"

"Good. I think." Tomás flexed his right hand, opened and closed it. "My grip's been weird in the mornings."

"Weird how?"

"Like the hand's awake before I am." He gave a quick embarrassed laugh. "That sounds stupid."

"No, it doesn't. Tell me exactly."

Tomás looked at his own hand. "I pick up the toothbrush and it's already squeezing. Harder than I meant. Yesterday I reached for the rail by my bed and my fingers were shut before I thought about holding it."

Yusuf opened the profile. It showed a right forearm graft, bilateral calf integration, and a spinal bridge. It was a standard decathlon build.

"How long's that been happening?"

"Maybe a week. Maybe less. I kept thinking altitude, or I slept on it wrong, or I was being an idiot."

"You're not being an idiot. If your system feels off, you say so."

Some of the tension went out of Tomás's shoulders. Yusuf ran the forearm scan. Dense synthetic fibers lit the display in fine cross-section where they anchored to tendon and bone. Resting tension sat slightly high. So did the small readiness spikes that should have stayed flat while Tomás lay still.

"Any trouble in the calves?" Yusuf asked.

"On stairs, a bit. Not pain. Just..." Tomás frowned. "Like my legs start the step before the rest of me."

There it was again.

"Could be altitude," Yusuf said, because that was the answer with the lowest immediate blast radius. "Could be overcorrection in the forearm calibration. I'm dropping resting tension about two percent. Your hand may feel weak for an hour while it settles."

"That's fine."

"If it isn't, you come back."

Tomás settled back without another question, forearm on the pad, throat exposed under the sensor bar. Most of them never asked how much else the system could see. Yusuf made the adjustment, watched the tension trace sink into range, and logged the approved note.

"Thanks, doc."

"Not a doctor."

"Still. Thanks."

After he left, the hidden buffer showed what the official pass had no reason to mention. The forearm elevation was there, but both calf grafts were lifting as well, shallow autonomous pulses rising before any voluntary movement. Sleep chemistry from the night before sat beside it: cortisol, hydration, inflammation around each interface seam. Tomás had come in for his hand and left half his night on Yusuf's screen.

By eight fifteen the Norwegian skier had described the first step of every staircase as arriving too early, her left hip catching before she was fully over it. At nine the diver with visual enhancement said the board had felt a little closer on her last two approaches, not wrong enough to miss, wrong enough to make her stomach tighten. The French sprinter reported nothing at all and still carried the same shallow lift when Yusuf opened the raw stream and looked where the summary screen didn't.

By lunch he had four files tiled across the monitor. They came from different sports, different enhancement histories, and different complaint language because bodies announced the same problem in sport-specific ways. The common field sat in every header, and inside forty-eight hours of calibration the same channel had begun to creep upward.

Autonomous activity wasn't an abstract engineer's squiggle. That layer tightened a calf before landing, steadied a wrist when a bar slipped, corrected a line of movement faster than conscious intent could manage. In tiny bursts it kept enhanced bodies from tearing themselves apart. Rising at rest meant the system was rehearsing movements nobody had asked for yet. If it kept climbing, Tomás's hand would close before he reached, the skier's hip would fire before the stair was under her, and the diver would start getting depth corrections from a body that had decided distance on its own. None of that was theory. The traces supported it. The cause remained a hypothesis. A bad altitude patch could do it. So could local stack noise. So could something moving through the shared calibration network. Four files made an ugly pattern, not proof.

He ate a cheese sandwich at the workstation and brushed crumbs away from the keyboard with the side of one hand. Kai Brennan's overnight file opened all the way out: bilateral motor latency, spinal-bridge throughput, visual load, the micro-corrections that kicked in before Brennan meant to move. On the motion plot, the proprioception gap had widened across three sessions, from fourteen milliseconds to twenty-two and then to thirty-one.

Thirty-one milliseconds meant nothing to most people. At sprint pace it meant foot strike arriving before the body finished registering the strike, correction entering the system after the knee had already committed to a line. Another jump and Brennan wouldn't just feel strange; he'd start getting the lane a fraction late, as if the report from his own legs had to travel back to him from somewhere else.

The Brazilian hurdler with the earlier latency spike wasn't scheduled today, but Yusuf pulled the filed report anyway and saw the same phrase he'd been seeing for weeks: momentary processing artifact. The wording was clean. It gave nobody a reason to panic. It wasn't false, exactly. It was just a polite institutional version of not yet.

An incident report sat two clicks away. If he opened one, Compliance would freeze the raw files to the case, preserve chain of custody, and route the full-resolution material straight into Meridian retention the moment tomorrow night's migration hit. The athletes might get monitored faster. Farah would get nothing. Yusuf wrote the official reports instead.

Brennan: proprioception drift noted, continued monitoring recommended, within acceptable parameters.

Norwegian program athlete: hip adjustment completed, seasonal norms.

Reyes: grip tension corrected, altitude probable.

The reports were technically accurate. They were not morally tidy enough to call honest.

Afternoon sessions did nothing to improve the picture. A German middle-distance runner showed a minute tremor in the quadriceps channel before standing from the rig. An Italian cyclist came back clean on first pass, then betrayed the same shallow correction lift when Yusuf reopened the maintenance stream and compared it to last month. By four thirty the pattern had spread from interesting to sickening. It wasn't epidemic yet. It was broad enough that coincidence started looking like cowardice.

A second Meridian notice dropped into the corner of the screen at five twelve while he was closing out the last chart.

`REMINDER: 31 JAN 18:00 endpoint inventory rehearsal for retention migration. Active external devices on calibration stations will be logged.`

He clicked it away and kept typing, more slowly than before.

On the Kirchberg side, the day still looked immaculate: filed, timestamped, technically defensible. Nobody reading the official system would see a body beginning to get ahead of its owner.

The calibration bay emptied by six. The last athlete left at five forty, and Yusuf spent the next fifteen minutes on shutdown protocols. It wasn't a full shutdown. The processing stacks never really stopped. He powered down the active hardware. He cleaned the sensor plates. He returned the rigs to standby. Sara and Luc handled mornings. Yusuf preferred evenings, when nobody wanted one more explanation and the room gave him back his own thoughts.

When the rigs were down, he took the personal drive from his bag. It was matte black, smaller than his thumb, and encrypted with a key that existed in his memory and one paper backup in the drawer of his room.

The hidden directory on the workstation was only a staging ground. It wasn't invisible, only local and unsynced, tolerated because engineers sometimes needed a place to compare two live traces side by side before deciding whether a calibration had genuinely failed. Weekly maintenance images wiped it. By tomorrow night Meridian's switchover would cut off fresh raw pulls entirely. Everything he meant to keep had to leave the machine before then.

The endpoint rehearsal mattered because the auxiliary port was meant for firmware dongles and vendor diagnostics. Under normal operating rules, device inventory happened after the bay locked for the night and nobody was watching live. Tonight Meridian wanted a rehearsal at six. If his drive was still mounted when the poll ran, its serial would land in the audit bundle with no service ticket attached. That would be a trace. It wouldn't be proof by itself, but it would be enough for someone diligent to start matching times and asking why Yusuf Qadir kept plugging personal hardware into calibration stations that were never supposed to hold patient-level raw physiology.

The system clock in the corner read 17:57.

Bollocks.

He plugged the drive into the auxiliary port. Brennan's widened gap was in the pile, along with Reyes's forearm and calf data, the Norwegian skier's hip values, the diver, the sprinter, the hurdler, the cyclist, and the cross-athlete comparison file. Last week's baseline pulls sat beside them because today's anomaly would be useless without them. He selected everything that still had explanatory value and started the transfer.

A progress bar appeared and gave him eleven seconds.

At second four he thought he'd chosen too much. At second seven the port light flashed amber and his stomach clenched hard enough to hurt. At second nine the indicator went green, and the transfer completed.

He ejected the drive and pocketed it. He closed the service pane and scrubbed the staging directory. By the time the workstation returned to the clean Kirchberg interface, the official day had become the only day the machine was prepared to acknowledge.

He turned off the monitor and left.

The connecting corridor had glass walls on the mountain side, and at this hour in late January the mountains were only shape and weight, a dark mass against a slightly less dark sky. The temperature display by the junction read minus twelve. Beyond the glass, snow had gone blue with evening. Inside, the facility held steady at twenty-one degrees, and the step from the bay's cold into corridor warmth was the only weather Yusuf felt most days.

His quarters sat on the third floor of the residential wing. It was a single room, larger than the athletes' rooms, with a bed, a desk, and a bathroom. A wall-mounted diagnostic display still showed facility status data he'd stopped reading months ago. There was a kettle, a jar of loose-leaf Yorkshire Tea his mother posted once a month, and a stack of books on the desk he was getting through at about a chapter a week. Farah had given him a history of the Birmingham canal network for his birthday three months earlier. He was still in the first third.

Tea came first. He slid the drive into the drawer beside its twin and opened the laptop.

Farah picked up on the third ring. Her face filled the screen, dark hair pulled back, the living room of the flat in Moseley behind her, one corner of the bookshelf he'd helped assemble two Christmases ago visible over her shoulder. She was smiling.

"Yu. Hang on, let me."

She reached off-screen, adjusted something, came back into frame. The camera settled.

"There. Hi."

"Hey. How was your day?"

"Fine. Normal. Went to Asda, made a lasagne. It was terrible but I ate all of it, so." She shrugged. She looked animated and present, with good colour in her face. Compression hid the smaller things until it didn't.

"Did you use Mum's recipe or that website one?"

"The website. Mum's has too many steps. I can't be arsed with bechamel from scratch when there's a perfectly good jar."

"She'd disown you."

"She'd disown me and then bring round a proper one. Win-win." Farah grinned. The pause after it ran a touch long. "What about you? Still in the lab at seven at night?"

"Just finished. Long day."

"They're all long days with you. You need to get out more. Go look at a mountain or something. You're in the Alps and you spend all your time in rooms that are trying to be refrigerators."

"One room, singular. There's a window in the corridor."

"Yu."

"I'm fine. I like the work."

He took a sip of tea and watched the places where it showed first: the beat before she answered, the extra half-second while a noun worked its way up, the slight miss when she reached for her mug and had to adjust her fingers on the handle. They were small things, easy to forgive if you loved somebody and wanted to stay stupid.

"How's the physio going?" he asked.

"Oh, fine. Same. She's dead enthusiastic. Lots of squeezing things." Farah held up both hands and mimed it. "I told her I don't need more grip strength, I need to be able to open the..."

She stopped. Her smile held in place while the sentence didn't.

"Jars," she said, recovering it. "I need to be able to open jars. The Tesco pasta sauce ones especially. Whoever designed those lids is my actual enemy."

"I'll send you one of those rubber grip things."

"I've got three. They don't work on the Tesco ones. I think they're sealed at molecular level."

Months earlier she'd held the kettle up to the camera and called it the thing for tea until the right word came back. Cheap nouns went first: kitchen objects, handles. Then the gaps got longer if the day had tired her out. Voss-Kessler never arrived all at once. It took little, stupid pieces.

"Mum called today," Farah said. "She's worried about you. Says you're not eating properly."

"How would she know?"

"She has sources. I think she's paying one of the cafeteria staff."

"She's not paying anyone. She doesn't even know where the cafeteria is."

"Well, she's worried. Eat a vegetable, Yu. One vegetable. For her peace of mind."

"I had a sandwich."

"What was in it?"

"Cheese."

"That's not a vegetable."

"There was lettuce."

"Iceberg doesn't count. It's basically wet paper."

He laughed, and hers followed half a beat later, still warm, just slower getting there. The delay had become measurable enough that he caught himself counting without deciding to count.

"You still there?" Farah asked.

"Yeah. Sorry. Thinking."

"About work?"

"About what I'm having for dinner."

"Cheese sandwich?"

"Probably."

"Hopeless." She yawned, and the yawn went on a little too long, her jaw moving with a looseness the camera did not quite hide. "I've got that thing with Priya tomorrow. Early."

"What thing?"

"We're going to the..." Her eyes shifted left, the way they did when a word had moved just out of reach. "Cadbury World," she said at last, smiling over the gap she'd just shown him. "She's never been. I've been four times and it's still good. They give you free chocolate at the end."

"Tell Priya I said hi."

"Will do. Eat a vegetable."

"Night, Farah."

"Night, Yu."

The screen went dark. Yusuf sat with the mug cooling in his hands and counted backward through the call: jars, Cadbury World, the pause before hopeless, the mug handle slipping once before she caught it properly. He'd counted two obvious word losses this month. Maybe there were three, if he counted the one she covered well enough that somebody else might have missed it.

There was no treatment. There were papers full of might and could and further study required. The one he kept coming back to, fourteen months old now, had a sentence highlighted in yellow: high-resolution neural maps from enhanced systems might trace the degenerative pathway closely enough to target it. Meridian owned most of those maps already. Tomorrow night, after the migration, every new one at Kirchberg would become Meridian property before he could even pretend otherwise. The licensing quote on his old access request sat starred in his inbox, page two still carrying a number that would've gutted a small lab.

Their reply had been polite enough to make him hate it more. It required institutional affiliation, ethics board approval, and indemnity coverage. Archive access was billed separately from live-stream access, and live-stream access was excluded from the standard license once the new retention model went active. Every line had been written to sound reasonable. Taken together, they meant the same thing as a slammed door.

The incident portal loaded its usual boxes: anomaly class, affected athlete, source dataset, retention authorization. He could fill the first one. He could maybe fill the second, if he picked a name at random and lied by being premature. The third led straight to the files he'd just stolen out from under tomorrow night's switchover. The fourth asked why he'd been keeping other people's raw physiology in a drawer beside his tea.

Formal reporting would make the problem real in exactly the wrong way. Compliance would lock the datasets, Meridian would inherit them, and Yusuf would get whatever filtered summary the company decided a facility engineer was entitled to see. He understood what that made him. The understanding did nothing useful.

He closed the blank incident form and mounted the encrypted drive on the laptop. He dragged the day's comparison set into the archive beside older pulls and watched the copy complete. The folders were organized by date, with backups nested inside backups. It was a private catalogue of bodies that did not belong to him, kept because his sister's nerves were dying and the institutions with the relevant data charged by the gate.

When the copy finished, he ejected the drive, returned it to the drawer, and turned off the light. Tomorrow night Meridian would own everything he failed to take first. Tonight the only full record of the day's anomalies was in his drawer.


# Chapter 4

Margot got to the coaching suite fifteen minutes early and docked the wrist mount before Tomas arrived. The ready-light blinked green against the inside of her right wrist. She watched it until her mouth went dry.

Professionalism, she told herself, meant running the feed diagnostic, checking the hurdle block, and reviewing Tomas's altitude targets before he came in. All of that was true. She also liked the room empty, the adhesive-tape smell, the rubber, the minute before a body entered it.

Tomas's file was already open on her pane. Week-two acclimation model suggested a ceiling of one forty-eight for hurdles, one fifty if he stayed smooth through six barriers. She had loaded one fifty before he walked in. Coaches nudged templates all the time. No one called that an edit. The real line was the one she had learned by repetition: four beats over and the floor still looked clean, six and it started leaving a shape.

Two older amber tags sat collapsed at the edge of the pane, thin as paper cuts. One was Hana from Tuesday. One was beam group from Thursday. Petra hadn't opened either. Tomas's stair session from Monday sat lower in the stack with a smaller bump on it, only two beats, easy to fold into altitude language if nobody read past the first note.

Tomas came through the door at 9:02 in training kit, hurdle spikes in one hand, water bottle in the other. The spikes were custom-fitted and Meridian-issued. He carried them carefully, still a little amazed by them, like they might stop belonging to him if he gripped too hard. He was smiling. He usually was.

"Morning, Coach."

"Morning. You eat?"

"Yeah, eggs and that quinoa thing from the cafeteria. It's not bad, actually." He made a face. "My mom would say I'm lying, but it's not bad."

"Sit down. Let me check your calibration before we start."

He sat across from her. The auxiliary cable clicked into the port on the back of his training vest. His numbers opened for her in the usual order. His heart rate was sixty-two. Core temperature was thirty-seven point one. Enhancement status was all green. Monday's stair set sat in recent history with the small, almost invisible manual bump she had already half-forgotten making.

"How's the shoulder?" Two days ago he had bruised his left deltoid in shot put, a training weight shifting in his grip at release.

"Good. Little sore when I raise it past here." He lifted his arm to about a hundred thirty degrees. "But it doesn't catch. Just, you know. It aches."

"Don't baby it on the hurdles. Your trail arm needs full extension or your hip rotates and you clear the bar like you're climbing a fence."

He laughed. "Got it."

"I'm serious. I watched your last tape. Your trail leg is fine, but that arm drops three centimeters on the left."

"Three centimeters? That's nothing."

"It's a tenth and then sixth place. You didn't come here for sixth."

"No." The smile changed, still there but tighter underneath. His family in Quito needed him to be good. Sometimes the need showed through the charm. "I didn't."

"Ready?"

"Ready."

She initiated the feed.

The world doubled. Her own body stayed in the rig, forearms cool, feet flat on the floor. Over it came Tomas's body, laid across hers through the link: hamstrings loose from the pre-session stretch, right quadriceps carrying the cool seam where synthetic muscle met natural tissue, breathing deep enough to widen her ribs half a beat late. His heart sat at sixty-two and arrived in her chest as a second beat that did not belong there.

When he stood, the load shift ran through her too. Calves engaged. Core tightened. Weight rolled clean across the balls of his feet. He was one of the easier bodies for her to take because his system still let effort through. Some athletes came to her already filtered, strain shaved down before it reached the coach-side feed. Tomas was newer, less polished. His body still told on itself.

He jogged one easy lap first, then did A-skips and two practice clearances. The pace barely counted, but she felt the left deltoid catch at the top of the arm swing and the correction his system made before he noticed it. By the second practice hurdle the shoulder had opened and his stride had gone cleaner than the model expected. Monday had gone like that too. The file said caution. His body said more.

Out on the track, he set ten hurdles at standard spacing and competition height, because Tomas always trained full spec, while she watched through the glass and felt him through the link at the same time. He bent to adjust the third bar and the stretch through his lower back hit her a beat later. His hamstrings were big and the release came easy.

"Pacing targets loaded," she said into the mic. "First set, seventy percent effort. Clean technique. Don't worry about the clock."

"Got it."

In the blocks, his heart rate climbed to seventy-eight. It was anticipatory and normal. The balls of his feet pressed into the pads. His quads coiled. His enhanced vision locked on the first hurdle. The rubber surface snapped into individual granules, bright and stupidly clear.

He pushed out of the blocks.

The first drive hit behind her sternum. Synthetic legs fired. The first hurdle came fast. Lead leg up, trail arm steady, hips high, that tight clean arc over the bar and the hammering landing after. Air touched the damp skin above his lip. The work came into her chest bright and hot and exact. His heart rate hit one thirty-eight.

She never called it beautiful out loud. The feed at full intensity was the best part of her day. Better than swimming had been even on the days she won. Her mouth went wet with it. If she wanted the bar cleared, his body cleared it. If she wanted air, his lungs dragged it in. When she wanted more, she kept taking until something started to shake.

He cleared the second hurdle, then the third, then the fourth. His form held. His heart rate was one forty-six. The pacing target she had loaded peaked at one fifty, already above model and still defensible.

Her thumb touched the adjustment tab and bumped the ceiling by four beats, to one fifty-four.

An amber strip flashed across her pane, telling her what she already knew: manual target edit recorded. Review if incident. If Tomas stayed on his feet, it would live in her coaching log and nowhere that mattered. If he went down, the floor trainer's card would show the new ceiling, the peak rate, and whatever note she wrote. Petra, if the file ever climbed that far, could open the audit trail and watch exactly when Margot had touched the number.

At the fifth hurdle, his visual field narrowed on the bar ahead. Distance compressed and opened with each stride, squeezing her ribs from the inside. His heart rate hit one fifty-one.
