Warmth spread out from the center of her chest. This was the part she came for. His breath had rough edges now. Each landing hit harder. She could feel the session turning mean and she wanted it to keep going.

He cleared the sixth hurdle clean. His breathing turned ragged enough that she started matching it without meaning to. His heart rate hit one fifty-four. His body was asking for more oxygen than the model liked. The strain got ugly. She liked that too.

She could have pulled it back.

At the seventh hurdle, timing slipped by less than a blink. She felt it before she saw it, the small theft of fine control when load started eating accuracy. His lead leg went up. His trail leg came late. His shin hit the bar hard enough to knock it sideways. His knee twisted inward as momentum kept driving him forward.

He went down ugly. He hit with the left hand first, then the right knee, then the shoulder, then the side of his face. Teeth slammed together and copper burst across her own tongue through the feed. The scrape ripped hot over her cheekbone and forearm. His heart rate jumped to one sixty-one. For half a beat the signal from his right leg fuzzed into blank noise and then came back raw and panicked: knee hot, palm burning, jaw ringing, where is the floor, why is the floor still here.

Her fingers hit the release on the wrist mount. The doubled world collapsed. She was back in the rig with the armrests slick under her palms, her own heart rate at ninety-one, and Tomas on the track rolling to one hip and grabbing his knee.

"Tomas. You good?"

"Yeah." He sucked air through his teeth and tried to stand, then sat back down again. "Yeah. I think so. Knee's a little fucked. Sorry. I caught it."

"What happened?"

"I don't know. I felt fine and then not fine." He pressed his thumb under the kneecap and winced. "Like my legs got full of wet sand."

"Your pacing fell off on the last three hurdles. Same thing from Monday's stair set. That's altitude. You've been here twelve days. It takes your legs right when you think you've got them. I should've started you lower. My fault."

He frowned, tested the knee again. "Monday was different, though. Monday it came on slow. This one just hit. I was fine and then I wasn't."

"That happens at this stage. First week the drop is gradual. Second week your body thinks it's adjusted and then the load hits a wall and everything goes at once. Same thing, just faster."

He looked at her. Not suspicious, just working through it. "But my legs felt fast. Right before. They felt really good."

"Yeah. That's how it gets you up here. The effort's harder than it reads because the oxygen isn't where it should be. Your system covers the gap until it can't."

He sat with that for a second. "Okay." Then: "So we kill the four hundred."

"We kill it. Training team checks the knee. We move the four hundred to Thursday if it settles."

"Okay." He sat on the track apron, drank from his bottle, and started unlacing his spikes. "Coach? That heavy-leg thing. Do I put that in the medical form or just the knee?"

"Just the knee. You put the leg thing in there and they'll order panels and you'll spend Thursday in the medical wing instead of on the bike."

He nodded right away. "Okay."

The facility trainer came in before Tomas finished with the spikes. He flexed the knee, cleaned grit and blood from the scrape on Tomas's cheek, and glanced at the short red incident card on his tablet: peak HR 161, fall event, target ceiling 154, coach note pending.

"What did you see?" he asked Margot.

"Late-set altitude drop," she said. "He lost the leg on seven. No blackout."

"No blackout," Tomas said. "Just hit and slid."

The trainer kept working through range-of-motion prompts. "Pain here?"

"Yeah."

"Here?"

"Not as bad."

He pressed along the joint line, nodded once, then looked back at the tablet. "One sixty-one. You raised him to one fifty-four?"

"At hurdle four. Monday he showed the same late-onset heaviness on the stairs. Same pattern, solid early, then everything dropped at the end. I bumped the ceiling four beats to see if it repeated or if Monday was a one-off."

"Did it."

"At seven. Yeah."

He looked at her, then back at the tablet. "Note both sessions. If the comparison's attached, it goes to Petra's night audit. If it isn't, live medical gets paged now."

"Sure."

Tomas looked from one to the other. "It's still just altitude, yeah?"

"Yeah," Margot said. "Your leg didn't go dead. You got late and ate a hurdle. That's it."

He nodded, and some of the strain went out of his face, like ordinary was all he needed this to be.

"Minor sprain," the trainer said. "Two days off hurdles. Bike work if it doesn't swell. Medical can clear the next block."

Tomas nodded again. "Okay."

"Go get ice and eat something," Margot said. "You're burning through everything up here."

"Yeah. See you Thursday?"

"Thursday."

After Tomas and the trainer cleared out, the room went thin and still. The ready-light on her docked interface kept blinking.

She opened the coaching report.

The ordinary cause-code menu was gone. In its place the screen had thrown up an all-caps warning: incident with manual target change, peak rate outside the acclimation band, attach comparison justification or auto-send to live medical review.

Of course.

The session graph showed his heart climbing in clean blue steps and the small amber nick at hurdle four where she had touched the ceiling. Monday sat beside it because she had said Monday out loud. She had handed the lie a witness.

Coaches kept a bank of phrases for moments like this, words meant to make the system lose interest: transient acclimation delay, hydration variance, load spillover from prior block. She had used them often enough that they were already in her fingers. That was its own problem. It meant practice.

She could have sent the whole thing to Petra instead. Petra would see the nick, the fall, the Monday bump, the older amber cuts still sitting in her queue, and start asking in that flat careful way of hers that never sounded like anger until it was too late.

Margot opened Monday. The system would not take altitude by itself after a manual ceiling change. It wanted a prior session attached or a physician paged.

The stair-session trace was ordinary until rep five, where Tomas's rate had climbed, stayed there, then gone sloppy at the end. She had already nudged that one two beats above model and tagged it conditioning variance. Now she changed the note. Late acclimation lag observed after sustained load. Athlete tolerated elevated ceiling before sudden heaviness. Monitor for recurrence. The amendment stamped her credential and the new time beside the old lie.

She returned to today's report and copied the shape of it over. Late-set altitude loss. Prior similar presentation Monday. Reduced intensity for two sessions. Bike re-entry Thursday if swelling controlled.

The note did what notes like that were built to do. With Monday attached and the cause tagged as recurrence instead of novel event, the system dropped today's fall out of live medical review and into Petra's night stack. The raw numbers did not move. The amber nick stayed in the graph. Monday stayed beside it with the fresh amendment stamp. The two older tags stayed where they were, waiting for Petra or anyone patient enough to stack them.

Margot closed the pane anyway.

---

At five in the afternoon in late January, the window had already gone half-mirror. The glass held cold and returned her outline in pieces.

Inside, the door locked with a clean click. She kicked off her shoes, shrugged out of the coaching jacket, stepped out of the track pants, and stood in a T-shirt and underwear with the feed mark still dented into her right wrist.

On the floor mat, she ran the old stretch sequence: hamstrings, quads, hip flexors. Her left hamstring bunched high under the sit bone and stayed there. The right one gave her nothing. She bounced once, then again, waiting for the muscle to wake up. It didn't. Her molars hurt from how hard she was clenching.

She got down and did fifty push-ups because swimmers counted in fifties. At thirty her triceps started to shake. At forty-two her breath got loud. At fifty she rolled onto her back and waited for her heart to punch up hard enough to matter. Her watch read one twenty-eight, then one thirty-two. Sweat gathered in the hollow of her throat and slid into her bra. There was nothing under it but her own body asking to stop.

She stood, did air squats until her quads burned, then a wall sit until the muscles fluttered and her knees hurt. It was still not even close. Her legs shook because they were tired, not because they were carrying anybody else's speed.

When she bent over the sink to rinse her face, copper rose in her mouth again. For a second she thought she had bitten through her own tongue. It was only the afterimage of his impact, nerves repeating something that had happened to somebody else.

In the bathroom mirror, she took stock the way she used to before weigh-ins: five eight, a hundred fifty-two, fourteen pounds over competition weight, broad shoulders gone soft at the edges, thighs still thick, stomach flat enough if she stood straight. She pinched the skin above her hip, let go, and watched the nail marks fill back with color.

Nothing was wrong with her body. It obeyed. It warned her before it failed. That was the whole problem.

The tub edge was cold through the cotton of her underwear. Her mouth kept going wet and then dry again. The skin under the wrist mount prickled. She pressed her thumb into the dent left by the dock until the mark went white and pain climbed up her forearm. When the ache faded, she did it again.

Her chest kept waiting for the second heartbeat from the feed. Nothing came. Her own pulse kept tapping along, small and regular. She pressed the wrist mark again, harder this time, until the tendon jumped.

At the desk, the live training board showed two swimmers in the pool, one cyclist in the erg room, the gymnastics hall booked until six. She clicked through the names without thinking. Bodies were still in motion. Heart curves were still building. Anyone still working would do. She got as far as the door, hand on the handle, before voices passed in the hall and made her let go.

It wasn't addiction. It was work. She said it once under her breath and felt the lie catch in her dry mouth. Athletes improved under her. Times dropped. She went back to the board and kept looking anyway.

The shower did nothing. Hot water hammered her shoulders until the skin went pink. Then there was soap, steam, tile. That was all.

Dressed again, she reopened the coaching dashboard.

Tomas's fall sat in the queue now as a full amber bar instead of a collapsed slash. Under it sat Monday stairs. Under that sat Hana Tuesday and beam group Thursday. Four times she had leaned on the numbers in nine days. Four notes in her own clipped language about athlete-specific tolerance, late heaviness, controlled overreach. She had told herself each one was separate because each body was separate. In the column they looked like repetitions.

The queue carried tiny thumbnails of the curves beside the notes. She did not need them. She knew the shapes already. Hana's long rise and neat plateau. Beam group's sharp spikes at the dismounts. Monday's stair sawtooth. Today's clean climb and sudden cliff.

The little audit marks beside each entry stayed grey. Petra hadn't opened them yet.

Tomas's message landed while she was still looking at the queue.

`Knee is bigger now but I can walk. Sorry again about the fall.`

He was apologizing to her.

She typed back: `Don't apologize. Ice twenty on twenty off. Compression sleeve tonight. We'll bike Thursday if the swelling drops.`

Then she opened Thursday's re-entry block. Medical had already shifted hurdles to bike work and physician-locked the ceiling at one forty-eight. It was conservative, boring, safe in the way people meant when they did not care what training felt like.

A warning bar slid over the plan when she touched the number: medical cap in force. Coaches could lower it, not raise it, unless they wanted the override stamped into Petra's morning audit. She changed the bike ceiling to one fifty-two anyway and toggled live sensory feed back on. No page tonight. Just her credential on the override and Petra's queue in the morning. She saved it before she could stop.

Thursday might die in medical before warm-up if the knee swelled. If it didn't, the block was there.

Nothing urgent sat in her email. Outside had disappeared into the glass. In the window she was only an outline, which was better.

She went to dinner because not going would mean staying in the room with herself.

The cafeteria was loud in the flat, tired way it always was after training. Trays knocked against tabletops, cutlery clicked, and chairs scraped concrete. Tomas sat three tables over in fresh sweats with a compression sleeve on his right knee and the scrape on his cheek drying pink. He was eating too fast. Two cyclists had him telling the story already.

"Seventh hurdle," he said, laughing a little because everybody else was laughing. "I was good and then altitude just took my legs away. Coach said it happened Monday too."

"Altitude'll do that here," one of the cyclists said. "First week I nearly ate shit on the stairs."

"Yeah. Same thing," Tomas said. Relief loosened his face. He was taking the explanation the way she'd hoped he would, like this was just one more dumb altitude fall.

Margot took soup and bread and sat far enough away that he did not wave her over. She listened to him repeat the lie twice more, each time shorter, cleaner, easier to say. By the second telling he had stopped mentioning the heaviness arriving all at once. By the third it was just altitude and a bad clip on the bar. The room took it from there with a joke, a nod, and somebody saying everybody had one stupid fall up here.

By the time he limped out with his tray balanced against his hip, the story belonged to the room. It would be what he told medical again if they asked. If he kept hearing it back from the room and from her, maybe that would be the version he stuck with.

Back in her room, the wrist mount waited on the desk. She picked it up, turned it over, thumbed the warm contacts, and pressed it to the pulse point on her right wrist without connecting it. It was dead metal on living skin. She held it there through four breaths, until the new pressure mark matched the old one, then set it down beside the dock. The shape stayed after the metal was gone.


# Chapter 5

Kai's left ankle clicked when he crouched into the starting blocks, the natural kind, tendon over bone. A fraction later the right one corrected itself. Synthetic muscle tightened to set the joint before he'd finished settling into the pedals. Most days the lag between the two ankles passed beneath notice. This morning it stayed with him, small and stupid, like a word misspelled on a sign he saw too often.

He was in lane three.

Dry air bit low in his throat on the inhale. Two weeks at Kirchberg had been enough for his lungs to stop complaining outright, but morning sessions still left a sting at the bottom of the breath before his chest opened. Six other sprinters moved through warm-ups around the oval. Novak in the far lane, doing strides with a gait so bilaterally perfect it looked unreal. Priya Chandrasekaran near the curve, shaking out her hamstrings, her right leg extending and retracting with the evenness of a fresh calibration. Two athletes Kai knew by face but not by name were jogging easy laps. Behind the glass partition at the hundred-meter mark, two coaches sat with tablets on their knees, watching telemetry.

The start tone sounded, and he came out of the blocks.

The first fifty meters were the part that still felt like running. Feet hit rubber. Knees drove forward. Burn built in his quads with the old familiar honesty of lactic acid and effort and muscle doing exactly what he told it to do. Arms pumped. Breathing locked to footstrikes, short hard exhales. The grafts stayed in the background then, waiting for the point where his body crossed its natural limit. For those first seconds, the work belonged to him.

Around sixty meters, the assist came in and the effort smoothed out. There was always a seam there, impossible to pin down, where his own power handed off to the helped version. Stride lengthened by a centimeter he hadn't chosen. Arm swing adjusted. Elbows tucked. Hands loosened. The burn in his quads changed texture and spread out, managed now, load redistributed before fatigue could bunch in one place. He was still running. The running had started doing itself.

At eighty meters the visual enhancement sharpened. By ninety, lane markings snapped into hard clean lines while the far wall came forward with too much detail and the edges of the track fell away into blur. Everything outside the lane ahead lost importance. When the curve came up at a hundred meters, natural balance had never found that angle, but his body banked into it anyway, precise and efficient, grey padding sliding by on his left over concrete block close enough to touch. The foam was built for bodies already slowing down, not for this speed.

At a hundred and twenty, and again at a hundred and thirty, the time felt good without a clock, the way a driver knows speed from engine pitch. At a hundred and forty his left foot hit and the track dropped out from under him.

It wasn't darkness. It wasn't silence. It was just gone. Sight vanished so completely even black never arrived. Sound went with it. The map of his own body, shoulders and jaw and knees and the tilt of his head, got ripped out in the same instant. One stride he had a body. The next there was only the thinking left behind: *I was running. I can't feel my legs. I can't feel anything.*

The thoughts came clear and useless. They had nowhere to land. Nothing grounded him. No breath filled his lungs. No weight sat in his chest. He tried to open his hands and found no hands. Tried to stop running and found no running from where he'd been dropped. Only the hard certainty that the body he couldn't reach had kept going. His body was still sprinting. No sensation came up from it, but the fact of it stayed obvious. The legs were still cycling. The arms were still pumping. The lungs were still hauling air. The bridge had locked the sprint into his muscles, and the stride kept its shape after he was gone.

Inside his own head he threw the order as hard as he could. *Stop. Slow down. Stop.* Nothing happened. There was no grip on the legs, no line down to them, no body left to hear.

Time had no size in there. There was no pulse to count, no breath, no footfalls. There was only math: the curve, the wall on the curve, padding meant for cooldown collisions, not this speed. He didn't know if the wall was half a second away or already at his shoulder.

Crash response came to him anyway, pure training. He pictured his chin down, his shoulder turned, his arms in. The picture stayed in his head. Nothing in his neck or ribs or hands heard it.

The world hit him shoulder first. Sound came back as a thud inside his skull when his right shoulder slammed into the wall. Foam flattened. Concrete took the rest. The joint jumped out of place with a wet grind, shoved forward and down until his arm no longer fit his own socket.

Momentum spun him off the wall and flung his face across the track. Fine black grit tore skin off his cheek and the skin above his left eye, then packed itself into the raw streaks. Blood came fast, hot at first, then cold in the dry air.

His left hand shot out. Reflex or the last of the stride still burning through him, he couldn't tell. The wrist folded under him and broke with a clean crack just above the joint. White pain shot up the forearm and stopped under the elbow.

He ended on his side and then his front, twisted between the two. Overhead panels glared. Blood ran across his left eye and turned the lights into red-smeared rectangles. The right shoulder sat wrong under the skin, too far forward, every small shift sending a grinding pulse through his chest. The left forearm was already swelling around the break.

His legs were still twitching.

They weren't running now. They were firing in scraps. Quadriceps bunched and let go. Calves jumped. Spikes scratched the rubber in short useless kicks. Whatever had kicked the stride loose was still sputtering through the bridge.

He watched his own thighs jump and couldn't do anything about it. Lying on the track in blood, shoulder out, left arm broken, he watched his feet scrape and kick as if they were trying to finish the rep. The legs were his. The command wasn't.

People were already running toward him. Novak reached him first. Blue-and-white shoes stopped two feet from Kai's face. Someone shouted for medical. The coaches behind the glass had stood up and abandoned their tablets. Through the blood in his eye he could still see Priya at the edge of the far lane, hands hanging at her sides, watching with the stillness of someone looking at a body and thinking of her own.

"Don't move him," a voice said somewhere above him. "Wait. Don't move him."

He wasn't trying. The left leg kicked once more, spike leaving a grey scuff on the track, and then stopped.

The medical team arrived with a backboard and a cervical collar. A woman from Petra Engel's staff dropped beside him, broad-shouldered, cold hands, calm in the way of someone trained for exactly this and still surprised to need it at a morning sprint session.

"Can you hear me?"

"Yeah."

"Tell me what hurts."

"Shoulder's out. Right one. Left wrist is broken." His own voice came back lower than he expected. The joke reflex wasn't there.

"Can you feel your feet?"

The twitching had stopped. What remained was his own body reporting in: toes, ankles, the deep used ache in muscles that had done work without permission. "Yeah. I feel them."

The woman gave the smallest nod, filing the answer away.

They rolled him onto the backboard. The shoulder shifted and a short closed-mouth sound came out of him, chosen over the scream that wanted out. Five other athletes stood in a loose semicircle now. Novak had folded his arms. Priya still hadn't moved from the far lane. The whole room had gone wrong around the crash. Even the quiet sounded wrong.

Blood dripped from his face onto the board in small red beads. His cheek burned with ground-in grit. The cut above his eye kept leaking into his lashes and tinting the edge of the world.

They carried him down the corridor between the track and the medical wing. Three minutes of fluorescent light and ventilation hum, ceiling panels sliding past at other people's walking speed. Kai stared up and kept trying to find the edge of the gap. There wasn't one. One stride he was running, the next he was nowhere, then his shoulder hit concrete and the whole world slammed back in.

The thirty-one seconds in between had been bare thought, stripped clear of lungs and weight while the sprint kept spending his body.

---

The medical bay smelled like isopropyl and cold metal trays. They reduced his shoulder first. Two techs held him while a third worked the arm, patient and mechanical, until the joint slipped back where it belonged. The pop was sickening. Pain changed shape when it went home, as if the shoulder remembered being out and resented the trip back.

"Shit," Kai said, and that was all.

They braced the wrist next. Bones sat out of line, and when the technician pressed along the break the pain told him exactly where it had failed. She locked the joint in a rigid splint and left his fingers free. It would need imaging. The grafts would make the bone knit fast, which didn't mean it would knit right.

A doctor Kai hadn't met cleaned the grit out of the abrasions with saline that stung clean and deep. Then she stitched the cut above his left eye, five sutures, neat and quick.

"You'll have a small scar here," she said.

"Sure."

Scissors slid under the laces of his spikes. The nurse cutting them off had to work carefully around the splint on his left arm and the raw scrape along his cheek. When she lifted his right heel clear of the mattress, his calf seized hard enough to jerk the whole leg straight.

The mattress frame rattled.

Everyone in the room stopped.

"Sorry," Kai said automatically.

"Did you do that on purpose?" the nurse asked.

"No."

She kept hold of the heel until the muscle let go. Another jump ran through the thigh, smaller this time, then faded. Her eyes went to the chart. "Residual autonomous discharge," she said to no one in particular, and wrote it down.

The phrase made him colder than the saline had.

After that they left a rolled towel under both knees as if he were liable to snap straight again. The towel sat there white and routine, one more small precaution set in place because his legs might jump without warning. That made it worse.

Imaging took longer than the treatment. They wheeled him one room over for X-rays, slid his arm and wrist into positions that kept finding new pain inside the old pain, and rolled him back to the exam room with a temporary sling pulling at his neck. A monitor clipped to his finger tracked pulse and oxygen. Another lead sat on his sternum. No one bothered pretending he was here only for a broken wrist and a dislocated shoulder.

Voices moved in and out beyond the curtain and then beyond the door. Track staff came through. A trainer came through. Somebody asked whether the morning sessions were canceled. Somebody else said not yet, we're waiting on Dr. Engel. A little later the same voices came back tighter. The word blackout reached him once. Then the voices dropped into the careful quiet people use when the injured person might still be listening.

He was still listening.

Between interruptions the room flattened into fluorescent light and machine hum. His shoulder throbbed in deep slow pulses. The break sat under constant pressure with bright flares whenever his fingers moved. Air touched the raw skin on his cheek. The towel stayed under his knees. The memory of the calf locking by itself when someone else picked up his leg stayed with it all.

He tested the toes under the sheet. They moved when he asked them to move. He did it again. The result was the same.

The third time, fear got into the sequence and made it stupid. He flexed and released, then flexed and released again.

The right foot answered a fraction before he finished thinking the second flex.

Maybe it hadn't. Maybe pain had sped the thought up or slowed it down or made him notice the wrong part of it. That would have been the generous explanation. He stared at the sheet until the two ridges over his feet looked steady again and didn't try another test.

The door opened and Yusuf came in with his tablet and a data cable. His face had the look Kai knew from calibration sessions, concentration pulled tight, eyes narrowed behind his glasses, an engineer already arguing with the data in his head.

"How bad?" Yusuf asked, dragging a chair to the bedside.

"Shoulder's back in. Wrist is broken. Face is rough."

"Yeah, I can see the face. Can I plug in?"

Calibration shorthand. He wanted system logs, bridge data, graft telemetry, visual records, everything the hardware had kept track of while Kai had been locked out of himself.

"Go ahead."

Yusuf leaned over him, found the port at the base of his skull, and clicked the cable in. His hands were steady, the same hands that always made the interface feel routine. He sat back and started reading.

Kai watched him read. The screen itself stayed out of sight, only a pale glow reflected in Yusuf's lenses. Timestamps, graphs, and scrolling blocks of system output moved across the display. Tightness gathered around Yusuf's eyes and then held there.

"What happened at one-forty?"

"That's what I'm looking at." His professional voice stayed clean and measured. He scrolled, stopped, scrolled back. "Your visual processing dropped to zero at the one-forty mark. Full shutdown. It wasn't a fade."

"It wasn't just visual."

"No, I can see that. Body mapping went at the same timestamp. Auditory input forty-two milliseconds later. So everything, basically. All at once." Yusuf moved to another pane. "Motor output didn't stop."

"My legs didn't stop."

"No." He was still looking at the tablet. "The bridge kept a stored sprint routine alive on the autonomous layer. Your grafts just carried on." He glanced up briefly. "Didn't need you there for that."

The room hummed softly while Kai tried to fit that answer anywhere useful. "How long was I out?"

"Thirty-one seconds." Another pane. Another pause. "Sensory return at one-seventy. Impact at one-seventy-three."

"I know where the wall is."

"I know you do."

His finger moved again, faster now. He scrolled, stopped, backed up, and zoomed in. The professional tone thinned by half a degree.

"Hold on."

"What?"

"There's a post-install verification fault in your crash dump." Yusuf didn't look up. "From yesterday's calibration, not from the impact. The receipt says the sensory package installed clean. The live module hash doesn't match it."

Kai stared at him. Pain had made his patience short. "English."

Yusuf finally looked up. "Your system says it downloaded one thing and ran another. Same label. Different payload."

"Because I hit a wall?"

"No. That's why I'm looking at it." Yusuf turned the tablet so Kai could see almost none of it and still understand the shape: pale lines, a red one cutting across them, a block of amber text repeated in two different places. "Impact damage mangles data. This doesn't look mangled. It's neat. The sensory fail-safe table was already rewritten before you left the blocks."

"Rewritten to do what?"

"To stay quiet when the sensory layer drops." He tapped the red line. "Normally if sight and body map vanish together, the bridge trips a hard stop. Legs lock or dump you to a protective crawl. Ugly, but not a hundred-and-forty-meter wall strike. Yours never fired. The autonomous layer just kept the sprint going."

The explanation sat there long enough for Kai to say, "So that was in me before the run."

"Yes."

"And you missed it."

Yusuf's mouth tightened. "The dashboard missed it."

"You were the dashboard yesterday."

"No, I was the person trusting a summary report." The words came out sharper than his usual bedside tone. A beat later he corrected by addition, not apology. "It keeps the version label and strips the live hash unless something fails hard enough to dump memory. Your crash preserved the part the normal report flattens."

He was already standing as he said the last sentence, still tethered to Kai by the cable. The wall terminal woke under his hand. The larger screen split into panes of logs and sync records.

From the table Kai could make out athlete names down the left column and the same block of amber digits repeating beside them.

"What are you checking?"

"Shared handshake logs." Yusuf opened another pane. "If the mismatch lived only in your box, maybe you've got a local corruption event. Storage fault, damaged sensory board, one-off nightmare. If the same payload hash shows up in the network receipts, it came through calibration and we're all fucked." The last part came in his own voice, not the professional one.

Kai watched the names climb past. His session came first. Then Novak, eighteen-oh-three last night. Hoffman, twenty-oh-nine. Priya, six-eleven this morning. Two swimmers followed, then a hurdler. More amber digits came after that.

Yusuf went still, then pulled the first record open beside the second and the third. "There."

"Same package label," he said, more to the screen than to Kai. "Same approved version string. Same clean install receipt. Same bad hash sitting underneath it. Christ." Another pane. "And the sensory rollback counter's up in all of them. Low, but it's there."

Kai pushed himself an inch off the pillow before his shoulder shut that down. "What does that mean?"

"It means your blackout isn't some freak one-body malfunction." Yusuf zoomed in until numbers turned to colored blocks. "This hash is propagating through the shared calibration handshakes. Every time somebody syncs, the package copies across and then pretends it's the ordinary sensory update."

"How long?"

"Can't tell yet." He moved faster, calling up older logs. "Forty-eight hours for sure. Maybe more, but the audit summaries roll up after that and dump the full payload comparison unless someone manually preserves it."

There were forty-odd people. Kai pictured the track again, Priya at the curve and Novak folding his arms, every body in the building running on the same invisible thing.

"Same hash means same blackout?"

"No." Yusuf snapped that out and then moderated. "No, not automatically. It proves exposure. It proves the sensory layer is carrying the same corruption. It doesn't prove everyone gets your exact cascade." He pointed at another column. "Expression depends on load, subsystem mix, what the body is asking for when the fault tips over. Some people may get drift. Hand numbness. Audio drop. A bad turn in the pool. You were at full sprint on a curve when it went."

"Lucky me."

Yusuf had no answer to that. He opened one more record, then another, then swore under his breath.

"What?"

"The rollback counter isn't just there after calibration. It's climbing after ordinary telemetry pings." His hands went flat on the terminal. "It's not only copying during full sessions. The network handshake's enough."

Kai felt the cold spread out from that sentence. "That's a virus."

Yusuf didn't answer right away. He braced both hands on the terminal and stared at the logs like staring harder might make them contradict themselves. His glasses had slid down his nose. He still hadn't pushed them back up.

"It behaves like one," he said at last. "Self-replicating firmware exploit, that's the plainest name for it. I need a proper diagnostic dump before I put that in writing. But yes. It copies itself. It uses the shared handshake. And it already moved beyond your system."

The room went very quiet as Kai looked past Yusuf at the names on the screen: Novak, Priya, people he'd been on the track with an hour ago, people whose bodies were running on the same infrastructure that had just cut him out and kept going.

"Can you fix it?"

"I don't know yet." The professional smoothness was gone now. "First thing is containment. Nobody trains. Nobody calibrates. Nobody plugs into this network again until I've mapped the scope."

"Every athlete."

"Everyone who's been calibrated here. Which is everyone."

The door opened before either of them moved. Petra Engel came in still wearing track-side scrubs under her coat, grey hair pulled back too tight, fatigue written across her face in old familiar lines. A brown-red smear marked one cuff where she'd gotten Kai's blood on herself earlier and either hadn't noticed or hadn't had time to care.

"I was told you wanted me now," she said.

Yusuf turned from the screen. "Yes. Right now."

Her eyes went once to Kai, taking inventory: sling, splint, stitched brow, monitor leads, the towel under his knees. Then back to Yusuf. "What do you have?"

"Not a local failure." He pointed at the wall display. "Kai's crash dump shows a live sensory hash that doesn't match yesterday's approved install receipt. Same bad hash is sitting in multiple athlete records over at least forty-eight hours, and the rollback signature is moving through the shared handshakes."

Petra took two steps closer to the screen. "How certain are you?"

"Certain on the repeated hash. Certain on the mismatch between receipt and live payload. Probable on active propagation. I am not yet certain every exposed athlete is at immediate blackout risk."

"But immediate risk exists."

"Yes."

"Why was this not flagged by the system?"

"Because the summary report kept the approved label and stripped the underlying hash unless a session failed hard enough for manual review." Yusuf's voice sped up again. "Kai hitting the wall gave me raw data before the summaries rolled."

Petra looked at him for one beat longer. Then the decision happened in her face before it happened in her body.

"Can you isolate the calibration server without bricking anyone's implants?"

"Yes. Local control stays local. We lose live telemetry and remote diagnostics."

"Good. Lose them."

She crossed to the wall phone and hit the emergency key.

"Engel. Initiate restricted-operations protocol immediately." Her English flattened further when she was angry. "Close the track, pool, weight room, and coaching suites. Suspend all calibrations, remote diagnostics, and elective maintenance. No athlete ports used without medical authorization. Notify operations and unit leads that medical briefing is in ten minutes."

She listened for half a second, then cut across whatever answer came back.

"No, I am not waiting for the facility director to arrive. Start it now. You can wake him with the building already closed."

The line crackled. Petra's jaw tightened.

"All enhanced athletes to quarters or common areas under observation. No one trains. No one connects personal equipment. Security if necessary."

Another pause.

"Yes. Now."

She hung up and turned back to Yusuf.

"You go to the calibration bay. Pull complete handshake archives before the automated rollup erases more detail. Preserve every payload receipt and every live hash you can get. I want a preliminary risk table by noon if that is possible, and if it is not possible I want to know why."

"Right."

"Don't say right. Say clear."

"Clear."

The exchange landed harder than the raised voices would have. Yusuf nodded once and reached for the cable at the base of Kai's skull.

Kai caught his forearm with the good hand before he could unplug it.

"How long has it been in me?"

Yusuf looked down at Kai's hand on him, then back at Kai's face.

"I don't know yet."

"Say a range."

"At least since yesterday's calibration." The answer came clean because Petra was in the room and there was no space left for softening. Then a second sentence followed, smaller, truer. "Possibly longer. I need the older records before I can tell."

Kai let go.

The cable clicked free.

Petra stepped to the bedside while Yusuf gathered tablet and adapters. Her gaze went briefly to the chart at the foot of the bed.

"You will stay here today," she said. "Observation, imaging review, neuro checks. No attempts to stand without staff."

"Wasn't planning sprints."

Her mouth moved as if something almost like a smile had passed near it and kept going. "Good."

The intercom came alive overhead before anyone said more.

"Attention staff and athletes. Restricted-operations protocol is now in effect. All training spaces are closed until further notice. Athletes are to return immediately to quarters or designated common areas. Do not connect to any diagnostic or calibration interface. Await further medical instruction."

The announcement repeated in German a second later. Before the French version finished, the exam-room door banged hard enough to hit the stop.

Priya stood there in warm-up pants and spikes with a residence aide half a step behind her. One hand was jammed against the port under her hair as if holding herself back from touching it. She looked past Petra to Yusuf, not to Kai.

"Mine was this morning," she said. "Tell me if it's the same thing."

The aide beside her said, "She left the track before we could clear the lane."

Down the corridor another athlete shouted, "I'm not sitting in quarters till somebody checks me."

Yusuf had already half-turned with the cable in his hand. Petra took it from him before he got a step.

"No one plugs into anything," she said. "Not him. Not you. Observation wing, now."

Priya didn't move. "I need to know if it's in me."

"And I need that archive intact," Petra said. "If he connects you now, he learns less and risks more. You will wait under observation."

For a second Priya stayed where she was, hand still at the port, looking at Yusuf like she could make him choose with his face. He didn't. The aide touched her elbow. This time she let herself get turned back into the corridor.

The French announcement kept going over the top of it. By the time the last words cleared, the corridor outside had changed shape entirely. Running footsteps sounded in bursts. Doors opened. Someone farther down the hall was crying and trying not to make noise.

Yusuf had gone still with his empty hand half raised, his diagnosis now in other people's bodies whether he named it or not.

Petra heard it too and didn't turn her head. "Go."

That got him moving. He slid the tablet under his arm, missed the pocket for the cable on the first try, tried again, and headed for the door.

"Yusuf."

He stopped.

Kai hadn't planned the sentence before it came out. "If you find out who's next, don't tell them like you told me."

It was a meaner line than he'd intended. Pain did that. Fear did it more.

Yusuf took the hit without arguing. "No," he said. "I won't."

Then he was gone.

Petra stayed long enough to adjust the monitor clip that had gone crooked on Kai's finger and to read the wrist imaging results that had just appeared on the wall screen. The report said distal radius fracture, non-operative for now, follow-up scan tomorrow. She read quickly, lips tightening once, then signed the order set with a thumbprint.

The intercom kept working elsewhere in the building. Through the open door came fragments of the shutdown as it spread: a trainer telling someone to leave the treadmills alone; a swimmer asking if this was about infection control; a metal cart rattling too fast over tile; security shoes, heavier than the rest. Once, for half a second, Priya crossed the doorway again, moving toward the residence wing while the same staff member kept pace beside her. She didn't look in. He was glad of that.

Petra followed his line of sight anyway.

"They saw the crash," Kai said.

"Yes."

"That's going to help."

"No," Petra said. "It will not."

Nothing in her tone pretended otherwise.

She checked his pupils with a penlight, asked his name, the date, where he was. Asked whether sensation in both feet still felt intact. Asked whether he had lost time since arriving in medical.

"No more than usual," he said.

The line came out dry enough to count as a joke in another room. It didn't count in this one.

"If that changes, you tell us immediately," Petra said.

"Yeah."

Another burst of noise went up in the corridor. Someone was crying now, quietly and angrily, the way people cry when they are trying not to give the sound away. Petra listened for two seconds, said nothing about it, and closed the exam-room door most of the way.

She left a hand's width of corridor in the doorway, enough to block the bed from passing eyes while a nurse could still clock him from the station. Institutional compromise sat visible in the strip of light.

"A nurse will stay on this wing," she said. "If your legs begin firing again, do not try to pin them down with the other hand. Use the call button."

"You say that like it happens a lot."

"I say it because I don't know if it happens a lot."

She took the chart and moved to the door.

"Dr. Engel."

She turned back.

"Am I the worst one so far?"

The question put the first hesitation into her body all morning. It wasn't much. A fractional stop before the answer.

"You are the worst one we know about so far," she said.

Then she went out.

The room got quiet again, carrying the rest of the building inside it. Doors were closing across the facility. Training was being cancelled mid-rep. Athletes were walking back to quarters with their bodies still attached to a network they had just been told not to trust. Somewhere down the hall, Yusuf was running toward the calibration bay before the logs disappeared into summary.

Kai looked down the length of the exam table. The sheet over his legs rose in two low ridges. Both feet pointed where they were supposed to point.

They stayed still.

He watched long enough for stillness to stop meaning safety and start meaning only this second, and this second, and this second after that.

The call button sat clipped to the rail by his good hand. It was plastic, off-white, ordinary.

He pressed it.

When the speaker answered, he said, "Could someone stay in here a bit?"

The reply came at once. "Yes. Someone's coming."

Footsteps passed in the corridor. More footsteps followed. A cart rattled by. A door slammed somewhere down the hall. The building had not settled yet and wasn't going to.

Kai kept his eyes on his feet and waited for the next movement to be his.


# Chapter 6

Yusuf had been in the calibration bay fourteen hours when Petra Engel came in with two new intake forms and said, "The hall's backing up."

Restricted operations had shut the rest of the building down around him. No training sessions, no calibrations, no scheduled maintenance. The processors and cooling system still kept up their constant sixty-hertz hum, but the silence beyond the bay door kept breaking in new places: intercom clicks, shoes in the hall, staff voices moving faster than usual. By dawn the hum had stopped sounding like infrastructure and started sounding sick. Forty-three enhanced athletes were in their quarters or the common rooms or the cafeteria, waiting for someone to tell them whether the systems inside their bodies were safe. Yusuf was supposed to be that someone.

Petra set the tablet beside his keyboard. The clinical precision of her posture had a new rigidity to it, as if staying upright required active work.

"Two more," she said.

Two medical intake forms were open on the screen. He read them while she stood beside him, not sitting, not leaning, just present.

The first was Marcus Ndlovu, twenty-six, South African, sprint relay. He'd lost all hearing for approximately three seconds during a start drill the previous morning, the same session Kai had been running on the indoor track on the other side of the facility. For those three seconds his body had stayed locked in the blocks in total silence. The hearing had returned on its own. Marcus had finished the drill, told no one, and come to medical at ten that night because he couldn't sleep and his left ear was still ringing. The intake nurse noted that he'd tried to laugh it off when he arrived. His hands had been shaking.

The second was Sonja Petrić, twenty-three, Croatian, artistic gymnastics. Her left hand had gone numb on the uneven bars during a release skill. For approximately four seconds, sensation from the wrist down had vanished completely. The synthetic grafts in her forearms had kept her grip closed on instinct, engineered muscle holding a bar her nerves couldn't feel. She'd dismounted cleanly and gone straight to medical. The intake notes recorded that she'd spent the entire wait opening and closing her left fist, over and over, checking that the fingers still answered.

Yusuf took off his glasses and rubbed the bridge of his nose before forcing himself back to the screen.

"Have you told them what it is?" he asked.

"I've told them the anomalies are under investigation. I haven't given them a mechanism." Petra's voice was measured, each word placed. It was the register she used when she was building a wall between herself and what she was about to learn. "I need to understand the scope before I say anything definitive."

"The scope is everyone."

The sentence left a metallic taste at the back of his throat. He'd rerun the count three times since dawn, hoping exhaustion had made him stupid the first two. Forty-three stayed forty-three.

Petra drew the stool out with one controlled scrape and sat. The tablet rested square on her knees, her hands folded over it so tightly the tendons showed at her wrists.

"Define everyone."

"Every enhanced athlete who's connected to the shared calibration network since the first of January." He pulled up the session log and turned the monitor so she could see. "Forty-three athletes. Everyone here except the two traditional-category trainers who don't interface with our systems."

"You're certain about the propagation mechanism?"

"Yeah." He brought up the map: nodes and lines, each node an athlete, each line a calibration session that had carried corrupted firmware. He'd built displays like this for routine load checks, boring little proofs that the bay was doing what it was meant to do. The neatness of this one made the back of his neck sweat. "Right, so it rides the handshake. Standard tuning session, standard firmware push, corrupted payload inside it. Every system that's accepted a push in the past month has got some degree of contamination."

Petra studied the screen. Forty-three nodes radiated from the central hub of his bay.

"Severity varies," Yusuf said. Kai's last session still sat on the map like a bruise, his eyes finding it before the rest. "Some systems only caught enough to cause— intermittent disruption, right, that's Marcus, that's Sonja, their firmware's got fragments but it's not— it hasn't cascaded. Others got the full payload." He swallowed. "Kai got the full payload."

"How do we determine who has partial and who has full?"

"Individual diagnostics on every system. Which means bringing athletes back into the bay and hooking them up to the same bloody network that infected them." The sentence turned his stomach every time it came round. Every obvious route to an answer started by asking people to trust the room that had done this to them. He stopped, breathed once. "So I'm building an isolated diagnostic protocol. Air-gapped from the main network. Standalone firmware analysis. It's going to take time."

"How much time?"

"Three days at least. Maybe five."

Petra stood. She touched the edge of her tablet and squared it with the workstation as if alignment itself might count as control.

"I'll inform the director," she said. "Then the athletes. All of them. They deserve to know."

"Yeah. They do."

She left. The bay door closed behind her.

Thirty seconds later the intercom clicked alive. Petra's voice came through the ceiling speaker, thinned by the cheap system but steady enough. All enhanced athletes were to report any sensory or motor anomaly to medical immediately. No one was to connect to shared systems or personal interfaces until engineering cleared them. Restricted operations would remain in effect until further notice. Hearing it out loud stripped the map back to what it was: forty-three people being told their bodies might have started making choices without them.

Yusuf stood and pulled the bay door open before the announcement finished. The space outside medical had already changed shape. A folding table had appeared outside medical with clipboards stacked at one end and symptom cards at the other. An endurance runner in thermal leggings sat with one shoe off, rubbing at a numb foot through his sock. A swimmer still had chlorine drying in white streaks along her collarbone. He knew half the corridor by scar pattern, graft seam, the way they braced before contact gel. Nobody looked confused anymore. Confusion had burned off fast. What was left was sequencing: who was next, how bad was bad, what counted, what got you locked out of your own body.

Petra was halfway down the hall, taking questions she couldn't answer yet and still making people line up one at a time. Nobody sat down yet. Yusuf watched an orderly drag over more chairs from the cafeteria. The screen behind him still held forty-three nodes, and now the space outside medical was trying to turn itself into forty-three separate emergencies. He'd spent years teaching athletes to treat the network as routine, safe enough not to think about. The thought curdled in him now.

When he looked back at the map, his hands were steady. He noticed this and couldn't tell whether it was competence or whether something in him had just gone numb.

***

Lena went to the medical bay at half past three in the afternoon because she had run out of reasons not to.

Restricted operations had emptied the facility's usual schedule. There was no pool time, no dryland training, no calibration sessions. The pool was locked. The indoor track where Kai had crashed was sealed behind yellow maintenance barriers taped across the track entrance. She'd eaten lunch in the cafeteria with Dara and two other swimmers, the four of them working through high-protein trays without talking about the one thing they were all thinking about. Afterward she'd gone back to her quarters and sat on the edge of her bed for forty minutes, doing nothing except listening for the intercom in case Petra said something worse.

By the time she reached the medical wing, Petra's announcement had turned the hall into an overflow waiting room. Cafeteria chairs lined the wall under the windows. Athletes sat in training gear and winter layers with symptom forms on their knees, too wired to read them and too frightened to put them down. Marcus Ndlovu was there with one finger pressed into his left ear as if he could keep the ringing from getting farther in. Sonja Petrić stood instead of sitting, opening and closing her left hand until the tendons jumped in her wrist. At the far end of the hall, a thrower Lena didn't know was arguing with an orderly in a voice gone thin from trying not to shout.

"Then what am I supposed to do if it happens again?"

"You come back here," the orderly said for what sounded like the fourth time. "You do not connect to anything. You wait."

Another nurse sat behind a folding table that had not been there yesterday. She slid a symptom card toward Lena without looking up.

"Everyone takes one."

"I'm only visiting," Lena said.

The nurse looked up then, taking in Lena's accreditation badge and the set of her face. "Keep it anyway. If anything happens, fill it out before you leave."

Lena folded the card once and put it in her pocket.

The room hit an old pattern in her before she'd looked at Kai properly: white cabinets, sealed trays, the sink with sensor taps, every surface asking to be kept clean. The developmental facility in Zug had trained the response into her body early. Stand still. Answer accurately. Wait for adults to decide what happened next.

Kai was in the second recovery room, propped up at a low angle on the adjustable bed. His left wrist sat inside a rigid brace that ran from his knuckles to mid-forearm. His right shoulder was held in an immobilization sling. The left side of his face showed six butterfly closures tracking the cuts along his cheekbone and brow, and the surrounding skin had swollen into a mottled yellow-purple that looked worse than yesterday, the bruising still settling into the tissue. He was watching something on the wall display and switched it off when she appeared in the doorway.

"Hey," he said.

"Hello." She stayed in the frame a second longer than necessary. "I don't have to stay. If you'd rather not."

"No, come in. It's fine." He lifted the braced hand in a stiff half-wave that visibly cost him something. "I've been doing nothing for about sixteen hours straight. I'm getting good at it."

She sat in the hard plastic visitor's chair, the kind designed to make your back ache if you stayed longer than ten minutes.

"How's the wrist?" she asked.

"Weird." He held it up. The brace caught the white overhead light. "The grafts are knitting the bone too fast. I can feel it happening in there. Like when your foot falls asleep and the blood comes back. Pins and needles, except inside the bone. Constant."

She nodded. The exact sensation wasn't familiar, but the category was. It belonged to a category she knew too well: a body doing work that should have stayed invisible, the repair itself becoming a new wrongness layered over the injury.

"And the shoulder?"

"Back in the socket. Sling for a week. Petra says full range of motion in ten days." He shifted against the pillow and grimaced as he adjusted the strap. "Which is insane. Should be six weeks. The system doesn't care."

Through the shared wall with engineering, the calibration-bay hum sat at the edge of hearing. Under it came the softer noise from outside: shoes, low voices, a clipboard slapped against a table, someone asking the same question again and getting the same answer.

"I saw Marcus Ndlovu out there," Lena said. "And Sonja Petrić."

"Yeah. Yusuf was here about an hour ago." Kai looked at the ceiling. "He says it's in the network. Everyone who's been calibrated in the past month. So everyone."

Lena's hand was at the base of her skull before she knew she'd moved it, two fingers pressed against the port as if checking it was still there.

"Mine has been doing things," she said.

She hadn't planned to say it. The sentence simply arrived, and he was looking at her with an expression that wasn't curiosity or pity or professional concern. It was recognition.

"During training?" he asked.

"During everything. Training, reading, meals." She chose each word with care, as if the order mattered. "I'll be present, and then I won't. When I come back I don't know how long I've been away."

"Your body keeps going when you're out?"

"Yes. In the pool the stroke continues. At meals I keep eating. From the outside it looks normal." She took the folded symptom card from her pocket and flattened it on her knee without meaning to start filling it out. "From the inside it isn't."

"What happens right before?"

Lena looked down at the card. It was all boxes and check marks, with a blank line marked DESCRIBE SYMPTOMS.

"Usually nothing. Sometimes there's pressure here, and then I am not..."

Kai said her name.

The pen was in her hand. She did not remember picking it up. The card lay open on the blanket over his knees, filled in with her compact handwriting.

ABSENT PERIODS.

UNAWARE OF DURATION.

MOTOR TASK CONTINUES.

NO ACUTE DISTRESS. MONITORING SUFFICIENT.

The letters were pressed hard enough to emboss the paper underneath. Her chair had scraped back from where it had been. Kai's good hand was wrapped around the call button, thumb set against the plastic but not yet pressing.

"Don't press it," Lena said.

"I nearly did."

"But you didn't."

"You were sitting up. You answered every question I asked." His thumb stayed on the plastic. "No pain. No shortness of breath. No acute distress. You sounded like a nurse doing intake. That doesn't make it fine."

"How long?" she asked.

"Maybe thirty seconds. Maybe a minute." He kept his voice low, as if louder speech might push her back out again. "You weren't asleep. You just started writing. I asked if you wanted me to get Petra and you said, 'No acute distress. Monitoring is sufficient.'"

Lena stared at the words on the card. They sounded like her and not like her. They were too clean, too detached.

"I didn't decide to say that."

"I know."

Her fingers closed harder around the pen. Only then did she feel the ache where the barrel had cut a groove into the side of her middle finger.

"What did I look like?"

Kai's eyes stayed on her face. "Focused. That's the bad part. You looked like you knew exactly what you were doing."

He looked at the button again. "I can still call Petra."

"Please don't." Lena folded the card once, badly this time, then flattened it against her knee again. "If Petra sees this now, I do not leave the medical wing. She keeps me under observation, writes an incident report, and somebody else decides when I am allowed back in the pool."

Kai's mouth tightened. "You just lost a minute sitting next to me."

"Yes."

"That's not a small thing."

"I know." Her finger rested on the line she had not meant to write. "But if I hand them this, the first complete account belongs to them. I want one copy that stays with me. I want to look at what I said when I was gone."

He stared at the pressure marks punched through the card. "That's a terrible argument."

"It's the one I have."

He kept the button in his hand. "If we'd been anywhere else, I'd have pressed this already."

"Yes."

"You're in medical. Staff are right outside. You're sitting up and arguing with me, so I'm not doing it this second. That's not the same as me thinking it's fine." He looked at the card again. "And I'm not helping you hide it. If Petra asks me whether I saw something, I'm not lying for you."

"That's fair."

"And if it happens standing up, or in the water, or you wake up somewhere else—"

"I'll tell Petra." She paused. "No. That's not exact. I will tell Petra if I lose more time than this, or if I come back somewhere different. I am not promising the polite version."

Only then did he lower the button onto the blanket.

"When the blackout happened," he said, and stopped. He was looking at his braced wrist. "I couldn't see anything. Or hear anything. And my legs kept going." He shifted against the pillow. "The grafts were still getting commands. They ran me at full speed, probably perfect form, straight into the wall. And I was just—not there." He breathed out through his nose. "The running was fine. My body ran fine. It didn't need me for that part."

Lena looked at the card between them.

"When it happens to me in the pool," she said, "my stroke gets better. More efficient." She touched the base of her skull. "The timing cleans up without me there to make it worse."

"Yeah," he said.

The room ticked forward in small medical sounds: a monitor tone every thirty seconds, the plastic shift of tubing, distant footsteps in the hall. Snow thickened on the outer window ledge. Kai's wrist kept up its deep internal buzzing while the bone knitted faster than it should have. Lena sat with the folded card in her lap and the groove from the pen still burning in her finger.

A nurse came in to check Kai's vitals. She took his blood pressure and graft integration readings in the ordinary post-trauma sequence. Conversation flattened in the nurse's presence the way it always did when a third person entered a room. Kai asked about the painkillers wearing off. Lena asked whether the queue outside had gotten any shorter. The nurse said no to both and left.

The door clicked shut. The call button still lay on the blanket by Kai's hip, and the fold in Lena's card had gone damp under her thumb.

"You really are keeping it?" Kai asked.

"For tonight," Lena said. "Tomorrow is a separate problem."

He nodded once. It was not agreement. It was what he could do instead.

"I should go," Lena said after a while.

"Yeah. OK."

At the doorway she stopped.

"The pool will still be closed tomorrow?"

"Yusuf said restricted ops for at least three more days. Maybe five."

"Then I'll probably come back, if that's all right." Her first version of the sentence died halfway out. The second one was smaller. "There isn't much else to do."

"Door's open." A trace of humor came and thinned out before it could become anything bigger. "Not like I'm going anywhere."

That almost made her smile.

The hall was still full when she stepped back into it. The same nurse behind the folding table was collecting completed symptom cards in a plastic tray under a printed sign that said REPORT EVERY EPISODE. Lena felt the folded paper in her pocket, warm from her hand.

She slowed beside the tray. If she dropped the card in, the missing minute would stop belonging to her. It would become a bed, hourly checks, staff voices deciding whether she was safe to be alone, whether she was safe to swim, whether the smooth mechanical version of her had become the real one. The card also held the only proof she had that the other voice existed at all, neat and calm in her own handwriting.

She folded it smaller and kept walking.

By the time she reached the residential wing, her hand was at the base of her skull again. It stayed there until she was inside her room with the door shut behind her.

***

Margot sat on the edge of her bed in the dark and thought about vectors. She didn't mean the mathematical kind. She meant transmission.

Petra's announcement had gone through every speaker in the building: no training, no calibrations, no personal interface connections until engineering cleared them. Everyone was to report every sensory lapse and every motor anomaly. By mid-afternoon the medical wing was jammed with athletes and symptom cards and staff pretending the line meant order instead of fear.

The calibration network wasn't the only way into an athlete's system.

Her room was filling with the grey of a winter afternoon dying in the mountains. The wall display, the stripped-down coaching version rather than the diagnostic suite the athletes got, glowed with baseline metrics: heart rate, neural baseline, integration status, all green. The display didn't know what she was thinking about.

The feed was sold as observation. It never was. Her wrist rig read the athlete and sent corrections back: pacing targets, micro-adjustments, pressure through the return channel. It gave her deeper access than a calibration tune-up and longer contact. If the virus could ride a routine handshake, the feed was a door propped open.

She ran the list in her head. Tomás Reyes had done five sessions in thirty-two days. Lena Morozova had done two. Amara from the 10K squad had done one. Hendrik, pole vault, had done one. That made nine sessions across four athletes. Nine times her equipment had opened a direct lane into someone else's system.

The thought of the rig made her mouth go wet.

She pressed the heel of her hand against the inside of her wrist where the strap usually sat. The skin there prickled anyway. Want moved through her body fast and stupid: saliva, a tightening low in the gut, the small tremor already starting in her fingers. Her jaw had already gone tight.

She should tell Yusuf.

The action itself was easy. Yusuf was in the calibration bay mapping the virus through the systems he could see. His map would be wrong without her. Feed sessions didn't touch his network. They ran through her personal hardware on a separate channel his logs couldn't reach. If the feed was carrying contamination between athletes, Marcus Ndlovu and Sonja Petrić and the rest of them were waiting on information she already had.

Margot picked up her phone. The screen lit her face in the dark. Yusuf's name was two taps away.

Her other hand was already in the bedside drawer.

The feed cuff lay next to a charging cable and two spare batteries. She touched the contact pads with her thumb. The want got worse. The pads were cold. Her body answered anyway: pulse climbing, mouth wetter, a cramped need low in the gut, as if the first second after connect had already started.

Telling Yusuf meant names, dates, durations. It meant telling him Tomás Reyes had hit two hundred and five beats per minute under her and she'd kept the return channel open because the feed felt best right there, at the edge where the body stopped hiding anything. It meant telling him she had overridden red warnings, muted safety prompts, and written altitude fatigue in a debrief after she'd pushed Tomás past the margin and watched him clip a hurdle.

It meant telling him about Lena too: the sync spikes, the stretches where the stroke stayed clean while something in the data went missing, the note Margot had entered after one session because she wanted another lap tomorrow: acceptable drift.

Petra would want the logs. Yusuf might not ask first, but Petra would. The files would show connection depth, manual overrides, suppressed alerts, every extra minute Margot had stayed in the channel after coaching stopped and appetite took over. They would show the places where coaching had ended and she had not. They would show the second vector. They would also show exactly what kind of coach she was.

She pictured handing all of that over and felt the stupid animal part of herself recoil, not from guilt, from loss. There would be no feed, no borrowed lungs, no legs loading under her before a jump, no clean hit of someone else's body arriving stronger and sharper than her own.

Somewhere outside her door, a door slammed. Margot didn't move.

The phone was still lit in one hand. The cuff was in the other.

Margot put the cuff back in the drawer.

Then she put the phone beside it and shut both away.

Her hand stayed on the wood a second too long. The tremor in her fingers did not stop. Neither did the wetness in her mouth.

She lay back on the bed fully dressed with one hand over the pale strap mark and watched the dark. The urge stayed.


# Chapter 7

The brace was off. Petra had said short stretches were fine as long as he did not get stupid with it, which left Kai the problem of deciding what counted as stupid. The graft-driven repair had run six weeks too fast. The bone in his left wrist no longer felt broken. It felt crowded. A busy, needling pressure worked at the join, new tissue packing itself tight around old fracture lines and synthetic anchors, his body hurrying through a timetable he had not agreed to.

Kai sat on the edge of his bed and put his left hand flat on the mattress.

He had started three nights ago, after his hand went early for a glass of water and he caught the quick flutter in the rebuilt forearm. Since then he had been trying to catch that flutter before the motion finished, before the hand got to pretend it had been his idea all along.

Index finger. He told himself to lift it, then waited.

There it was. A low gather in the synthetic muscle, too small for anyone else to see. He pressed the finger harder into the mattress and held. One beat. Two. Three. The pressure thickened, pushed up his forearm, found the welded ache in the wrist. Sweat pricked under his T-shirt even though the room was cold.

When he let go, the finger lifted at once.

Not much. Barely anything. Enough.

He tried again with the middle finger and got nothing but pain. Third try, the same small early kick. He caught it for half a beat, maybe less, and the success sent an ugly little flare of satisfaction through him. Private override training, one finger at a time, in a room that smelled like radiator heat and laundry powder. Petra would have called it premature loading and told him to stop. Yusuf, if he had known, would have had a more technical name for it. Kai only had the feeling of getting one hand on a door that kept trying to close.

His shoulder clicked as he reached for the brace. The joint had started settling into place with engineered neatness. The cuts on his cheek and brow were already down to thin pink lines. He was healing on graft time, being rebuilt around the damage, and none of it felt like recovery exactly. More like being overtaken.

He fastened the brace, flexed once against the strap, and went to dinner.

---

The cafeteria was half-empty at seven-thirty. Most of the athletes ate at six, their days partitioned into training blocks and recovery windows that left no room for late meals unless a session had run long or someone was avoiding company. The stragglers were support staff finishing shifts, a pair of physiotherapists arguing about ice bath protocols at a corner table, and Kai.

He took a tray through the line. Chicken breast, rice, steamed broccoli that had sat in the warmer long enough to give up, and a cup of coffee that tasted like it had been brewed at noon and punished ever since. The windows along the far wall showed the mountains as dark cutouts against a sky holding its last blue, that deep alpine February color that made dusk seem to start before anyone was ready for it.

Lena was sitting alone near the windows. She had a plate of pasta half-finished and a book open beside her tray, though she was not reading it. Her thumb sat between the same pages as if she had been holding a place for a long time.

Kai set his tray down across from her. "Hey."

She looked up like surfacing. "Hey." She moved the book closer to her plate, making room, but she did not close it.

"What are you reading?"

She glanced at the cover as though she had to check. "A novel. In German. I keep starting it."

"That good, huh."

"Apparently not."

He smiled a little and sat. The joke had not cost much. That felt useful.

They ate without talking for a minute. The room gave them enough noise to hide inside: trays being cleared in the kitchen, the physiotherapists still fighting about inflammation protocols, the heating system working against the alpine cold on the other side of the glass. Kai's shoulder clicked when he raised the fork. He had mostly stopped noticing it except when the room was quiet enough for the sound to travel.

"How's the wrist?" Lena asked.

"Getting there." He lifted the hand a little. "Petra says I can start grip exercises next week. Which is doctor language for stop doing them now."

"You were already doing them."

"I was messing with it. That feels different."

"It doesn't sound different."

"That's fair." He set the hand down. "The bone's basically done. Solid. It still feels unfinished, though. Like somebody hit fast-forward and skipped the part where your body catches up."

Lena nodded. She pushed a piece of penne around her plate without eating it. "My mother broke her arm when I was twelve. Skiing. She wore the cast for eight weeks and complained about it every day. I remember thinking eight weeks sounded impossible."

"It's impossible when you're twelve."

"I know. I didn't know it then." She shook her head once. "I thought adults were weak."

Kai drank the coffee and regretted it immediately. "Where was that? The skiing."

"Engelberg. We went every February when I was young." She paused, then corrected herself. "My parents went. I went sometimes. Usually I was in training camp."

"Every February?"

"Every school holiday, mostly. The program ran blocks all year. Summer was Tenero for the outdoor pool. Winter was altitude work here or in Austria. Spring was a facility outside Munich for strength testing." She said it in sequence, like stops read off a board. Then she winced at herself. "That sounded like a brochure."

"I've heard worse brochures."

She almost smiled. "I was home ten days at Christmas and two weeks in August."

"Home being where?"

"Zug. My parents' apartment. But I'd been living at training sites since I was eight, so." She made a small closing motion with her hand. "The word catches when I say home."

He looked at her across the table. The overhead light flattened everyone a little, trays and faces and steam pressed into the same pale plane. At the base of her skull, where her hair was pulled up, the port caught his eye the way a scar did: part of her body, impossible to stop seeing once noticed.

"Did you like it?" he asked. "The program."

She looked down at her plate. "I liked swimming. The program..." She stopped, shook her head once. "No. That's me making it sound balanced. I liked the water. The rest was already decided. My parents signed the Meridian developmental contract before I was born. Embryo screening, the link at four, calibration every six months. By the time I had opinions, the paperwork was older than I was."

"It was there from the start."

"Maybe. Yes. But when people say that, it sounds organic. It was signatures. Appointments. Adults saying excellent." She picked up her fork and put it down again. "When I was five or six they had me in the rig twice a week. The technician would say, can you feel this, can you feel that. I always said yes because yes made the session shorter. Maybe I felt it. Maybe I was just good at being efficient. I can't separate those now."

"Jesus."

She gave one shoulder the smallest lift. "It's what I have."

Kai split a broccoli stalk with the side of his fork and ate it.

"I had a before," he said after a minute. "A long one. Long enough to be annoying about it, anyway." He nudged rice into a neater shape than he needed to. "So now when the graft jumps the line, I can feel that it jumped a line. There's still an old version under it. Or right behind it. Whatever."

Lena looked at him for a beat. "Yes. Close."

He took another mouthful of rice. "Where's your Lafayette?"

She frowned.

"Lafayette, Indiana. Sorry. My version of home." He leaned back a little. "Fields behind my parents' house. Soybeans forever. Summer storms you could watch coming for an hour before they hit. And the high school track where I first ran, cracked rubber and floor wax and water stains in the ceiling. That's the picture I get."

"I don't think I have that."

"Nothing?"

She took her time. "There was an old pool in Zug. Before they renovated it. Windows on the east wall." Her thumb worried at the edge of the napkin. "At six in the morning the light came through the water and striped the tiles. I used to get there before anyone else if I could." She looked past him at the dark window. "Not for the pool exactly. For the ten minutes before it turned into a training place." Her mouth pulled tight for a second. "That is probably it. Not a town. Just one room, empty."

"One room still counts."

"Maybe. I don't know if it counts the same way." Her voice stayed gentle. "You miss weather. Your school. Probably the road out to it. I miss one room for ten minutes of the day. That's different."

Kai finished his rice. The cafeteria had thinned to almost no one, a janitor starting to mop the far end of the floor in broad patient strokes.

"You said your mother broke her arm skiing," he said. "Does she still ski?"

"Yes. With great confidence and moderate talent."

"That sounds like every skier I've ever met."

"It's not untrue." This time the smile came and stayed half a second. Then it went. "She sent me a package last week."

"What kind of package?"

"A mother package. Chocolate from the shop in Zug, wool socks I didn't ask for, and a letter telling me to rest." Lena opened the book and unfolded a strip of waxed paper she had been using as a marker. Inside were two dark squares of chocolate gone slightly soft at the edges from the heat of the room. "I finally opened it this afternoon because even I could see I was being absurd."

"You're hiding chocolate in literature now?"

"It improves both." She broke one square in half and slid a piece across to him on the waxed paper. "Take it."

"You sure?"

"It's not medicine."

He picked it up with his right hand. Orange peel, dark cocoa, the kind of expensive bitterness Midwestern supermarkets sold in a locked case near Christmas and called European. "This is way better than anything in here."

"That's a very low bar."

"Still." He chewed, swallowed, and looked at the other half still on the paper. "Why leave the package unopened?"

She considered that. "Because if I opened it I had to answer it."

"That sounds bleak for socks."

"It's only partly about the socks." She looked at the book instead of him. "My mother thinks I'm dealing with a virus outbreak and a bad schedule. She thinks I'm tired, busy, irritated. All the normal versions." She turned the waxed paper over with one finger. "Not that I'm losing pieces of training. If I answer, I have to decide how much of the real week goes back to Zug."

Kai turned his coffee cup a quarter turn.

"I do that with calls," he said. "Not the time-loss part. Different disaster. My mom asks how rehab's going and I talk about putty exercises and cafeteria coffee and how annoyed Petra gets when I pick things up with the bad hand. I don't mention the hand reaching early sometimes."

"You haven't told Petra."

"Nope."

Lena lifted her eyes. "That seems unwise."

"It does, yeah." He scraped the last of the chicken into one corner of the plate. "I keep thinking if I can get one clean read on it first, one thing I can name, then it becomes real in a useful way instead of just more trouble."
