She waited a beat longer than most people would have. "That's also not wise."

"You're having a real banner night for bedside manner."

"I'm trying honesty."

"Terrible habit. Don't start."

The smile came back, smaller this time. He looked down at his tray and pushed a loose grain of rice through the sauce with his fork.

"So," he said. "We're both sending home the cleaned-up version."

"Yes."

"Badly?"

"Probably." She pushed her plate away a few centimeters. "Although my mother prefers optimism to accuracy. It makes the work easier."

"My mom prefers worst-case scenarios and prayer. That's its own kind of work."

Lena folded the napkin beside her tray into a smaller square without looking down. "Does it help her?"

"I think it keeps her busy."

He had not meant to keep going, but the room was almost empty now, the janitor closer, the mop water squeaking faintly across the tiles. The late hour made continuation feel less like disclosure and more like failing to stop in time.

"She asks me things on the phone," he said. "Old stuff. Does your knee still pop going downstairs. Do you still get that cramp in your calf when it rains." He shook his head. "And I keep saying no, that one's gone. No, that got replaced. No, that doesn't hurt like that anymore."

"What does she say?"

"Nothing. She just moves to the next one." He rubbed the side of the coffee cup with his thumb. "I think she's trying to find one complaint that still belongs to the old me. That sounds worse now that I said it."

Lena watched him a moment. "And are you."

He let the question sit between them a moment.

"Sometimes," he said. He looked down at the cup again. "If the wrist aches or the shoulder clicks, there's this stupid half-second where I'm relieved. I know that one. It belongs to me." He rubbed harder at the paper sleeve on the cup. "That sounds bad too."

"No," she said quietly. "I know."

His thumb went still on the paper sleeve.

The janitor parked the mop bucket by the next table and gave them the look people in institutional buildings gave when they wanted a surface empty without having to say so.

Lena closed her book and stacked it on her tray. "Do you want to walk?"

"Outside? It's freezing."

"I know."

"That wasn't a counterargument."

"No."

He glanced at the window, at the dark pressed up against the glass. "Yeah. All right."

---

The cold hit them like a wall. They had grabbed jackets from the row of hooks by the cafeteria's side door, the heavy insulated ones the facility kept for anyone moving between buildings in winter, and even through the padding the air found every gap. Minus twelve, maybe colder. Lena felt it in her sinuses first, a sharp chemical cleanliness the building's recirculated air never achieved. Then in her teeth. Then in the thin strip of skin between glove and sleeve.

The path from the cafeteria to the residential wing was cleared and salted, but beyond the buildings the snow was deep, banked into drifts the maintenance crew had pushed to the margins with their plows. The sky was black and enormous. Stars came through in a density the facility's light pollution could not fully wash out, though the sodium glow from the training halls yellowed the nearest snow and faded to nothing within a hundred meters.

They walked past the residential wing and onto the access road that led down toward the valley. The road was plowed but icy in stretches, and Lena watched her footing with the automatic caution of someone trained since childhood to protect her body from unnecessary injury. The mountains were shapes at this hour rather than scenery, massive dark presences that blocked the stars where they met the horizon, their ridgelines rough against the sky.

Kai walked with his hands shoved into his jacket pockets, shoulders hunched against the cold. His breath made small clouds that the air tore apart before they travelled a meter.

"I always forget how many stars there are up here," he said.

"You can see the Milky Way in summer. When it's clear."

"I haven't been here in summer."

"It's different. The lower fields go green and there are wildflowers on the lower slopes. The snow pulls back to the high ridges." She looked up toward the dark line above them. "No. That's not it. You can see the rock again."

"I knew what you meant."

"That doesn't make it a good sentence."

"It was fine."

They reached the bend where the view opened: the valley below, dark peaks across the gap, the town of Meiringen visible as a scatter of warm lights on the black floor of it. The air smelled like pine resin, cold stone, and the faint metal tang that snow sometimes had before more weather came in.

Kai stopped first. She did too.

"Can I ask you something?" he said.

"Yes."

"When did you first notice the desync?"

She had answered that before, for Margot, for diagnostics, for herself in the inventory she ran each night in bed. But Kai was not asking for data in the way the others did. He had too much of his own.

"In October. Maybe late October." She tucked her hands deeper into her pockets. "I came off a wall and hit a clean gap where the turn should have been. I had the breath after it. I had the next stroke. The turn itself was gone."

"Did you know in the moment?"

"Not exactly. Not there." She breathed in through her nose; the cold pinched high under her eyes. "The fear came after. When I looked at the telemetry. If it had been messy I think I would have felt it. But the bad part is it wasn't messy."

"But you knew it happened."

"Because the data said it did." She folded her arms tighter. "And the data liked it. The lengths I barely remember are cleaner. Straighter line. Better tempo." She stopped, dissatisfied with the neatness of the sentence. "That sounds almost good. It's not. It means whatever keeps swimming when I'm gone may be better at it than I am."

Kai let out a breath that might have been a laugh in warmer air. "Fuck."

"Yes."

He opened his mouth, closed it again, and stamped one foot once on the ice before looking back down into the valley. Headlights moved along the river road below, tracing the bend and disappearing behind a stand of trees.

"My mother keeps asking whether restricted operations means I have more time to rest," Lena said. "As if the problem is admin."

"You going to tell her?"

"Not now." She pressed her chin deeper into the collar of the jacket. "I can't decide if that's protection or cowardice. Probably both."

Kai rubbed his face with his good hand. "Same. Different version."

"You're still not telling Petra about the hand."

"No."

"Will you?"

He gave a small shrug. "Probably when it does anything embarrassing in public."

"That's not a plan."

"It's the plan currently available."

She should have let that go. Instead she said, "I think I do the same thing with everyone here. There's the symptom I report because it fits in a form. Then there is the other part."

"What other part?"

Lena watched her breath leave and vanish. "The minute after. The checking. Looking at a clock and doing the math backward. Looking at people to see if they are behaving like there was a gap I missed."

Kai did not answer at once. His silence was careful, not blank.

"Yeah," he said finally. "The counting after. Seeing if anybody else noticed."

The wind moved across the road again, lighter this time, just enough to set dry crystals skating over the salt. For a while they watched the lights below.

Then Kai pulled his left hand from his pocket and flexed it. The brace creaked in the cold. Lena watched him open and close his fingers, slow and deliberate, waiting for the early jump.

"You're doing it again," she said.

He glanced over. "I know."

"What are you checking?"

He took a second. "The synthetic muscle from the reconstruction jumps early sometimes. Not much. Just enough that I can feel it if I pay attention." He flexed again, slower this time. "I've been trying to catch that little kick and hold it off."

"Can you?"

"A little. Or maybe I just make it later. Hard to tell."

"Show me."

"There's not much to show."

"Not see. Feel."

He hesitated, then pulled his right glove off with his teeth and tucked it in his pocket. Lena did the same with hers. The air hit her bare fingers like a burn. She stepped closer and set two fingertips lightly against the inside of his forearm above the brace, where the repaired muscle sat under skin and synthetic weave.

"All right," he said quietly. "Wait."

He flexed.

Nothing at first, only the cold skin of his arm and the brace pressing into her knuckles. Then a small hard gather jumped under her fingers, precise and unpleasant, like a cable pulling tight in a wall. Half a beat later his hand closed.

Lena looked up at him.

"That was before," she said.

"Yeah."

He flexed again and she felt it a second time, now that she knew where to look for it. The motion itself was almost an afterthought. The first thing was the warning.

She took her hand back and pressed it against the scarf at the base of her neck, where the port sat underneath.

"I wonder if mine does that," she said. "Not the blank. The first second before it."

"Maybe."

"In the pool I only know after. I miss time, then I look at the telemetry and build the missing part backward." She kept her hand at her neck. "Or Margot asks what I remember and I give her the hole. That's all after."

"It's a different system."

"Yeah. But it still gets there ahead of you."

"Maybe. Or maybe I only tell myself that once I see the data." She looked at him. "This would be different. Standing still. One cue. You'd be outside it."

She kept her hand there, thinking of turns, of tiled walls, of the half-second before the world went missing. The cold had sharpened every surface. She could feel the seam of the scarf under her glove, the hard oval of the port below skin and scar tissue, the pulse in two fingers pressed against it.

"No," she said.

Kai looked at her. "No what?"

"Not tomorrow." She pulled off her right glove and tucked it in her pocket. The air bit at once. "Now. If I wait until tomorrow I'll file it under bad ideas and never do it."

"What are you doing?"

"I want to see if there's a lead-in." She slid her fingers under the scarf and touched the skin just below the port. The cold made the metal rim feel deeper than it was. "Not in the water. Here. Standing still." She swallowed. "You say now whenever you want. I turn when I hear it. You watch. I watch the port. If the link gets there first, maybe one of us catches that first movement."

Kai did not move. "On the ice?"

"I'm standing still."

"That's not the same as safe."

"It's close enough." She swallowed. "Please."

The word came out flatter than she intended. He heard the need under it anyway.

"All right," he said. "But if it feels off, we stop."

She nodded once. She fixed her eyes on the facility lights and tried to loosen the back of her neck.

The first time he said it, she turned cleanly. Nothing but cold skin and the ordinary pull of muscles under the jaw.

The second time she thought she felt a pull, but it was only fear arriving early and calling itself data.

The third time a hard, thin tightening drew below the port before she had fully taken in the word.

"Now."

Her chin started left. She caught it halfway. Not a big movement. A twitch. The muscles along the scar locked under her fingers, a wire pulled tight. She tried to hold still and the correction shifted somewhere else.

The valley lights narrowed to one yellow window in the residential wing. Sound flattened. Her right hand slid higher under the scarf without instruction, thumb jamming against the metal rim, nail scraping skin. For a moment it felt as if her body had stepped half a pace ahead and was waiting for the rest of her to catch up.

"Lena," she heard, but from distance.

Then his hand was around her left forearm, hard enough to hurt through the jacket.

"Lena."

The world came back all at once: the sting in her neck, the road under her boots, the cold in her bare fingers, Kai standing too close because he had had to get there fast. She was pitched slightly toward the icy edge of the road.

She took one breath. Then another.

"How long?"

"Three seconds. Maybe four." His grip eased but did not leave her arm. "You went still. Then your hand went for your neck. Before that your chin twitched left, like the turn started and got checked."

She pulled her hand out from under the scarf. Even in the dark she could feel the wetness on the side of her thumb where the nail had cut skin.

"Did I stop it at all?"

He looked at her face as if checking whether she was fully back. "A little. I think. You caught the turn for a second. After that it moved somewhere else."

"So there is a lead-in."

"There was one tonight," he said. "Standing still, with me giving the cue. I know that much. I don't know if that's what happens in the pool every time or if we just tripped a different bad thing."

That made sick sense. She hated that it made sense.

She looked past him at the snowbank, white with sodium light, and saw the clean print of one boot sliding half an inch toward the drop before his hand had caught her.

"Again," she said.

"No."

The refusal was immediate. Not sharp. Not negotiable.

"I need to know."

"You know enough to do the next one somewhere that is not frozen concrete over a hill."

"It's a road."

"It's a mountain road."

He was right. The irritation that rose in her had nowhere useful to go. She closed her bare hand into a fist and felt the sting at her neck answer it.

"Sorry," he said a moment later, because he had read the silence wrong.

"No." She shook her head once. "Don't say sorry. Just say what happened."

"I did."

"More exactly."

Kai let go of her arm. His hand had left a band of warmth through the jacket that the cold was already eating.

"Your eyes changed," he said. "Not dramatic. They just stopped tracking. Like you were looking through the buildings instead of at them. And you were digging at the port hard enough to bleed."

She touched the side of her neck and winced. The skin there felt raw, a small crescent of heat in all the cold.

"Shit," she said, the word awkward in her mouth.

"Yeah."

For a second neither of them moved. The mountains stayed where they were. The lights below kept burning. Her pulse thudded in the cut under her scarf.

"I hate..." She stopped and pressed her thumb against the cut at her neck. "I hate that you know what I look like when it happens."

He nodded once. "I figured."

She waited through one breath, then another. He only stood there in the cold, steam leaving his mouth. Her thumb eased off the cut at her neck.

Then he said, "What I hate is you were already doing this alone. Maybe not out here yet. But somewhere."

Lena looked at him. He had gone very still again.

The cold had worked through the jacket now and settled in her joints. Her fingertips were going numb again, though the scrape at her neck still burned.

"We should go in," Kai said.

"Yes."

He bent and picked up the glove she had dropped without noticing. He had to use his right hand. The left stayed stiff at his side. He held the glove out to her and she took it. Their fingers touched, nothing more than that, but she felt the contact all the way up her forearm.

They walked back along the access road slower than before. Kai stayed on the outside edge, between her and the dark drop to the valley, like he was not making a decision about it and had only happened to drift there. The facility grew as they approached, its windows bright squares of warm light. Before they reached the side door, Lena could hear the heating system through the wind, a low mechanical hum riding over the snow.

At the side door Kai pulled the handle and warm air poured out around them, thick and almost sweet after the thin cold. Lena stepped inside first. Fluorescent light laid everything flat again: linoleum floor, the smell of cleaning solution and residual cafeteria grease, melted snow darkening the entry mat.

She took off the heavy jacket and hung it on its hook. When she unwound the scarf, Kai saw the thin red mark before she could tuck it away. Not much blood. Enough to show.

"You were right," she said, not looking at him. "About the ice."

He pulled a face. "I hate being right in a doctor's voice."

"That wasn't a doctor's voice."

"What was it?"

"Annoying teammate voice."

"I can live with that."

She checked the blood on her thumb, rubbed it away on a paper napkin from the hook shelf, and regretted that immediately when the paper scraped the cut.

At the point where the residential wing branched, they both stopped. The outer door thudded once behind them as the wind leaned against it.

"What does it look like from outside?" she asked. "When it starts."

Kai shifted his weight. He would rather have answered a question about bone density or split times. That was clear.

"Your face goes flat first," he said. "Not empty. Just less like you. Then your eyes fix on one thing and stop doing the little adjustments. That's when I said your name."

She nodded slowly. The description hurt in a way the cut did not.

"All right."

"Kai."

"Yeah?"

"If it happens again, don't wait to be polite."

"I won't."

"Even if we're in public."

"Okay."

The knot in her chest loosened enough for a full breath.

"Good night," she said.

"Night."

She went left. At her room door her fingers slipped once on the handle, then caught. When she glanced back, he was still there, not half-raising a hand this time, just waiting long enough to see the door open before he turned away.


# Chapter 8

Kai saw the Meridian delegation before he finished breakfast: three black SUVs grinding up the mountain road through overnight snow. He watched them from the cafeteria windows one-handed, fork in his right, coffee cooling untouched beside him. The vehicles pulled into the loading bay with coordinated timing, one after another, as if they'd practiced the spacing. Three people climbed out of the first: two men in dark coats and a woman in a grey wool suit who crossed the icy concrete without slipping, a leather portfolio held flat against her chest.

"That's Jin-seo Park," Tomas said across the table, leaning forward to follow Kai's gaze. "Athlete liaison. She was at the Seoul orientation last year." He paused with his spoon over a bowl of porridge. "She's all right. She actually listens."

Kai kept watching the woman in grey. Behind her, the rear doors opened on the second SUV and more people stepped out with hard cases, laptop bags, a flat archive box the size of a legal file. Nobody came up the mountain with that much paper to make life easier.

Outside, the sky was the flat grey of late February at 2,100 meters, snow stacked along the ridgelines in wind-carved drifts. The second and third SUVs emptied out behind the first: technicians, lawyers, or whatever Meridian sent when it wanted to remind people who ran the network inside their nerves. The noise in the cafeteria thinned around the windows. Someone two tables over said, very quietly, "Fuck."

Tomas watched with a different expression now, attentive and measuring, the look of someone keeping track of people who could turn a season on or off. "They don't usually send this many," he said.

"No."

"You think it's about the virus?"

Kai finished his coffee. "Yeah, Tomas. I think it's about the virus."

---

An hour later, thirty-four athletes and most of the Kirchberg senior staff filled the second-floor conference room. The space had been rearranged overnight. Training boards were stacked against the wall. The usual tables were gone, replaced by rows of chairs facing a long table at the front. A projection screen glowed with Meridian's logo. Printed binders waited at every seat, each one tabbed in red and marked with an athlete name in block print. So did bottled water stamped with the Meridian mark, which had not appeared anywhere in the facility before that morning. Someone had carried those bottles up the mountain on purpose.

The fluorescent lights buzzed at a frequency Kai had stopped hearing weeks ago. He sat near the right wall, three rows back, his chair angled so he could see the door and the front table at once. Lena was two rows ahead, hair still damp from the morning swim, the faint chlorine smell that never fully left her. Margot stood against the far wall with her arms crossed, watching the room with flat coaching attention. Yusuf sat in the front row beside Dr. Engel. His hands were folded. Petra's back was straight, mouth thin, the posture of someone who had been briefed and hated what she heard.

Jin-seo Park stood behind the table. She was smaller than the job made you expect: compact, dark hair cut at the jaw, no jewelry except a slim watch she touched once before she started. She waited for the room to settle without asking for silence. When it did, she opened with Kai.

"I know some of you," she said. "Several from Seoul. Others from your files, which isn't the same thing." She let that sit for a beat. "Eight days ago, one of you hit a wall at sprint speed because his body kept running after his senses cut out. I've read the medical report. I've seen the telemetry. What I'm about to show you predates Mr. Brennan's crash by eight months. Meridian has been tracking this vulnerability for eight months. The disclosure moved because of what happened to Mr. Brennan."

She glanced at Kai. The room followed her glance. He felt attention turn toward him, quick and hot, then settle. A Hungarian weightlifter two seats ahead turned all the way around to look at him, then faced front again.

"Eight months," the weightlifter said. "You sat on it for eight months."

"The initial detection was eight months ago," Jin-seo said. "Characterizing the virus took the rest of that time. The timeline was bad. Here's what we have now."

She tapped the screen. A schematic filled the wall: the calibration network that linked every enhanced athlete in the building, clean blue lines and white nodes, dressed up to look reassuring. Kai had seen rougher versions on Yusuf's whiteboards, coffee-ringed and half erased. This one looked built for an investor call.

"The virus propagates through the shared calibration network," Jin-seo said. "Routine calibrations, firmware syncs, diagnostics. Every time the network opens a transmission window, the corruption has a path. It targets the firmware layer that governs sensory processing. When it triggers, you lose sight, hearing, proprioception, spatial orientation, all of it. It's a total blackout. The autonomous subsystems in your enhancements don't stop because you stop perceiving them. Your legs keep driving. Your arms keep stroking. Whatever output the system was running, it keeps running."

No one spoke. The silence had pressure in it. Thirty-four people kept questions under their tongues. Someone shifted in a chair and the creak sounded too loud.

"We've developed a firmware patch that cuts the replication routine out of the sensory-routing layer and rewrites the tables it keeps corrupting. Installation takes about four hours per athlete in the calibration bay. The access route is the same one used for a full recalibration. Dr. Qadir's team would handle the procedure." She nodded at Yusuf. He did not nod back. "Meridian has run it against captured infected handshake traffic from three facilities and on retired enhancement stacks under load simulation. In those tests, the blackout trigger did not recur."

Someone near the back said, "That's not a body."

"No," Jin-seo said. "It isn't. What we know in live systems comes from the access route, not long follow-up on this payload. Expect headache, nausea, and bad sensory lag for a day if your handshake settles slowly. No heavy training until the bay clears you."

"What's the price?" Margot asked from the wall.

Jin-seo turned to her. "The patch requires full read-write access to the firmware layer of your enhancement system, and Meridian would retain that access permanently in order to maintain compatibility with future updates."

"Access to what, exactly?"

Jin-seo picked up one of the binders and turned to a marked page. "I'll read it directly. Better that than a cleaned-up version."

She looked down at the text.

"'In consideration of the Installation and ongoing maintenance of the Meridian Apex Enhancement Stability Protocol, the Athlete hereby grants to Meridian BioSystems, its successors and assigns, an irrevocable, transferable license to all biometric, genetic, neural, and physiological data generated by or through the Enhancement System, including but not limited to real-time performance metrics, neurological activity patterns, genetic expression data, and baseline physiological markers. This license shall survive termination of any sponsorship agreement, retirement from competitive athletics, or deactivation of the Enhancement System.'"

She set the binder down and kept her eyes on the room.

The binders opened across the room, first a few, then almost all at once. Paper rasped. Tabs clicked against metal rings. Meridian had color-coded the surrender: blue for installation terms, red for data rights, grey for dispute resolution, a yellow insert for calibration scheduling. Kai flipped three pages with his thumb and found the same sentence coming back in different clothes, each version of it written to outlast sponsorship, retirement, even deactivation. Somebody in the back laughed once under his breath and stopped when no one joined him.

The first voice came from the second row. A Swedish cyclist, narrow face gone red at the ears. "Transferable means what? You can sell it?"

"It means Meridian can sublicense data to research partners, medical institutions, and other authorized entities under the standard data-sharing framework."

"So you can sell it."

"The framework includes restrictions on who can access it," Jin-seo said, and another athlete cut across her.

"Can you or can't you?" another athlete said. Behind him someone was still turning between tabs — "Which section is that even in?"

"Answer him," somebody else snapped.

Jin-seo took one breath. "Yes. Meridian can sell or sublicense the data."

The room moved without getting louder. Heads turned. A chair leg scraped. Kai watched the back of Lena's head. She had not moved. Her hands lay flat on her thighs, still in that full-body stillness she went to when she was forcing herself to keep processing.

Aissatou Diop spoke from near the front. She did not raise a hand. "If I don't sign and I go to Doha, what am I doing? Getting into the blocks and hoping I stay able to see lane three?"

"The Apex Games will let you compete without the patch," Jin-seo said.

Aissatou gave a short, ugly laugh. "That's not what I asked."

Jin-seo nodded once. "If you compete unpatched, the blackout risk remains. In sprint conditions, on apparatus, in the pool, on the bike, the outcome could be fatal."

Dr. Engel leaned forward. "The medical assessment supports that," she said. Her voice stayed careful, each word weighed. "The blackout risk under maximal exertion is real. I have reviewed the clinical data. The install itself uses a route your systems already tolerate for recalibration, so the immediate procedural risk is lower than the room seems to think. What I do not have is follow-up data under competition load. I can't speak to the contract. I can speak to the danger."

That was enough for the first wave. A German javelin thrower with a pale integration scar at his collarbone opened his binder, skipped straight to the signature tab, and signed as fast as if he were approving a hotel bill. He shut the binder and shoved it to the edge of his seat, then wiped his palm once on his thigh.

Across the aisle, the Swedish cyclist who had kept pushing on transferable held his pen above the page for a long second. He looked toward the windows, not really seeing them, jaw working. Then he wrote his name hard enough that Kai heard the nib squeal.

The swimmer in the front row had already found the schedule insert clipped behind the contract. She checked the first available calibration slot, checked it again, and signed with the quick blank face of somebody choosing between bad timing and worse timing.

Aissatou uncapped her pen and set the point to the line. The tendons in the back of her hand stood out. After a few seconds she capped the pen again and slid it under the binder's spine.

At the back, three athletes from smaller federations bent over the page together, not conferring, just moving at the same moment because the room had finally shown them what refusing cost. One signed. One didn't. One kept turning pages like more pages might help. By the time the room spoke again, twelve binders sat at the aisle or in signed hands.

---

The remaining athletes sat with binders on their laps or open in their hands. The room had split cleanly enough that Kai could see it from his chair. One side had signed and was trying not to look at the paper anymore. The other still kept touching pages, turning back, checking clauses, as if there might be a version in there that was less final.

Adewale, a middle-distance runner from the Nigerian team, lifted his binder off his knees. "This part — survives retirement. So when I'm done, when I'm forty-five coaching kids somewhere, they still have all of this?"

"The license survives retirement," Jin-seo said. "Yes."

He let the binder fall back to his lap. "Christ."

Forty-five hit Kai harder than irrevocable had. Forty-five was a real age, not contract language. It was some other life starting, and Meridian was still reaching into it.

A Brazilian gymnast two rows over cut in before Jin-seo could continue, chalked finger pressed to the page. "Baseline physiological markers — that's not even — that's my blood."

"The license covers all data generated by or through the Enhancement System," Jin-seo said. "Because the system interfaces with baseline biology, the streams stay tangled in practice."

The gymnast said blood and Kai felt his wrist answer inside the brace, a slow ugly throb. Meridian already had the crash footage, the scans, the telemetry. Blood made it sound as if even the part of him that predated the system had stopped being his.

"Those are separate requirements," Yusuf said.

It snapped every head in the room toward him. He stayed seated. His hands were still folded, but now the knuckles were white.

Jin-seo looked at him. "Dr. Qadir."

"Emergency firmware access is one requirement," Yusuf said. His voice was too level, which meant he was working hard at it. "Permanent rights to baseline biological data is a completely separate — those are different technical problems. You don't need the second to do the first. I can see from the install manifest where the patch wants to sit and what layer it's supposed to protect. I can't audit sealed Meridian code once it's living inside your system."

Kai had spent weeks letting Yusuf explain what his own body was doing. Hearing him draw the line here, with Meridian three feet away, made the binder in Kai's lap feel heavier. If Yusuf was willing to say this part out loud, the grab was worse than the room had let itself believe.

One of the Meridian men at the wall shifted his weight. Jin-seo lifted a hand without looking at him and he stopped.

"On a clean system design, sure," she said. "On what's actually deployed — you've got legacy builds, live support obligations across multiple facilities. They don't come apart on this timeline."

Petra spoke before Yusuf could answer. "They are different categories, yes," she said. "The install profile reads like an aggressive recalibration: headache, nausea, sensory lag, maybe a day or two of poor timing while the system settles. We can supervise that here. What we cannot supervise yet is weeks of race-load behavior, because that evidence does not exist. The contract as written asks for more than the technical problem requires, and the blackout risk will still be here while people try to negotiate something narrower."

That landed harder than Yusuf's objection. Yusuf could be dismissed as technical, territorial, difficult. Petra almost never stepped outside medical scope unless she had to. Kai looked down at the red tab under his thumb and saw a trap arranged into sections.

Several people who had already signed pulled their binders back into their laps. The German javelin thrower opened his again with two fingers, staring at his own signature as if he no longer remembered making it. Somebody in the back said, "Then why is it written like this?" Another voice came over it: "Can I take mine back?" A third said, "Don't touch them yet."

No one signed. No one even uncapped a pen.

Jin-seo put her fingertips against the edge of the portfolio. For a second she looked like she was choosing between three versions of herself and not trusting any of them.

"In 2034," she said, "my brother was in the Asia-Pacific qualifiers. He was a triathlete on a Meridian system, the same hardware generation some of you are still on." She looked at the room, not the table. "During the cycling stage his firmware went into cascade failure. One leg fired full extension and the other locked. He was doing forty kilometers an hour on wet pavement."

The room went still enough that Kai could hear the projector fan.

Kai pictured the crash too easily. He saw wet pavement, one leg locked, the other still firing, the body obeying a system after the rider had already lost the bargain. His shoulders tightened before he noticed.

"He went over the bars and hit shoulder first, then head, then the bike came down on him. He broke his neck, three ribs, and his pelvis." Her voice stayed even. The corporate polish was gone. "He stayed alive for nine hours after the crash. The cascade was still firing in his legs when they got him to the trauma unit. They strapped him down because the muscles kept kicking against the bed. No one there could reach the base layer to shut it off. Meridian kept the authorization code on a corporate channel. The nearest technician with access was in Singapore."

She drank from her water bottle and set it back down with care.

"Jun-ho died in Busan at two in the morning while an engineer was still in the air over the South China Sea." She looked at Yusuf when she said the next part, then at the room again. "So — yes. They're different things. Emergency access and data rights, those are different problems and Meridian knows it. They bundle them because that's what Meridian does." She touched the edge of the binder in front of her. "I brought these up the mountain anyway because the patch is real and the blackout is real and I was in that room."

Aissatou's fingers stopped drumming on her binder. The Swedish cyclist looked down at his own signature as if he might still be able to smudge it. No one said anything.

Margot had not changed position at the wall, but her jaw had locked hard enough to show in profile. Yusuf stared at the schematic on the screen as if he could peel the system open by looking at it. Petra lowered her eyes to the table and pressed her thumb once against the seam of her notebook.

Then Kai heard paper slide against paper. A pen cap clicked.

He knew from the drag of paper and the click of the cap that it was Tomas. He looked anyway.

Tomas turned past the signature line once, then back to it. His thumb rested for a second on the small-print cross-reference at the bottom of the page, the part that tied this consent to the rest of Meridian's sponsorship machinery. He signed in careful block letters, printed the date, and shut the binder.

Kai knew that logic. Meridian never left a decision in one document if it could wire it into five. Refusal would not stay inside the calibration bay. It would move into travel approval, housing, the stipend schedule, every checkbox buried in the contracts athletes stopped reading after the first month. Kai had savings, shoe-company money, old deals under other logos. Tomas had a first Games berth and the same blue M on every page that mattered.

Tomas set the binder on the empty seat beside him and folded his hands in his lap. He looked straight ahead, accusing no one and asking nothing. It was just the face of someone who had found the narrowest place the trap would let him stand.

It wasn't reverence after that. It was arithmetic.

Near the back, one of the athletes who had been whispering through the whole meeting, a narrow man in federation warm-ups three seasons old, asked without lifting his head, "If I wait, do I lose my place?"

One of the Meridian men answered from the wall. "Patch scheduling will prioritize completed agreements."

Nobody in the room pretended not to hear that.

Two more binders opened almost together. A woman in the second row signed, then turned straight to the yellow schedule insert as if the real decision had moved there. The athlete beside her kept reading for another five seconds and signed anyway.

Kai watched Lena. She had opened her binder now, but only far enough to remove the schedule insert. She read the available slots, folded the paper once, and tucked it back into the pocket without touching the line for her name.

Lena leaving the signature line untouched steadied him more than it should have. The blank box under his own name had started to feel small, like stubbornness dressed up as principle. Seeing hers stay blank made it look like a choice again.

Adewale kept his binder shut under one palm like he was pinning something down. Aissatou was reading the liability waiver now, lips moving once over fatal. The Brazilian gymnast had the paragraph about baseline markers under one chalked fingertip and still had not turned the page.

By the time Jin-seo checked her watch, only six binders were still closed or unsigned.

"I'll be here through Thursday," she said. Her thumb touched the face of the watch, the same small check from the start. "If you need time, take it. If you want to ask questions privately, do that. No one is being marched to the calibration bay this morning."

She gathered her portfolio. The two men from Meridian rose and began collecting the signed binders with practiced efficiency. One wore thin surgical gloves. The latex flashed pale each time he bent to pick one up.

Before the stack was halfway built, a woman from Meridian legal laid out a second clipboard on the front table. The heading at the top read CALIBRATION BAY APPOINTMENTS in blue block letters. The first slots started at thirteen hundred.

The signers did not need to be told what it was. Chairs scraped. The front-row swimmer stood first. So did the Swedish cyclist, binder already gone from his hands. The gloved technician checked names against the signature stack and handed out small white cards with bay numbers and times, each one stamped Meridian red in one corner.

"How bad's the first night?" the cyclist asked.

"Headache. Motion lag. Sometimes your timing goes strange till the handshake settles," the technician said. "That's why the early slots stay under observation. If the system comes back clean, light work tomorrow."

That moved the line faster, not slower. Better to find out on this mountain that the lane lines doubled or your legs came back half a beat late than in Doha, in front of cameras and a clock that didn't care.

Tomas stayed seated long enough that Kai thought he might refuse the next step after all. Then he got up, gave his binder to the gloved man, and stood in the short line with the others, shoulders squared the way athletes stood for accreditation photos. When he came back past Kai's row he had a white appointment card between two fingers and would not quite meet Kai's eyes.

Kai's own binder lay open on his lap. At some point he had opened it without noticing. The signature line was blank. Behind it waited the addenda Tomas had touched: sponsorship compliance, support obligations, data-use schedules, calibration consent. His name sat there in print, neat and harmless-looking, with a box on the next page for preferred installation time.

When the gloved hand reached for another signed binder, his left wrist answered with a deep throb inside the brace. He closed his own and pressed his good hand flat over the cover until the pulse eased.


# Chapter 9

Yusuf talked to himself when he worked. It wasn't the self-conscious muttering of someone performing eccentricity, just a steady half-voiced account of whatever his hands were doing, so constant it barely registered as speech anymore. It came in fragments of logic and technical shorthand, with the occasional "right" serving as both punctuation and encouragement. The calibration bay at two in the morning was the only place it didn't sound odd. Diagnostic terminals threw blue-white light across his face, the desk, and the paper cup he had filled three hours earlier from the corridor machine, the espresso inside gone cold enough to leave a bitter film at the back of his tongue.

Three displays held three versions of the same problem. On the left, captured handshake traffic from infected calibration sessions, athlete names stripped out but not the body data. In the center, Meridian's patch package from the afternoon meeting sat sealed and proprietary, asking for permanent read-write privileges across every enhancement system in the building. On the right, Yusuf's own map of the handshake sequence crawled with notes built from reverse-engineered logs, public documentation, and six years of remembering which parts of Meridian firmware always behaved differently from the brochure.

He flexed his fingers once, set them back on the keyboard, and told the empty room what he was about to try.

"Right. Upstream first. Small cut. No heroics."

The first interception point sat just above sensory routing, early enough to catch the payload before it could write itself into the live enhancement stack. On replay the foreign block hit his hook, stalled there, and vanished into the sandbox partition he had built an hour earlier. Good. A line of numbers populated underneath.

`sensor queue delay: 84ms`

"Eighty-four? No. No, that's vile."

He took out two logging calls, moved a checksum audit to a lower priority thread, and ran the same replay again.

`sensor queue delay: 41ms`

It was better, but still ugly. Forty-one milliseconds was not abstract at sprint speed. It was a step landing a fraction behind the body's confidence. It was a hurdler seeing the barrier where it had been rather than where it was. It was a swimmer feeling the wall a breath too late and twisting through the turn with one shoulder not quite under her. Costly, certainly. It was not automatically catastrophic.

He pushed the sim harder. A preserved sprint session ran through the patched handshake. The digital skeleton on screen drove cleanly through acceleration, then showed a slight smear at maximal cadence, the knees coming up on time but the upper body adjusting a beat late, as if the head and shoulders had needed persuading. There was no collapse and no blind panic, just a body no longer quite first with itself.

Another replay pulled up a turn sequence from the aquatic calibration bank, stripped of everything except force curves and joint timing. The virtual swimmer reached for the wall and caught it half a hand-width short, fingers skidding before the palm found tile. It was enough to bruise ribs on a bad day, not enough to drown anyone by itself.

"All right. You'd hate it, but you'd stay in one piece."

The second interception point lived deeper, down near the autonomous motor governor where the virus seemed to preserve its own path. There the numbers looked cleaner immediately.

`sensor queue delay: 6ms`

"That's more like it."

For about half a second it was. Then the stumble-recovery routine flashed yellow.

`assistive catch protocol: unavailable`

Silence held in the bay long enough for him to hear the fluorescent tube nearest the windows buzz on its bad ballast.

A balance-failure sim loaded under his fingers: full sprint, wet-surface coefficient, lateral displacement introduced at the left ankle. In the replay the runner clipped into instability and pitched forward. No recovery pulse came through, and no throttle cut the synthetic force. The ankle over-rotated, the knee kept driving, and the body went down with the smooth inevitability of a machine following instructions that no longer deserved obedience. In the frame after impact the lower leg sat at the wrong angle. In the frame after that the torso kept trying to move.

Yusuf took his hands off the keyboard.

That layer was what caught a body when its owner clipped a hurdle, misjudged a turn, hit a wet tile, lost half a step. It was the thing that kept a stumble from becoming shredded ligaments and bone through skin. He could tell an athlete the first patch would slow them. He could not tell them the second one would probably let them keep their times while removing the piece that stopped them breaking themselves open.

For a moment Farah crossed his mind anyway, not her face but her hands on the video call last month, trying to fasten a cuff and missing the button twice because the fine control went on her without warning now. If Meridian owned every stream outright, the early deterioration patterns in people like her would disappear into licensing language and quarterly strategy decks. He pulled his attention back before that thought could turn into permission.

"First one, then. Ugly but honest."

He went upstream again, widened the quarantine lane, and blocked reintegration. The payload went into the sandbox and stayed there when he replayed variant twenty-nine. Variant thirty passed. Variant thirty-one passed. Variant thirty-two passed after he tightened a validation check that had been too generous by one packet header. The next run broke something else.

`coach link: connection refused`

He stared at it, then swore softly and opened a fresh branch.

The coaching feed used the same bidirectional lane the virus used. Of course it did. Meridian had built the whole ecosystem to prefer openness when the openness enriched them. Margot's interface was only the most obvious example. Live biomechanical correction, external pacing, remote override permissions in narrowly defined circumstances, post-session telemetry sync, all of it depended on a handshake that treated the body as a system endpoint first and a person second.

"Fine. Mirror only."

He built a one-way relay that preserved outbound metrics and stripped inbound write privileges. The relay held for a moment, enough to make him lean closer. Then the payload rode back in on the callback the coaching stack still expected for acknowledgement.

`coach mirror: compromised`
`ingress source: remote callback`

"Dirty little bastard."

He killed the relay, opened a read-only metrics export, and tried again. This time no malicious block came through. This time no coaching data came through either, not in any way a live session could use.

`coach link: unavailable`
`telemetry export: delayed batch only`

Which was the same answer with better manners. Athletes could take his patch and keep ownership of their bodies, or they could keep the real-time tools half the building had started treating like an extra limb. At the moment he did not know how to offer both.

The matrix on the right-hand screen filled slowly as he worked.

`mapped variants: 34/47`
`stable quarantine: 28`
`stable quarantine + intact catch protocols: 19`
`stable quarantine + coach continuity: 0`

Nineteen was not nothing. Nineteen was also not the kind of number a sane man put near a live nervous system.

He forced himself through three more runs anyway. Variant thirty-three threw a checksum mismatch that traced back to a routing exception he had introduced an hour earlier and forgotten. Variant thirty-four passed on rerun. Variant thirty-five made the latency wobble between thirty-eight and forty-three milliseconds depending on how aggressively the sim loaded proprioceptive feedback. It was good enough to stay on screen and bad enough to leave his jaw sore from clenching.

Across the room the bay clock changed to 02:41.

He saved the current patch branch to the facility environment under the label Maintenance tooling update, buried inside the calibration utilities under a name no one would notice unless they were already suspicious. The second save went to the encrypted archive on the personal device in his quarters. It took a fraction longer there because the archive rekeyed every time he touched it. He watched the progress bar finish and hated the part of himself that always relaxed a little when the private copy existed.

"Enough for tonight," he said.

It was not enough. The center screen still held Meridian's sealed package like a locked door. On instinct he opened the virus code instead.

---

As always now, he started with the damage pattern.

A copy of the payload went into the sandbox. A preserved handshake replay followed it. On the output pane, vision dropped to null first. Hearing followed. Proprioception jittered, fragmented, then went dark in irregular blocks, not all at once but fast enough that a body would feel itself vanish in pieces. The motor governor stayed green throughout.

At the bottom of the screen a skeletal runner kept moving, knees lifting, arms driving, head steady, while every sensory pane above it stayed blank.

Yusuf felt his shoulders lock. Kai had hit a wall with his legs still driving.

"Designed," he said, and hated hearing the word out loud.

He tagged the governor output and reran the trace. Vision null. Hearing null. Proprioception gone in blocks. Motor path untouched. Assistive routines untouched. The bite pattern never wandered.

He ran the payload through a second replay, a lower-speed balance drill rather than a sprint. The order stayed the same. The bite pattern stayed the same. Sensory channels were stripped out while motor channels were spared. Then he ran a third, because repetition was the only honest way to trust a result, and it came back the same again.

Compiled output replaced the functional view, with the virus on the left and standard Meridian calibration firmware on the right. They shared the same processor target, the same file-size class, and the same hex density across the middle blocks. He ran an allocation scan and watched the histograms populate at the same block sizes: 64, 128, 256.

The same block sizes repeated on both sides of the screen, along with the same alignment boundaries and the same padding after control structures. He ran the scan again because the first result landed in him like a dirty joke, then pulled up an archived Meridian patch from last quarter and ran that one through the same analysis just to make sure fatigue had not made him credulous.

It was the same family of patterns. Not identical, not enough for courtroom certainty, but close in the way related tools were close. It carried a specific compiler habit and a preference for neat block alignment even where efficiency did not require it. Someone had taught the code how to stand.

His coffee had gone cold enough to grow a skin on top. He reached for it anyway, took one swallow, and put it back down untouched after that. The bitterness turned his mouth dry.

He went to exception tables next. The virus carried the same nested error hierarchy Meridian liked, too tidy for most independent shops and too fussy for the black-market clone kits he had spent years cleaning up after. It used the standard overflow routine, the same fallback that kept the system live long enough to write its own autopsy, and the same mildly sanctimonious logging stamp that Meridian engineers claimed was for patient safety and everyone else knew was for liability.

"Bollocks."

Stopping there would have been cowardice. Similarity was not proof. Similarity was how smart people embarrassed themselves in public. He opened a public toolchain sample, then an older non-Meridian firmware package from a facility partner, and checked their allocator behavior against the virus. Both diverged quickly, with different padding, different exception depth, and different memory-cleanup habits. That still was not proof, but it tightened the circle.

He opened the string table. The first entry was a generic library path, useless. The second was a standard debug call, just as useless. A third path surfaced halfway down.

`/mbio/dev/tools/nlink/`

His stool struck the cabinet behind him before he realized he had pushed back from the desk.

mbio meant Meridian BioSystems.

nlink meant neural link.

The fluorescent tube near the window buzzed again. Yusuf looked away from the path, then back, as if the extra second might turn it into something less obvious. It did not. The string sat there with the flat confidence of something never meant for outside eyes.

He opened a notes pane and typed what he could defend if anyone ever demanded the chain of reasoning.

String table contains a Meridian dev path.
Exception hierarchy is consistent with current calibration firmware.
Allocator profile is consistent with recent Meridian builds.
Public and partner toolchains do not match the same pattern family.

The first line was easy. The last was easy enough. `Meridian` in the second and third lines gave him pause. He deleted it once, replaced it with `vendor`, stared at the evasion for a breath, then put `Meridian` back. The path was still there. The exception handling still matched. The memory pattern still matched. A stolen toolchain could leave those marks. So could a planted breadcrumb. So could an internal build nobody had scrubbed properly. Years of engineering had taught him the discipline of stopping just before the claim you wanted and writing down only the claim you could keep.

To test his own caution, he pulled a March Meridian hotfix from the facility archive, one of the builds that had briefly broken a calibration scheduler in two training bays before the vendor rolled it back and called the fault site-specific. It carried the same logging stamp and the same pointer-advance quirk in the overflow fallback, eight bytes where any sensible team would have used four. The virus had that quirk too. He swore again, more softly this time.

There was no accusation yet and no grand theory. What he had was structural resemblance strong enough to be frightening and weak enough to be deniable.

He opened a fresh note beneath the byte-level comparison and typed the order instead of trying to argue with it.

`broader data-rights push`
`virus outbreak`
`sealed patch demanding permanent access to live streams`

He read the three lines twice. Still filthy. The toolchain habits could be stolen. They could be planted. The sequence did not get cleaner.

The private archive received the byte-level analysis. The facility environment did not. Whatever sweep Meridian's audit tools ran across the network next week, he did not intend to hand them his own suspicions wrapped and labelled. When the save finished, the virus windows disappeared and the patch came back full-screen.

For a long minute he just looked at it. Thirty-four variants were mapped. Nineteen preserved stumble recovery. None preserved live coaching. It was enough to tempt him and not enough to forgive him if he was wrong.

Variant thirty-five was still waiting, the simulated body frozen mid-stride on the right-hand monitor. He keyed the next run in by hand because his fingers needed work more than his mind needed another thought.

The bay door opened at quarter past three.

Yusuf did not turn at first. Technicians crossed the calibration wing at every hour, and the room had enough entry points that a door opening usually meant nothing. These footsteps stopped instead of passing. Someone stood there without speaking.

"Dr. Qadir."

He turned.

Petra Engel stood in the doorway with her clinic coat still on over a grey jumper. Her hair, usually pinned into submission, had come loose on one side. She carried a paper cup from the corridor machine and looked as though she had been awake on principle rather than by choice.

"Dr. Engel. Bit late."

His own voice gave him away. He heard the Birmingham flatten out as soon as he spoke, the professional register locking over the sentence and squeezing the Birmingham out of it.

"I've been reviewing facility access logs for the past week," Petra said. "Routine audit, given the circumstances."

"Sure."

"Your badge accessed this bay at eleven forty-seven tonight. It is now three fifteen. You have no calibration sessions scheduled before eight."

"Sometimes I work late. You know how it is."

"I do."

She crossed to the secondary station and looked at the displays without asking permission. He did not minimize anything. The virus windows were gone. The patch remained, dense enough to be obviously real and incomplete enough to be dangerous.

"This is not a standard calibration tool," she said.

"No."

"What is it?"

"An alternative approach to the virus. I'm seeing whether I can block propagation without handing Meridian full access to every live system in the building."

Petra set her cup down and stayed standing.

"On your own," she said. "Without authorization. On facility hardware."

"Yes."

"Show me the failure modes."

That made him look up properly.

"Not the summary," she said. "The failures."

Something in him loosened a notch at the specificity. He rotated the center monitor toward her and pulled up the comparison table. Upstream intercept on the left. Deep intercept on the right. Latency traces under both. A cluster of test replays stacked beneath them.

"This branch catches the payload before it can write into the live enhancement stack," he said. "Forty to forty-three milliseconds of sensory delay depending on the load. Stumble recovery stays intact. Coach link dies."

"And the other."

"Six milliseconds. Almost clean. But it drops the assistive catch protocol."

"Run it."

He ran the deeper branch. The balance-failure sim loaded, the runner clipped off-center, and the body folded with that terrible smoothness again, no corrective pulse, no reduction in synthetic force, just joint after joint obeying the last instruction it had been given until the screen model landed in a collapsed knot that would have been torn tissue and fractured bone in real life.

Petra did not flinch. She just leaned closer.

"Again," she said.

This time the upstream branch. Same destabilization. Same displaced ankle. The body lurched, recovered badly, and kept moving with a visible hesitation, the torso correcting after the legs rather than with them.

"Forty-one milliseconds," she said, reading the trace. "That is a long time in a lane."

"Yes."

"But this one keeps the protective routine."

"Yes."

"And the coaching interface."

"Gone."

He brought up the relay attempt and the failed mirror with the callback compromise. Petra read the lines in silence.

`coach mirror: compromised`
`ingress source: remote callback`

"So any live coaching channel is also a route back in," she said.

"Through the present handshake, yes."

"Can you separate them?"

"Not tonight."

"Can Meridian?"

"Meridian can do whatever Meridian's sealed package actually does. They've not shown us."

That earned him a brief look, tired and sharp at once.

"You have not run this on a person."

"No. Replays only."

"Good."

Only then did she sit on the stool beside the station. Her movements stayed measured even now, but tiredness had started to show through them as something physical rather than temperamental, a slight care in the wrists, the caution of someone whose body had been awake too long and knew it.

"Is this the only unauthorized work you have been doing in this bay?" she asked.

The question landed with enough precision that lying to it would have been insulting. A flinch crossed his face before he could stop it. Petra caught it.

"I've also been running additional analysis on the virus code," he said. The vowels tidied themselves further. "The standard diagnostic approach is not getting all of it."

"The access logs show you have been here after hours on eleven of the last fourteen nights," she said. "Including nights before the virus was formally identified."

He could explain that. The anomaly tracking, the weeks of tiny mismatches, the small failures that had started before anyone called it infection. The explanation was true. It also opened the wrong door and kept opening it.

"I was concerned about network anomalies before the formal diagnosis," he said. "The virus confirmed the pattern."

"And the transfers to an unregistered device?"

His hands went still on the desk.

"System logs show small encrypted exports from this station," Petra said. "Regular. Off-book. IT security is busy with the outbreak, the delegation, and everything else that is currently on fire. They have not followed the trail yet. They will."

Yusuf pushed his glasses up his nose. The gesture bought him half a second and nothing useful arrived in it.

"I've been backing up biological calibration data," he said. "Athlete biometrics. More than my job covers."

"Why?"

Redundancy. Network stability. Data integrity. All the stupid little lies presented themselves in a queue. Petra had not come down here at three in the morning for any of them.

"Some of it's — look, the calibration data has neurological applications that aren't being pursued. Degenerative disease work." He heard himself stalling in the technical language and pushed through it. "My sister's got Voss-Kessler. It's progressive and Meridian doesn't give a shit because there's no product in it. The data's already sitting there. Once that contract locks everything down, nobody outside them gets near it."

Petra said nothing for long enough that the server fans took over the room.

"How much data?" she asked.

"Enough to matter. Not enough."

"Identified?"

"In the archive, yes."

Her mouth tightened, not in surprise. In calculation.

"There was a decathlete at the Stuttgart facility," she said. She picked up the cup, found it empty, and set it back down again. "Marius Koch. He was twenty-four."

Yusuf kept quiet.

"His body started rejecting the synthetic grafts. Immune cascade, inflammatory markers climbing, organ involvement beginning before anyone stopped calling it calibration variance." She corrected herself: "Before I stopped calling it that. I let the classification stand for eleven days before I escalated. I should have been faster."

She was looking at the code on his center screen rather than at him.

"I wrote three reports. Recommended immediate removal. The facility director agreed with me — forwarded everything to Meridian medical oversight with his own recommendation attached." She paused. "Meridian reclassified the rejection as within acceptable parameters. Told us to keep him training while they monitored."

"What happened?"

"He died. Eight months later." Petra turned the empty cup once between her palms. "Organ failure. His body had been in active rejection the entire time they kept him training."

Something old and exhausted moved across Yusuf's chest. Not sympathy exactly. Recognition. The institutional kind.

"I filed a complaint," Petra said. "The investigation found — protocols had been followed. Technically they had been. Right forms, right intervals, right language." She stopped. "I kept waiting for someone to ask whether the protocols were even designed to catch what happened to him. Nobody did."

He looked at his hands because there was nowhere useful to put his eyes.

"I'm not telling you this so you'll feel sorry for me," she said.

"I know."

"Good."

She leaned toward the screen again and tapped the latency table with one finger.

"This matters," she said. "Forty-one milliseconds. Protective routines intact. Coaching link dead. The other branch is faster and it drops the safety catch." She was quiet a moment. "Nobody else has shown me anything like this. It's been Meridian's sealed package or — people in meetings talking about liability."

"And this," he said.

"And this. Which at least tells me what the tradeoff is."

"I should report you," she said. "If security audits this bay and finds out I stood here — saw unauthorized exports, unsanctioned firmware work during an outbreak — and said nothing, then I'm part of it. Best case I lose this post. Worst case it's my license."

Now he looked at her.

"I'm not going to report you tonight." She said it without looking away. "Not because I — I don't know what your motives are. I'm not pretending the archive isn't a problem." She let out a breath. "Meridian wants permanent data access across every system in this building. And you're the only person who's shown me what the alternative costs. Those are the two things I'm weighing."

The bay hummed around them. Somewhere in the corridor a trolley wheel rattled over a seam in the floor and passed on.

"There are conditions," she said.

"All right."

"You don't put this on an athlete. Not one. If you get to the point where you think it's clinically ready, you bring me the full latency table, the fall-recovery data, every failure case. I review it before it goes near a live system."

"Understood."

"If you go around me, I report everything."

"Understood."

"Second. I don't want details of your archive unless they become clinically relevant. Names especially. You're in enough trouble already and I don't intend to make it worse."

"That's generous."

"It's containment," Petra said.

That almost made him laugh. It came out instead as breath through his nose, too tired to qualify as anything.

"The transfer logs will be audited," she said. "Two weeks, two months — I can't say when. But they will be. You should think about what you're keeping, what you're moving, and what holds up when someone actually looks."

"You make that sound cheerful."

"I'm not trying to."

Fair enough.

Petra stood. For a second he thought the conversation was over. Then she paused, eyes settling not on him but on the notes pane still open on the left-hand screen, the one where his virus analysis sat in plain text because he had been too tired to close it.

String table contains Meridian dev path.
Exception hierarchy consistent with current calibration firmware.
Allocator profile consistent with recent Meridian builds.
Public and partner toolchains do not match the same pattern family.

"Is that what I think it is?" she asked.

Every sensible response arrived late. He chose the least stupid one.

"It's what I can justify so far."

"Not what you believe."

"Belief's cheap."

Petra read the lines again. "And your justification is?"

"Structural signatures. Toolchain behavior. A dev path that should never have been in a stripped build. Enough to be ugly. Not enough to survive accusation."

"Do you think the virus is deliberate?"

He looked back at the screens. The skeletal runner remained frozen mid-stride from the last sim, one foot off the ground, waiting for an instruction that had not yet come.

"I think it behaves like something that was built by people who understood exactly what they were leaving intact."

Petra absorbed that without visible reaction. When she spoke again, the hedging in her voice had gone.

"Then keep that analysis off the network. All of it. You don't have evidence — you have resemblance. If Meridian's legal team sees those notes before you've got something reproducible, something independent, they will call it defamation." She paused. "And if you're right and they find out you know, you've told them what to scrub."

"That's the current plan."

"Good. At least one of us is planning."

She picked up the paper cup and moved toward the door. Her hand found the plate and stayed there a moment.

"If this shows clinical promise," she said, still facing the corridor, "I want to see the data before it goes near anyone. I'm asking as a physician. Not as the medical director."

"All right."

"And Yusuf."

"Yeah?"

Only then did she look back.

"Don't assume that being useful to me makes you safe."

"I won't," he said.

Petra gave a short nod.

"Good night, Dr. Qadir."

"Night."

The door shut. Machine hum filled the space she had occupied, then the smaller sounds returned beneath it: fans, relays, the low electric hiss of the displays. Yusuf sat still long enough to feel how hard his heartbeat had been working under the whole conversation.

On the center screen the patch matrix waited where he had left it.

`mapped variants: 34/47`
`stable quarantine + intact catch protocols: 19`
`coach continuity: 0`

In a few hours the building would wake and start asking bodies to trust the same handshake again.

No miraculous new answer had appeared while Petra was in the room. Variant thirty-five still sat at forty-one milliseconds, catch protocols intact, coach channel dead.

He reopened variant thirty-five and then, on a sour impulse, duplicated the branch into thirty-six with a note to strip the callback acknowledgement one layer earlier. Probably pointless. Possibly not. The kind of thing a tired engineer did because the hands could keep moving after judgment had started to fray.

Outside the windows the mountains had begun to separate from the dark, ridges emerging one by one as if someone were rubbing charcoal off the horizon with a thumb. The room looked worse in that light. Cables. Coffee rings. His own reflection floating over the monitor, glasses catching terminal glare, shoulders narrower than he remembered from a few years ago.

He keyed in the new run.

The replay hit the hook, hesitated, and spilled a fresh column of latency numbers down the pane.

`latency: 39ms`
`catch protocol: intact`
`coach callback: rejected`

Yusuf leaned closer and swore under his breath.


# Chapter 10

"What lap is this?"

Her voice came up through the audio pickup, flat and tinny in the tile room. One hand was on the gutter and the other on the lane rope. Water slid off her cap and down her face. Her mouth stayed open between breaths. She stared at the opposite wall like the room might sort itself out if she waited long enough.

Margot had already disconnected. The feed was gone. Warm water, loaded muscle, Lena's heart hammering up under her sternum. Then dry glass, dry air, her own pulse shrinking back to size. The skin under the interface stood up red and ridged when she tore the strap loose. Below the observation deck Lena looked like somebody dropped into the wrong part of the morning.

Before eight, the deck console ran local. Anything serious enough to finish its countdown would queue for central routing and fire the moment the campus network came online. The console in front of Margot still held two red alerts in its history bar, each one stamped with her own wrist-unit override.

By the time Lena hauled herself out and sat on the edge with both feet in the water, Margot had the session log open. The paired device had mirrored the whole set by default: raw feed depth, heart-rate stream, pace commands, alert dismissals, local timestamps. She changed the auto-label on both alerts from sustained HR anomaly to coach-authorized build. She reclassified the set from aerobic to mixed-intensity. She added a note field: post-set disorientation after middle-build effort, resolved poolside. Then she switched the export to summary sync only.

Summary sync kept the official record thin. Splits. Heart-rate curve. Final set classification. Coach note. It stripped out the raw feed, the countdown history, and the command log that showed exactly when she had killed medical escalation. Anyone checking later would see a hard middle split and a messy recovery. Anyone wanting the ugly version would need her wrist unit and the local cache still sitting on it.

"Coach."

Margot looked down.

Lena had one hand clamped to the port at the back of her neck. Her face had not come all the way back yet.

"What happened?"

Margot kept her eyes on the console. "You built the middle too hard and came up spun. Give it a minute."

Lena frowned. "I don't remember the end."

"You finished the set." Margot shut the log. "Shower first. We'll look at it after breakfast."

This time Lena just stared at her. Margot picked up her bag before the stare had time to become a second question.

The corridor to residential took four minutes. Linoleum, fluorescent panels, antiseptic in the vents, stale coffee from the cafeteria wing. A sprinter she did not know was stretching against the wall, calves marked with faint graft seams. They nodded at each other and kept moving.

In her room, Margot sat on the bed and took the interface back out. It was brushed steel, the connection pads still warm from her skin. Four years of use had darkened the edges with oils and pulse sweat. The memory light held solid amber. Unsynced local cache.

She woke the screen.

Session buffer: 07:12.

Raw sensory packets retained.

Local action history retained.

Export profile: summary sync pending dock.

The purge option sat in the lower corner. Two taps, thumbprint confirm, and the raw feed would be gone before anyone in medical knew there had ever been more to ask for. The summary would still post when she docked the unit. Petra would have the clean story and nothing else.

Margot opened the action history instead.

Standard aerobic set loaded. Pace target 1:04. First hundred 1:03.8. Second 1:04.1. Six fifty-two and thirteen seconds: pace target lowered to 1:00. Six fifty-two and forty-one: pace target lowered to 0:58. Six fifty-three and twenty-two: Class III page armed. Six fifty-three and twenty-four: page dismissed by coach unit. Six fifty-four and nine: second red alert issued. Six fifty-four and eleven: alert reclassified.

The device kept the order plain and ugly and exact. She did not need the replay to remember why each line was there.

At the start of the third hundred, the little choices had gone out of Lena. Usually there was a fraction more pressure on the left palm, a breath held a beat too long, a turn adjusting for what the last stroke had done. Then that texture vanished. The hand went in. The body pulled. It kicked. It breathed. Everything landed on time and all of it was dead flat.

Earlier sessions had carried a blink of this. Half a second where the stroke lost its texture and came back. Margot had logged drift and kept going.

This wasn't drift.

She opened the buffer.

The replay came back with no mercy. Through the feed Lena's body felt wrong in three places at once: lungs pulling air with no panic in them, legs turning over without fatigue arguing back, arms catching exactly the same pressure every cycle as if pain had been cut out of the lane. From the deck the stroke had still looked clean. Inside the feed there had been nobody there making the small corrections that made it Lena.

Margot watched herself lower the target to one minute and felt the answer come back before any trace of assent or resistance. She watched herself do it again to fifty-eight. Lena hit the three-hundred wall hard enough that the impact rang through Margot's teeth and fired off it with both legs at once. Heart rate 178. 182. Yellow warning. Cleared. 188. 190. Red box. Sixty-second countdown. Margot killed it at fifty-eight because the feed was still giving and she wanted one more wall.

The missing stretch ran hot and blank. The whole body was working. None of the person was in it. That clean absence hit Margot in the jaw and shoulders like a drug finding an old vein. She cut the replay after three strokes and still had it in her mouth.

Her hands shook hard enough that she nearly dropped the unit. She sat there breathing through her mouth until the room steadied. The purge icon stayed in the corner, patient, unchanged.

The order should have been Petra first, then Yusuf. A Class III-equivalent event meant raw data and a grounded athlete. Petra would not stop at the summary if Lena reported missing time. She would ask for the unit. She would pull the cache herself or hand it to Yusuf and tell him to do it. Then she would get everything: the pace jumps, both canceled countdowns, the relabeled alerts, the summary-only export, the lie at poolside, the raw segment where Lena's body kept swimming after Lena was gone.

Margot set the unit on her thigh and looked at it the way other people looked at a bottle they had already opened.

She could tell herself she was waiting for more information. She could tell herself she did not want Petra yanking Lena out of the water on the basis of one ugly session during a week when everyone at Kirchberg was already half-convinced the virus lived in every ache and skipped beat. She could tell herself imaging first thing tomorrow would change nothing except panic.

The cleaner sentence sat under all of it.

If Petra saw the buffer, Petra would take the unit.

Margot put the interface in the nightstand drawer and shut it.

She wanted Lena safe. She wanted the feed more. That was the order it sat in her body.

On the wall display, her heart rate had come down to 84. Her respiration was normal. Enhancement status read none. That line stayed blank. It always would. She had gone to Brisbane in 2028 in an unenhanced body and finished fifth in the 200 fly. Now she sat on a narrow bed in Kirchberg with her own lungs working at an ordinary human rate and a sour ache climbing the back of her throat because ordinary was not enough.

The room stayed bright. She lay back anyway. Ten minutes after disconnect, her legs still gave a small useless tremor now and then, as if they expected another wall. The empty part had started already. It always started fast.

She lasted six minutes before opening the drawer again.

This time she did not reopen the replay. She went back to the action history and read the pace changes until the numbers stopped looking like numbers and turned back into touch: the clean blank stroke, the hard wall, the countdown she had killed with her thumb. She could not tell whether that made her feel better or worse.

There were people she could call. Petra, who would go cold and formal and start protecting Lena in ways that would also ruin the training block. Yusuf, who would ask for the device before he asked how she was. Lena, who would hear the lie in Margot's voice before the first sentence was over. Margot did not call anyone.

By eight the campus network would wake fully and the pool schedule would fill with sanctioned movement. Coaches would have clipboards. Athletes would have breakfast in them. Somebody would make a joke about altitude and somebody else would nod because the building ran on people agreeing to the explanation that cost the least.

Margot slid the unit back into the drawer and forced herself to leave it there.

When she finally shut her eyes, the thing she felt most clearly was not guilt. It was hunger with nowhere to go.

---

Lena spent the afternoon trying to find the missing laps.

She started with her body because the body had done them whether she remembered them or not.

After the shower she sat on the closed toilet lid in her room with a towel around her shoulders and checked herself the way Petra checked injured athletes. Skin first. No split knuckles. No blood in the whites of the eyes. Then muscle. Her lats were blown out the way they got after a race-pace back half, not after an aerobic four hundred. The tendons at the front of both shoulders ached from hard wall contact. There was a raw spot on the inside of her lower lip where she had bitten through the turn and not felt it until later. None of that matched what she remembered doing.

At her desk, she pulled up the session telemetry Margot had filed that morning. The screen gave stroke rate, splits, heart rate, and neural link activity. The graphs ran clean from start to finish.
