The second half showed a moderate rise. The summary field carried no desync marker. The interruption flag read none.

There was a coach note.

Post-set disorientation after middle-build effort, resolved poolside.

Lena read it twice. It matched what Margot had told her at the pool. Hard middle. Disoriented at the wall. Nothing else. She remembered the lane rope under her hand, the chlorine taste too sharp in the back of her throat, her lungs trying to climb out through her mouth, and her own voice asking what lap it was because the question had arrived before the rest of her did.

The record said she had swum four hundred meters.

Lena remembered two hundred and fifty.

She remembered the warm-up, the first hundred, the second turn, the beginning of the third, and the flip at 250.

Then the lane rope was under her hand. Her chest tore for air. The pool lights were too bright. The set was over.

The telemetry said her body had done the rest. Her arms had pulled. Her legs had kicked. The walls had registered contact. Her heart rate had gone into the 190s. The split graph showed a sudden hard middle build she had never been assigned. All of it was real. None of it had been experienced.

Smaller slips had happened before. Twenty minutes gone in her room while a page sat open in front of her. A turn pattern last season's firmware chose for her. Margot had called those within acceptable parameters, and Lena had accepted it because Margot had the data and the authority, and because the program had trained her early to trust whatever could be graphed.

This morning was not that.

She touched the port at the base of her skull. The ceramic edge sat smooth under her thumb, skin-warm, built into her like a joint or a tooth. She had had it since she was four. She pressed harder, as if pressure might give her a clearer answer than the screen did.

The Kirchberg desync protocol sat three menus down. She pulled it up and read the relevant lines again. Class II: intermittent divergence under five seconds. Report within twenty-four hours. Class III: sustained divergence, loss of voluntary motor control, or loss of conscious awareness during link-directed activity. Report immediately. Ground the athlete.

Three minutes was not Class II.

From the protocol page she could open the athlete report form. The button sat there the way an emergency exit sits there, obvious and ugly and hard to use without changing the rest of the day. Lena looked at it, then closed the menu and opened the telemetry again.

The summary view gave her numbers and a story about the numbers. Heart-rate spike. Coach-authorized build. Post-set disorientation resolved. Missing time had nowhere to attach itself on the page.

She checked the splits against the training plan Margot had loaded before dawn. The first two hundreds matched. The back half did not. The set was supposed to sit at 1:04 pace. The graph showed the third hundred tightening, then the last hundred breaking under the minute mark before the recovery at the wall. Margot had changed the target in the water. The entry offered no reason.

Lena stared at that for a long time.

The clean explanation would have been that Margot had seen something and tested it. Coaches did that. They changed sets in real time all the time.

The less clean explanation was that Margot had felt something wrong and pushed harder anyway.

Lena saved the session locally, shut the wall screen, turned it back on, and opened it again as if the numbers might have altered in the dark.

They had not.

At twelve thirty she went to the cafeteria and stood with a tray she could not make herself fill. The room smelled of coffee, eggs, and bleach from the morning mop. Two cyclists were arguing softly in French over a lactate chart. Someone dropped cutlery at the far end and nobody looked up. Ordinary noise kept happening around her with the insulting confidence of things that had not lost any time.

She took yogurt, fruit, black bread. She ate half the bread and none of the fruit. Her jaw worked. Her stomach accepted the food without opinion. When she looked down at her own hands on the table, they felt temporarily assigned to her.

By one fifteen she was back in her room with the protocol open again.

The report form asked for the same things it always asked for. Time. Session type. Subjective symptoms. Whether the athlete had retained voluntary motor control. Whether conscious awareness had been continuous. Whether the coach had already filed. Whether medical had been informed.

Lena did not fill it yet. She wanted to hear Margot say the morning out loud first. Not because Margot was more likely to tell the truth if asked directly. Because Lena needed to see what Margot would do when the missing time was no longer a number on a screen but something Lena put in front of her face.

She found Margot outside the coaching suite later that afternoon. Margot had coffee in one hand and her coaching bag over her shoulder. Her hair was down. She looked tired. The quick coach scan happened as soon as Lena rounded the corner, automatic and clinical.

"Hey," Margot said.

"Can I ask about this morning?"

"Sure."

A technician pushed a cart past them and they both stepped aside. Wheels squeaking. Ventilation humming through the wall.

"I checked the session summary," Lena said. "It says mixed-intensity build."

"Because your middle pace climbed."

"I didn't choose that."

"No," Margot said. "You didn't."

Lena waited.

"You changed it," she said.

"I adjusted the set."

"After I stopped remembering."

Margot shifted the coffee cup to her other hand. "After your stroke went flat."

"Flat is not my question."

"It's part of it."

"My question is why you changed the target when something was already wrong."

Margot looked down the hall once, then back at Lena. "I saw drift. I wanted to see whether load would pull you through it."

Lena felt the words arrive in her body a beat before the meaning did.

"You felt me go wrong and pushed harder."

"I changed the pace."

"Until the last hundred went under a minute."

"Yes."

"You were on the feed."

"Yes."

"So you knew I wasn't choosing any of it."

Margot's jaw moved. "I knew you weren't swimming like yourself."

"That is not what I asked."

"It's what I've got."

Lena kept her hands at her sides because if she folded her arms now it would look too much like holding herself together.

"When I got out," she said, "I asked what happened."

"You were disoriented."

"I asked what happened."

Margot started to answer, then didn't.

"You told me I'd built the middle too hard," Lena said. "You said I came up spun."

"I told you what the summary was going to show."

Lena stared at her. That was the first thing Margot had said all afternoon that sounded close enough to truth to hurt.

"Then why isn't it Class III?" Lena asked.

"Because the summary won't carry Class III."

"The protocol says loss of conscious awareness."

"I know."

"Then why did you file against it?"

Margot rubbed her thumb once over the cardboard seam of the cup. "Because if awareness loss goes in against a clean summary, Petra grounds you before dinner. That's automatic."

"She should."

"Maybe."

Lena waited.

"Then it keeps going," Margot said. "Imaging. Link review. Admin. Selection. Meridian if they want the clearance file."

"They don't read it as my coach believed me."

"They read it as the graph stayed clean and the athlete said something else."

"Because you made the graph clean."

"I filed the official summary."

"Stop changing the words."

A door opened somewhere behind them, then shut again.

"Did I have a severe event or not?" Lena asked.

Margot took too long.

"I had missing time," Lena said. "I had no control of the last part of that set. You felt that."

"I felt the stroke go blank. I felt you stop adjusting," Margot said. "Then the heart rate went red."

"And you kept sending pace."

A pause. "Yes."

The corridor seemed narrower after that.

"So this is not about whether it happened," Lena said. "It's about whether I'm allowed to say it happened."

Margot's face gave almost nothing back. "It's about what happens to you when you say it without proof the system accepts."

Lena breathed in through her nose, slow enough that the breath shook anyway.

"And what happens to you."

Margot's eyes came back to her face. "It turns into a whole lot more than you and me."

"You want me to leave it alone."

"I want you not to walk into a hold and a review on one bad morning."

"Bad because of who?"

Margot said nothing.

"Do you want me to flag it now?" Margot asked after a beat. "I'll do it if that's what you want, but once it moves, you don't pull it back."

Lena thought about the calibration rig, Yusuf's equipment mapping her link while she lay still inside it. She thought about a physician asking her to describe three missing minutes while the official graph on the wall stayed clean. She thought about a selection note carrying the mismatch forward into a season with four months left before the Games. She thought about the way the program had trained her since childhood to understand that the body could panic, misremember, dramatize, and lie, while a recorded system error was a fact.

"I don't know," she said.

Margot nodded once.

"Then wait until you know."

"That helps you."

"It might help you too."

Lena could not tell which part of that sentence was the lie.

"I'll think about it," she said.

"OK."

Margot took another drink, adjusted the strap of her bag, and moved past her with no hurry in it.

The quarters were quiet when Lena got back. Her wall display still glowed green. Heart rate 68. Neural link status nominal.

She sat on the bed and opened the report form.

Time of incident.

Session type.

Symptom description.

Loss of voluntary motor control: yes or no.

Loss of conscious awareness: yes or no.

Supporting telemetry attached automatically from official session record.

She selected yes.

A yellow banner appeared across the top of the form.

Class III submission triggers immediate suspension of training clearance pending physician review.

Below it, the attached summary populated on its own.

Session classification: mixed-intensity.

Coach note: post-set disorientation after middle-build effort, resolved poolside.

Objective severe-event markers: none in attached summary.

The cursor waited in the free-text box.

Lena typed: Missing approximately 150m of conscious experience during link-directed swim set. No memory after 250m turn. Awareness resumed at wall with severe respiratory distress and disorientation.

She read it back. The sentence was accurate. It looked insane sitting beside the clean summary.

She imagined Petra opening the file and doing exactly what a physician should do. Grounding her. Imaging her. Pulling her from the water. Then the screens after that. Selection staff. Institute admin. Meridian compliance. People she would never meet reading her sentence beside the attached summary and stamping the mismatch with the words institutions used when a person said something the graph did not.

Uncorroborated.

Subjective.

Athlete-reported.

When she was ten, a developmental coach had put a hand on her shoulder and told her not to promote sensation into fact. Wait for the data. She could still feel the weight of the hand.

She moved the cursor back to the box and added a second sentence.

Coach changed pace target during missing interval.

She stopped there.

If she submitted that line, Petra would ask for the raw session. If the raw session still existed, Margot was finished. If it did not, the yellow warning and the attached summary would be all that remained, and Lena's report would sit under them by itself.

Her thumb found the edge of the port at the base of her skull.

The status bar pulsed green.

She closed the form without submitting it.

The system asked if she wanted to save as draft.

No.

The form disappeared. Nominal stayed lit on the green status bar.

She left the session unflagged.

Her thumb stayed pressed to the port until the skin went sore.
By the time the outdoor track lit up outside her window, the wall display had turned the glass into a mirror. The green bar kept pulsing.


# Chapter 11

The first time was in his room, because her room had a window that faced the floodlit outdoor track and neither of them wanted to see it.

A dim green status bar pulsed on the wall. He'd stopped checking it every hour about a week ago. That probably counted as progress.

Lena sat at the head of the bed with her knees drawn up and her phone facedown on the mattress. She'd come over after the pool. The skin beside her eyes was still marked where her goggles had pressed. They'd been talking about nothing, a movie someone had put on in the common room, whether the cafeteria coffee was getting worse or they were all just adjusting to the altitude. The conversation had run down the way conversations do when neither person wants to leave but neither has a reason to stay that they're ready to name.

At the other end of the bed, Kai sat with his left wrist on his knee, the ache doing its low-grade thing, the bone reminding him it had been broken and reassembled on a schedule the rest of his body hadn't agreed to. The scar tissue on his cheek had smoothed to a pink shine. Six weeks ago he'd been bleeding on the track with his legs still twitching. Now he was sitting on a narrow institutional bed with Lena still there and not leaving yet, and the distance between those two states had no name he could find.

"You're staring at your wrist," Lena said.

"I'm not staring."

"You're looking at it the way you look at the screen." She tilted her head toward the wall. "Checking."

Flexing his fingers, he tried to ignore how nearly normal the motion felt. "It does this thing where it almost feels normal, and then it reminds me it isn't."

"Almost normal," she said. "I know that one."

Her attention had the same quality she usually gave to things she was trying to measure: the distance to a wall, the temperature of the water. Clinical, but not cold. Interested. He liked being looked at by her, which was a simple thing to feel and he let himself feel it.

"Come here," she said. Plain, certain, simply what she wanted.

The mattress dipped as he moved up the bed. Narrow, built for a single athlete sleeping alone, it had no interest in two people trying to figure out how much space to leave between them. They ended up close enough that he could feel the heat off her shoulder. She reached over and picked up his left hand, turned it over, ran her thumb along the inside of his wrist where the line from the brace had faded to a thin white mark. Her thumb was warm. The skin there was thin. The touch landed exactly where he'd been looking for damage. A beat later the bridge came in behind it, late and officious: temperature to two decimal places, pressure spread across the wrist, her pulse ticking through her fingertip. He was used to the echo. Three years. But he noticed it now, here, because the second layer carried information about another person's body that he had not asked for and did not want.

Their first kiss was simple. She tasted like the peppermint tea from the cafeteria and her lips were slightly chapped from the pool chemicals, and those were details that belonged to him, things he noticed, not things the system measured and reported back. She kissed him back, and for a few minutes nothing was complicated.

Then it got complicated, though only a little at first. He pulled her shirt over her head and she lifted her arms to help and it caught on her left ear and she said "ow" and laughed, and he laughed, and that was human enough. Her body was a swimmer's body, shoulders wider than her hips, muscle layered across her back in the specific architecture built by years of pull. He'd known this about her in the abstract. Touching it was different. His hand moved along her ribs and he could feel the individual muscles between the bones, the way her breathing expanded her ribcage more sideways than forward. The system processed this too, feeding him biomechanical data about her thoracic expansion pattern that he did not need and could not stop receiving. He needed to know that she was breathing and that his hand was on her body and that both of those things were good. The rest was noise.

Her fingers worked his shirt buttons open with the deliberate precision she brought to most tasks, one button, then the next, which made him smile because most people either fumbled with buttons or turned them into performance and Lena just undid them like they were a problem she intended to solve correctly. When the shirt was open she put her palm flat against his sternum and held it there. He didn't know what she was checking. Maybe just that he was warm.

"Your heart rate is elevated," she said, almost clinically.

"Yeah," he said. "That's happening."

A small smile crossed her face, the kind that arrived and left without making a production of itself. He kissed the corner of it and felt her hand slide up to his shoulder, the right one, the one that had been dislocated, and when she pressed the joint he winced before he could stop himself.

"Still sore?"

"Little bit."

Even after lightening her grip, she didn't move her hand. They were on their sides now, facing each other on the narrow mattress, and the logistics of the bed meant they were either touching everywhere or falling off. They touched everywhere.

His mouth moved down the side of her neck and nudged damp hair aside. His thumb found the port there. She didn't flinch. It was as much a part of her body as the vertebrae below it. The system started feeding him measurements anyway, and he shut that out as hard as he could and kissed the skin beside it instead. A quiet sound came out of her, human and immediate and not available for diagnostic interpretation.

The bed made them work for every inch. When he tried to shift over her, his right shoulder pulled hard enough that he had to stop and breathe through it.

"Don't," Lena murmured. "Stay down."

She shoved his shirt the rest of the way off, climbed over him, and knocked one knee into the wall hard enough that both of them laughed, quietly, into each other's mouths. His elbow clipped the wall screen and it woke in a flash of green, throwing his vitals across the room in text: heart rate 118, neural bridge latency nominal, right shoulder ROM 94%. Reaching across him, Lena switched it off without looking.

Then she sat back on her heels, pushed her shorts and underwear down together, and kicked them free with more impatience than grace. One heel caught in the fitted sheet. She cursed softly in German, tugged loose, and looked down at him with a quick embarrassed smile that disappeared almost as soon as it arrived. When he reached for her, she took him in hand and guided him against her. The first pressure made him suck in a breath. Then she lowered herself, slowly, face tightening before she let the air out through her mouth. Heat closed around him in increments. The mattress creaked. His left hand ended up twisted in the sheet, the right at her waist, and for a few seconds there was nothing in the room but the wet drag of her settling over him and the relief of having nowhere to look but her.

On top of him was the only position that made sense; his shoulder couldn't take more. Her knees bracketed his hips, his hands settled at her waist, and whatever embarrassment remained got crowded out by the fact of her body moving over his.

The dual-track sensation wouldn't stay in the background. Her thighs slid against his hips, warm and slick, and before he could settle into that the bridge was already breaking the contact into force distribution, tension, the exact pull of muscle under skin. He kept trying to stay with what mattered: that she was on him, that he wanted her there, that the rest was interference. It wasn't easy. The system didn't turn off because he wanted it to.

Sex with the echo was new. Three years with the neural bridge and he had felt its doubling in every handshake, every training drill, every time someone bumped his shoulder in the cafeteria line. But here the doubling was different because what he felt mattered more. Each time she lifted, cooler air touched him for a beat and his hips wanted to follow. Each time she came down again, the bridge annotated it immediately: force, angle, pressure, her body rendered into useful numbers while she was still moving over him. When she leaned down and kissed him, wet ends of hair brushed his face and the system quietly supplied chlorine concentration, exhaled CO2, data so irrelevant it felt obscene. The clinical track kept pace under everything, technically accurate, describing the same event and not the same event at all.

His hands were on her hips and she was moving and he was moving with her and his right hand, the hand that worked fine, that had never been broken, connected to the same spinal neural bridge that governed everything below his neck, shifted. He didn't tell it to. His fingers spread slightly, redistributed across her hip, the grip adjusting from loose to firm with a smoothness that recalibrated the contact for stability. The adjustment took less than a second. It was ergonomically perfect. His hand found the exact angle that held her weight evenly, that supported her movement without restricting it, and it did this the way the system did everything when it got ahead of him: efficiently, without effort, without him.

Because she was moving, his body kept moving with her; it had gotten very good at continuing to perform acts he had stopped commanding. But the system's signal was suddenly louder than the raw one, and the hand on her hip felt less like his action and more like a motion he was watching from inside his own arm.

His hand had touched another person's body with a competence he did not generate.

It lasted a second. Maybe two. His fingers on her hip, holding her with a precision that was not his, while the rest of him tried to locate the boundary between the grip he'd intended and the grip the system had decided on. He couldn't find it. The muscle was his muscle. The nerve was his nerve. The bridge connecting them just happened to be faster and better at this than he was.

Deliberately, he loosened the grip, put his palm flat against her side, pressed it there with conscious force. That was him. He felt the decision travel from his brain down his arm and arrive in his fingers with the slight delay that meant a human being had made it.

Lena's breathing changed. She slowed. He looked up at her face and found her studying him now, taking stock, as if she'd registered the adjustment and was cataloguing it.

"I'm OK," he said. It came out flatter than he meant it to.

A small nod, then her mouth was on his again, slower this time. She took his right hand and set it higher on her ribs, waited until his fingers stayed where he put them, then slid off him and onto the mattress. "Slow," she said. Not a warning. Just instruction.

Back on her shoulders, she drew him down between her thighs with one hand at the back of his neck. He braced on his left arm and the outside of his right forearm to spare the shoulder. The angle was awkward. The bed squeaked every time his hips met hers. Her heel dragged up the back of his calf and hooked there. When he found a rhythm that didn't jar the joint or drive her into the wall, she let out a breath against his jaw and said, "There."

For half a minute, maybe a little more, the room held. Sweat cooled along his spine. Her mouth was open against his throat, her hands moving over his back in short strokes that were more practical than romantic because the mattress kept shifting under them and both of them were trying to stay on it. Nothing about it was elegant. Nothing about it was optimized. That was exactly why it felt good.

Then it wasn't.

It happened in the middle of an ordinary moment, with nothing in it that warned him. She was beneath him, they'd shifted slowly around his shoulder, and her hands were on his back and her eyes were open and she was present, fully, looking at him with an attention that was specific and warm and hers. And then she wasn't.

There was no sound, no seizure, no visible malfunction. She went still. Her hands stayed on his back but the pressure behind them went flat, like someone had stopped squeezing a held object. Her eyes didn't close. They stayed open, stayed pointed at him, but the person behind them, the sharpness and precision and faintly clinical interest and the warmth under it, went away. What was left looked like her. It had her face, her body, her hands on his back. But the presence was gone. The eyes looking at him were looking without seeing, the way a window looks at a street.

Kai stopped. He was still above her, weight on his left arm, right shoulder aching, and the woman under him was physically unchanged and completely absent.

"Lena?"

Nothing. Her eyes were open. She was breathing. Her body was warm. No one was home.

Two seconds. Three. He didn't know what to do. Pull away, touch her face, call someone. The options lined up in his mind and stayed there, equally available, equally useless. He stayed where he was. His arms held his weight. His right shoulder sent a slow ache up into his neck. The staying was his. The holding was his. The hurting was his. He held onto that because those were decisions he had made.

Then she came back. He saw it happen. The eyes first, the quality of light behind them changing. Then her hands, which pressed into his back with renewed intention. Then her face, which registered where she was and what was happening and arranged itself into an expression that was not quite confusion and not quite fear, more like someone finding herself in a place she didn't remember entering.

"Hey," he said. His voice came out rough. "Hey. You went somewhere."

Blinking, she looked at him, then at her own hands on his back, as though confirming they were hers.

"How long?" she said.

"A few seconds."

After a beat, he rolled off her, carefully, and lay beside her on the narrow bed. They weren't touching except at the shoulder, the mattress too small for distance but too narrow for pretense.

"A few seconds," she repeated, as if fixing the number in place.

"Yeah."

Both of them stared at the ceiling.

A slow breath in, then out. Then another. The rhythm of someone re-establishing inventory, checking that the body answered to the right name. He recognized it because he'd done the same thing in the medical bay after the blackout, the same deliberate breathing, the same careful audit of what was still there.

"I'm here," she said. To herself as much as to him.

"I know."

The hallway light under the door made a thin gold bar on the floor. Outside, wind moved against the window with the flat, constant pressure of altitude.

For a while neither moved. Then Lena sat up and reached for her shirt at the foot of the bed. The fabric had twisted itself into a damp rope. She shook it out once and pulled it over her head inside out. Looking down at the backward seam, she frowned as though someone else had dressed her, then peeled it off and fixed it without comment. Kai found her shorts by the wall and handed them over. A beat passed before she took them, not long, not dramatic, just enough to make the missing seconds present again.

By the time they were dressed enough to be less naked, the room smelled of sweat, wet cotton, and the clean detergent Kirchberg used on everything. She climbed back under the blanket without asking. He made room.

For a few seconds she lay on top of the blanket instead of under it, staring at the dark wall screen as if it might have an answer if one of them were stupid enough to wake it. Then she pulled the blanket over herself and set both hands on top where he could see them. Only after noticing did he realize he'd done the same thing with his own.

---

Sleep didn't come. The wall screen stayed off. The wind outside was steady and without opinion. Somewhere down the hall a door closed softly. The calibration bay's low hum was there if you listened for it, always there, the base frequency of a building designed around machines that tuned human bodies.

Every few minutes someone passed in the corridor and the bar of light under the door brightened, then thinned again. Lena lay on her back with one knee bent under the blanket. Kai could see the outline of her hand on her stomach each time the light shifted. Nobody stopped at his room.

"Are you cold?" he said.

"A little."

The blanket came higher over both of them when he pulled it up. The fabric was the same thin institutional weave as everything else in the athletes' quarters, designed for function, not comfort.

Kai flexed his left hand under the blanket, opening and closing his fingers. The wrist ache was there. It was always there. It sat in the bone like a tenant who had paid up and wasn't leaving.

"I should tell you what it's like," Lena said. She was on her back with her hands folded on her stomach, her hair drying into a rough fan against his pillow, and she said it the way she said most things, as a decision she'd made and was now carrying out.

"You don't have to."

"I know I don't have to. I want to tell you."

Silence held long enough that he thought she might have changed her mind. She opened her mouth once, closed it. Then she started.

"I don't black out," she said. "Everyone asks that first. It isn't that."

She turned her head to look at him. The dim light from the hallway caught the edge of her jaw.

"The body keeps going. Mine keeps going." A pause. "No. That isn't — the part that decides is still there, but it gets crowded back. The trained part holds the shape." She pressed her hand flat against her stomach. "My hands do the correct thing. If someone talks to me, I answer."

Her breathing caught. Kai's hand had stopped flexing.

"It sounds normal enough. Unless you know how I sound when I'm there." She paused. "When I'm actually there. From outside it's just efficient. Nobody stops anything. Then I'm back and seconds are missing."

"Coming back is —" She stopped. Started over. "It isn't being gone. I don't feel that part. Coming back is when I find out my body was doing things without me." Her voice was steady, but it cost her. "And nobody could tell."

The wind gusted against the window. Something metallic ticked in the heating vent.

"Nobody," Kai said.

"No. If I'd come back and kept going tonight, you wouldn't have known. If my body had stayed on the same line, you wouldn't have known."

Yes sat behind his teeth. He had felt the moment she left. But that wasn't the question. The question was whether he would have noticed once her body started moving again, or whether he would have stayed over her, touching a version of Lena that hit every mark and wasn't her.

"I don't know," he said.

A nod. She didn't look hurt by that. She looked like she'd expected it, which was worse.

"What does it — what does that stretch feel like after?" he said.

"I don't feel it. That's —" She stopped. "I only know it was there afterward. There's a cut. Usually it's seconds, but it isn't the time. One moment I'm in the pool and the next I'm at the wall and two laps are gone. Sometimes the water's colder. That's how I know." She was quiet. "Or someone answers a sentence I don't remember starting. And I look at the race footage and the stroke is mine, the turns are mine, everything looks right. But I was somewhere else."

"Margot said it's within acceptable parameters," Lena said. Her voice changed when she said Margot's name, going careful.

"You believe that?"

"No." Simple. Definite. A conclusion she'd measured against her own evidence.

They lay there. The bed was narrow and their shoulders were touching and the contact was nothing like what it had been half an hour ago. It was just contact. Two people in a small bed because neither one had left.

The heating vent clicked again. His stomach growled, which was stupid and perfectly timed and they both heard it.

"Sorry," he said.

"You didn't eat?"

"Half a protein bar. I got distracted."

The almost-smile was there in her breathing even if he couldn't see it in the dark. The moment was small and ordinary and he was grateful for it, proof that they were two people in a room and not two case files lying side by side.

"Can I tell you one thing?" Kai said. He hadn't planned to. She had given him the ugliest part of it, and he couldn't just lie beside her with that.

"Yes."

"About the blackout." He touched the scar on his cheek without thinking about it. "Everyone knows about the crash. The shoulder, the wrist. That's just injury. I've been hurt before."

The ceiling gave him the most boring line in the room, so he focused on the seam where it met the wall while he said the rest.

"Everything dropped out. Thirty seconds, they said. Not dark. Just — nothing there." He stopped. "And when I came back my legs were still going. The synthetic muscle was still firing the sprint pattern. I was on the track with blood in my eyes and my legs were still in the race."

Lena said nothing. He could feel her attention beside him.

"Yusuf showed me the telemetry after. The split was clean all the way to the wall." He let the air out of his lungs. "That's the part. It ran clean and I wasn't in it."

Beside him, her breathing changed. Shorter. More controlled.

"And that was terrifying," Lena said. She said it as fact.

"Yeah." He could hear his own breathing. A little fast for someone lying still.

After a second her hand came off the blanket and set itself on the middle of his chest, flat and deliberate, the same inventory-taking touch she'd used earlier. He let it stay.

Her palm stayed there through several breaths. Under it his heartbeat kept trying to outrun the fact that both of them were lying still. The bridge gave him rate, variability, recovery curve. None of that helped. What helped was the actual weight of her hand, warmer at the heel than the fingers because she'd pulled it out from under the blanket to touch him. The numbers were just numbers. This was Lena, here on purpose.

"I keep telling myself it's just equipment. Good spikes." He stopped. "Then tonight my hand moves before I think it."

Putting it that plainly made his chest go tight.

"Tonight," she said after a silence. "Your hand."

He let out a breath. "You felt it."

"Your grip changed on my hip. It got exact. The pressure landed in exactly the right place and stayed there. It didn't hurt. That wasn't it. It felt corrected."

"I didn't tell it to."

"No."

"It just found the angle and stayed there. I was still catching up."

For a long time she stayed quiet. Their shoulders were still touching. The wind kept pressing at the glass.

"I thought that would make tonight impossible," she said.

"Did it?"

She took her time. He let her. The ceiling was still boring and the woman beside him was present, right now, in a way he could feel.

"No," she said. "But I felt it."

"What now?" Kai said.

"Tomorrow I tell Yusuf exactly what happened," she said. "Not the coach version. Not the short version."

He waited.

"I want other people there. You. Anyone else who's having symptoms. And Margot can hear it in the room instead of writing the summary afterward."

He listened to the vent tick and thought of the way the institute liked its bad news: one athlete in one room with one official version by the end of it.

"And Park?" he said.

"Yes."

He turned his head toward her. "Why?"

"Because Meridian came here and called their patch the safe option." Her voice stayed level, but he could hear the force under it. "If she still believes that, she can say it after she hears what this actually is. Not through a report. Not through Margot."

"You think Yusuf will want that many people in one room?"

"He doesn't have to want it. He has to hear it."

The idea of saying the hand part out loud to a room full of people made something tighten low in his chest.

"You want me to tell them about tonight too," he said.

"Yes. And the blackout. If I talk alone, this stays my problem. If you talk alone, it stays yours."

He lay there with that for a second. If they stayed separate, they stayed manageable: separate bodies, separate case files.

"I'll come," he said.

"I know."

His hand found hers on the blanket between them. Her knuckles were cooler than her palm. The skin at the base of her thumb caught slightly against his. He closed his fingers and tried to stay inside that small, ordinary awkwardness while the bridge fussed in the background, logging temperature, grip force, contact across both their palms as if it could improve a handhold by naming it. He stayed with the roughness, with the fact of her, with the part that was his.

"It's doing it again," she said.

The correction was there immediately. His fingers had redistributed around hers, grip micro-correcting to equalize pressure. He hadn't told them to.

"Yeah."

Before he could loosen his hold, Lena turned her hand over and laced their fingers together. Another correction moved through his grip inside hers, small and unwelcome. Then another: thumb shifting, pressure evening itself across her knuckles, palm settling harder against skin than he'd meant. She didn't comment on them again. She just held on, accepting the movement without pretending it wasn't there. The wall stayed dark. The hallway light held its thin gold bar across the floor. Outside, wind moved against the window with the constancy of altitude, and the mountains were behind the glass in the dark.

They lay there awake, feeling each correction as it came, waiting for daylight and the room where they'd have to say it out loud.


# Chapter 12

The room they had spent the night waiting for looked smaller in daylight. Yusuf had pushed the conference table against the far wall and arranged chairs in a rough semicircle. It hadn't helped much. Eight people in a room built for six, and nobody looked comfortable.

Kai sat between Lena and Yuki Tanaka, a Japanese sprinter whose left hand still went numb without warning, a gift from the virus. Meridian's patch hadn't fixed it because Yuki hadn't signed. On Lena's other side sat two middle-distance runners Kai knew by event but not by name, a woman with close-cropped red hair who ran the 1500 and a South African in his late twenties who ran the 800. Behind them all, in a chair she'd pulled a full meter back from the group, Margot sat with her legs crossed and her arms folded, as though she'd come to audit rather than participate.

Jin-seo Park had the chair nearest the door. Lena had invited her, which had surprised everyone, including Jin-seo, who sat holding a paper cup of coffee she'd stopped drinking twenty minutes ago.

Through the west-facing windows, the Bernese Alps stood in late-afternoon light the color of old tin. The lower ridges were patchy where snowmelt had started, grey rock showing through the white in irregular blotches. Water ticked off the eaves in an uneven rhythm that Kai had been trying not to listen to since he sat down.

Yusuf looked tired. He'd been working nights in the calibration bay. It showed in the red threading his eyes, the shirt untucked on one side, the way his Birmingham accent sat heavier than usual. He had his laptop open on a stool.

"Right," he said. "So. Short version."

He turned the laptop toward the group. On the screen was a schematic Kai couldn't parse, firmware architecture in color-coded subsystems, the visual language of a field he had never learned to read. He stopped trying after three seconds.

"I can build a patch that targets the virus's propagation mechanism. Just that part. It walls off the conversation your systems have with the calibration network, so the corruption can't ride it from body to body." Yusuf tapped the screen. "It doesn't require full read-write access to your enhancement systems. Doesn't require the data license Meridian's asking for. It's compartmentalized."

"Works how?" Yuki asked.

"Your systems still calibrate. They still do what they do. But the shared pathway gets isolated, so the virus can't keep moving through the network."

"And the blackouts?"

"Should stop. The cascade starts in corrupted firmware. If the corruption can't spread, it shouldn't trigger."

"Should," Margot said from the back of the room.

Yusuf looked at her. "Should. Yeah. That's the honest word."

He pulled up a second screen. Performance metrics. Reaction times. Force output. Sensory acuity scores. Kai recognized the format from his own quarterly assessments.

"Costs," Yusuf said. "Three things you need to know. One, this hasn't been tested on a live human system. I've run it in simulation and on isolated hardware, but that's not the same thing. I can't promise it works. I can't promise there aren't side effects I haven't modeled."

Water ticked off the eaves. Kai could feel everybody doing the arithmetic and hating the sum.

"Two, even if it works, you're looking at a performance reduction. Ten to fifteen percent. The patch shuts down some optimization pathways. Reaction time. Motor efficiency. Sensory processing. You'll be slower. Less precise. Still enhanced, but the ceiling comes down."

"Ten to fifteen on what?" the redheaded runner asked.

"On the part that cleans up after you," Yusuf said. "Starts. Turns. The last little correction before you even know you needed one. Off the gun you'll feel the gap first. Not a dramatic one. Just enough that the race has started and your calf is still arriving. In the pool the wall stops arriving early. If your hand is short, you feel the drag and fix it yourself."

The South African runner grimaced. "So every mistake gets bigger."

"Not every mistake," Yusuf said. "Just the ones your system's been hiding for years."

Yuki lifted her left hand off her knee. Two fingers lagged before they curled. "And the sensory side?"

"You lose the smoothing layer," Yusuf said. "Things land dull or late and you do more of the sorting yourself."

"Ten to fifteen percent isn't making a final," the redheaded runner said. "That's not a medal conversation. That's a first-round exit."

"Yeah," Yusuf said. "Could be."

"And three?" Kai asked.

Yusuf looked down at the laptop long enough that Kai knew the answer was bad.

"The patch permanently severs the neural coaching feed interface."

Margot's chair scraped the tile. A small involuntary sound came out of her before she caught it. Her body went first. By the time her face caught up it had gone bloodless, the same stripped look she wore in telemetry reviews. Her left hand had already snapped to her right wrist. Her thumb jammed under the edge of the interface hard enough to lift the skin; when she pulled it free, a red crescent stayed under the strap.

After *permanently*, all Kai could hear was water off the eaves.

"The feed uses the same bidirectional data pathway the virus does," Yusuf said. "I can't wall off the virus without closing that connection too. They're the same architecture. So if you take my patch, no more real-time coaching feed. Permanently."

He looked at Margot. Everyone looked at Margot.

She didn't move. Her arms stayed folded across her chest and her face stayed blank. Kai couldn't get past how fast the color had left it.

"OK," she said. "Untested. Double-digit hit. No feed. Right before the Games."

"That's what I just said, yeah."

"And Meridian's is tested."

"Meridian's patch comes with a contract that signs over every piece of biological data your body's ever generated. Forever. Transferable. They can sell it."

"I know what the contract says." Margot leaned forward. "I'm saying your fix is worse for the athletes. More risk. More cost. And you're asking them to take your word for it."

"It's their data."

"It's their bodies. And you're proposing to experiment on them. No feed means nobody feels a drift while it's happening except the athlete inside it."

Lena still hadn't spoken. She sat with her hands in her lap, her back very straight. Her hair was pulled back tightly enough to leave the port bare.

"How would the patch interact with existing desync symptoms?" she asked.

Yusuf turned to her. His voice shifted when he spoke to Lena, slower, more careful, the calibration-bay voice that made bad news sound manageable.

"Honestly, I don't know for certain. It should stabilize the desync by interrupting the corrupted feedback loop. But desync is cumulative. Whatever changes your system's already made to your neural patterns, those don't reverse just because the cause gets cut off. If the link has rewritten a motor path, that rewritten path stays. If it's pushed words out of reach, those don't come back just because I close the door behind them."

"So the damage stays."

"The damage to date stays. The progression stops. That's the theory."

"And the missing time?" Lena asked. "Sometimes I come back with my shoulders burning and no memory of the set that put it there."

Yusuf's mouth tightened. "That part doesn't reverse either."

"Or the patch locks in whatever state my system is in right now."

"That's possible too. Yeah."

Lena nodded once. Her fingers laced tighter, then loosened, then laced again. Kai had learned that pattern over the last few weeks. She was checking that her hands were doing what she'd told them to do.

Kai kept both hands flat on his thighs.

"What's Meridian's performance hit?" he asked.

"None," Yusuf said. "Their patch is designed to maintain full optimization."

"Because they keep full access."

"Because they keep full access. Yeah."

The South African runner rubbed the back of his neck. "So the choice is give up our data and compete at full capacity, or keep our data and compete at eighty-five percent with a patch nobody's ever tried on a living person."

"And if we do neither?" Yuki asked. She pressed the pads of her numb fingers into her thigh hard enough to dimple the fabric. "If we wait."

Yusuf looked at her hand. "Then you keep whatever symptoms you've got and you gamble on how fast they spread. Numbness stays numbness until it doesn't. A dropout at breakfast is one thing. A dropout off the blocks is your teeth on the track before you know you've gone."

The redheaded runner looked at Yuki's hand. "Yesterday my calf fired on the stairs and I hit the rail hard enough to bruise my ribs. I'm not even sure that was the virus."

"That's the choice," Yusuf said.

"That's a shit choice."

"Yeah. It is."

The room held.

Yuki looked at her hand again. "This morning I dropped a bowl because I didn't know I'd stopped holding it," she said. "It shattered in the cafeteria. I stood there with cereal on my shoe and still didn't feel the cut until I saw blood." She held up the pad of her index finger. A narrow strip of plaster crossed it. "That was before training."

The South African runner stared at the plaster. "My federation clause drops if I'm not top eight at trials. Not medal. Top eight. If I lose a tenth on the first lap, they put someone else in my lane and that's that."

The redheaded runner gave a short laugh with no amusement in it. "My sponsor bonus disappears if I don't make the team final. So yes, I'd like the ethical version. I'd also like rent in August."

Kai looked from one face to the next. Yuki kept her cut finger lifted away from her knee. The South African runner stared at the floor with his lips moving once, soundless.

Margot stood. She didn't leave. She walked to the window and stood with her back to the room, looking at the mountains. The late light caught the feed interface band at her pulse point and the raw strip of skin she'd scraped open under it.

"You built this in the calibration bay," she said. "On institute time. Using institute resources. Does Petra know?"

Yusuf's expression tightened slightly. "That's not—"

"Does the facility director know?"

"This isn't about facility authorization."

"It's about whether anyone checked your work." Margot turned back toward him. "You built a firmware patch in your spare time. You want to install it in people's nervous systems. The only quality control here is you telling us the simulation looked clean."

Yuki looked down at her numb hand and pressed it into her thigh as though confirming it was still there.

"Why'd you build it?" the South African runner asked.

Yusuf looked at him.

"You're the calibration engineer. You could've recommended Meridian's patch, filed your reports, gone home clean. You didn't have to spend your nights building something nobody asked for. So why?"

Yusuf's posture changed. The shoulders settled. The spine straightened. When he got that composed, Kai had learned to watch him harder. It usually meant he had found the version of the truth he could live with saying aloud.

"Because Meridian's solution is architecturally excessive for the problem it solves. The data access they're requiring isn't necessary."

"That's the technical answer," the runner said. "I'm asking the other one."

Jin-seo's paper cup gave a faint crack under her grip. The sound was small, but in the room it landed like a plate breaking.

Yusuf looked at the screen on his laptop. Then at his hands. Then back at the group.

"My sister has Voss-Kessler syndrome."

Kai had expected money, maybe ego, maybe simple hatred of Meridian. Not a sister.

"It's progressive. Neural deterioration. Rare. No treatment. She's twenty-nine and her speech is already—" He stopped there, jaw working once. "Meridian wants a license to data that could actually matter for people like her. Not in some abstract future. Specific mapping. Motor pathways. Speech pathways. The stuff you generate every day."

He took a breath and kept going because stopping would have been worse. Kai could hear the point where this had stopped being an argument and turned back into one body he couldn't save.

"If Meridian locks that data under their contract, the research path closes. Their license is irrevocable and transferable. They're not sharing it with outside researchers working on a condition that affects four hundred people worldwide. There's no money in it." He looked around the room. "So yes. I built an alternative that doesn't require giving Meridian permanent ownership of your biological information. And yes, part of that is because I don't want them owning data that might help my sister."

Yuki spoke before anyone else. "Did you use our calibration data for it?"

Yusuf didn't dodge. "Yes."

"For this specifically?"

"Yes."

Jin-seo exhaled through her nose. "Without asking them."

"It was already collected," Yusuf said. "I stripped identifiers where I could."

"Where you could," Margot said.

The redheaded runner glanced sideways at the man beside her. He rubbed his chin. Yuki looked back at the laptop as though the diagram on the screen had changed meaning. Kai felt the room tip a few degrees. The screen stayed bright between them, and nobody asked another question about the code.

The South African runner said, "So your reason to protect us and your reason to put this in us are sitting in the same chair."

Yusuf didn't flinch. "Yeah."

Kai felt that hit harder than denial would've. Yusuf didn't blink or look away. Across from him, the South African runner let out one breath through his nose and said nothing.

Then everyone looked at Yusuf like the patch came with his sister attached.

---

Jin-seo spoke first.

"I'm sorry about your sister."

She said it plainly. Not corporate condolence. Not performance. She'd set the cold coffee on the floor by her chair. Her hands were in her lap now, fingers laced so tightly the knuckles had gone pale. Kai believed her, which made the room worse.

"Thank you," Yusuf said.

"I understand why you built it." She looked at Lena, then at Kai. "My brother died from a cascade failure. 2034. Same kind of architecture — shared network, propagation." She looked down at her hands. "He was twenty-six. Running the 1500. At eight hundred meters his legs stopped and the rest of him kept going."

The muscles around her mouth tightened.

"Heart rate hit 240 before the shutdown engaged. It was late."

Outside, the mountain shadows had lengthened across the snowmelt. Kai could see why nobody rushed to tell her she was wrong.

"So I can't —" She stopped. "I'm not going to tell people your patch is safe enough because Meridian's contract is bad. Those are different problems."

The South African runner stood before Yusuf answered. "Ms. Park," he said. "Room 214, yeah?"

Jin-seo looked up at him.

"If Meridian's offer is still open, reopen my file tonight." He dragged a hand over the back of his neck. "My sisters eat off my contract. I can't show up slower and call it a principle."

She pulled a tablet from her bag and woke the screen. Contract text lit her face blue.

"All right," she said.

That changed the room. The choice had been theory until somebody put his name back on it.

Margot pushed away from the window. She didn't sit.

"Then talk about what actually happens to the bodies," she said. "Not the philosophy. The bodies."

Kai looked at her. She hadn't fought this hard over contracts or consent. Only over the feed.

"What does ten to fifteen feel like?" she asked.

Yusuf was quiet for a second. "Starts drag. Fine correction goes. If your foot lands wrong, the mistake runs higher before it gets caught. Ankle, knee, hip. In the pool you're late to the wall and you wear the drag with your whole shoulder."

Kai's own shoulder gave a dull pulse at that, memory arriving before thought: the wall, the wrong angle, the part of the body that kept going after the rest of him had vanished.

"And the feed?"

"Gone."

Margot reached back for the chair and missed it on the first pass. Her fingers closed on air, then found the wood with a hard clack that made Yuki look up.

"No coach in the body if something goes wrong," she said. "The athlete's alone in it."

"That's right," Yusuf said.

"If they take your patch and it fails during competition, what happens?"

Yusuf was quiet for a beat too long. Kai knew that pause too. It was the space where the answer got checked for how ugly it would sound.

"Say it," Margot said.

"Whatever sense drops goes," Yusuf said. "Sight, balance, spatial mapping. The body keeps the last command it had. If you're in drive phase, it keeps driving. If you're turning in the pool, you keep turning blind. Same as competing unpatched."

"Which can kill someone."

"Yes."

"And if it works, they're still slower at the Games. Start drags, wall's late, landings are raw." She looked at Yuki. At the redheaded runner. "That's a missed final. That's someone's rent."

"I know that," Yusuf said.

"Do you?"

Lena spoke before he could answer.

"I want to understand the desync interaction better."

"OK," Yusuf said.

"If your patch works, this is what stays." She looked at her hands. "The gaps. The missing time. I have soreness after sets I don't remember doing."

"Yes."

"And Meridian's keeps the optimization access."

"Yes."

"So with yours I stay like this."

Yusuf nodded.

"And with theirs the link keeps its access. Maybe the symptoms improve. But it keeps choosing before I do."

Yusuf didn't answer.

"If I wait?" Lena asked.

"The corruption keeps writing," Yusuf said. "The gaps can get bigger. You come back halfway down a length with water in your nose and no memory of pushing off. A turn goes missing. It doesn't stop by itself."

Kai pictured her surfacing with chlorine in her sinuses and no memory of the wall. His jaw locked hard enough to ache before he noticed.

Lena pressed her hands heel to heel. One lagged a fraction before lining up with the other.

"If I go back to my room, by morning the damage is worse." She lifted her eyes to Yusuf. "If I'm doing it, I want it tonight."

Nobody said anything.

Yusuf looked at her hard. "Are you sure?"

"No," Lena said. "Set it up anyway."

He held her gaze, then nodded once. "All right. Forty minutes."

The redheaded runner stayed where she was. "I'm not signing because Meridian tells me they tested themselves," she said. Her voice had thinned out now, tired more than angry. "And I'm not letting you put that thing in me tonight either. Lena goes first. If she's still herself tomorrow, you show me the numbers again."

"Fair," Yusuf said.

Yuki flexed her left hand open and closed. Two fingers stalled halfway. "I stay one more day," she said. "Not because I trust anybody in this room. Because I want to see what happens to her." She looked at Lena, then at Jin-seo. "After that I choose."

Kai kept looking at Margot's right wrist. The skin under the strap was rubbed raw. While Lena spoke, while Yusuf said *forty minutes*, Margot's hand kept creeping back to the interface band as if she could hold the feed there by force.

Jin-seo stood and lifted the tablet.

"I'm in room 214 if you want to talk," she said to Lena.

Lena nodded.

"Come with me," Jin-seo said to the South African runner.

He went with her.

Margot left next. No announcement. No line on the way out. She picked up her jacket from the chair back and walked through the door with her right wrist tucked against her ribs, sleeve pulled down over the abraded skin as though she could hide it.

The redheaded runner left after that, not with Jin-seo and not with Yusuf either. At the door she looked back at Lena, opened her mouth as though there might be something useful to say, then gave up and kept going. Yuki stayed seated long enough to touch each fingertip to the chair edge in turn. When she got to the ring finger, nothing happened on her face at all. Then she stood and went.

Yusuf closed his laptop. He looked at the half-empty room, at the chairs in their ragged semicircle, and for a second his face just sagged before he pulled it back into place.

"I'll be in the calibration bay," he said.

He left with the laptop under his arm.

Kai looked at Lena. "You OK?"

She kept staring at her hands.

"No," she said. "Come with me anyway."

She stood. Kai stood with her.

In the corridor, the fluorescent light flattened everybody. Jin-seo stood with the South African runner under the fire-evacuation map, tablet in hand now, contract text bright against his face. He scrolled too fast to be reading. Farther down, Yuki sat on the bench by the vending machine with her left hand on her knee and her right forefinger moving from fingertip to fingertip, checking what answered. The redheaded runner had stopped at the window at the end of the hall, arms folded, looking out at the darkening ridge as though the mountain might choose for her.

When Lena stepped out, all three looked up.

The South African runner looked back down first.

"Tomorrow," the redheaded runner said. "Tell us what it cost."

Lena nodded once.

Yuki said, "Tell us if you can feel the wall."

Then Lena turned toward the calibration bay, and Kai went with her.


# Chapter 13

The calibration rig's contact surface had gone from cold to warm to tacky with Lena's sweat in the first two hours. By the fourth, the dampness against her back had stopped mattering. Overhead, the fluorescent panels hummed at a frequency the system would normally have filtered out, a constant sixty-hertz buzz. Yusuf had disabled her automatic sensory filtering before the procedure began, and now every ambient sound arrived untouched: the equipment drone, the click of his keyboard, the thin whistle of ventilation through the ceiling vents.

"Stage four," Yusuf said. He took off his glasses and cleaned them on the hem of his shirt. He always did that at transitions, as if each new stage deserved a cleared view of the monitors. "This one remaps the motor pattern library. It's the longest stage. Ninety minutes, maybe more."

"What will it feel like?"

"Honestly, I don't know exactly. The simulation modeled pressure behind the eyes and at the interface." He touched the base of his own skull, mirroring her port. "The link's going to run hot again. Same as before, just sustained. I'll watch thermal output the whole time."

"And if it gets too hot?"

"Then I pull the stage and we're left with a half-rewritten motor library." He glanced at the screen. "Let's not do that."

"Okay."

"Ready?"

"Yes."

Heat bloomed at the port, the familiar two-centimeter circle of metal and ceramic at the base of her skull. It followed the neural pathways like liquid finding channels. That was the closest description she had. Signal routes were not supposed to have temperature, but she could feel the warmth moving where signal had always moved. It reached her temples, pressed behind her eyes, and settled there as a dull force that was neither pain nor comfort.

Past the cranial pathways it kept going. Down through the cervical interface, into the motor networks, into the fine wiring that governed hands, shoulders, core, every chain of muscle firing through her freestyle stroke. By filling it, the heat made the architecture legible.

"How's that?" Yusuf asked.

"Six. Seven. Still seven." His one-to-ten scale had become a shorthand during the earlier stages. Seven meant manageable, uncomfortable, not yet close to losing stillness.

"Good. Stay with me."

Above her, ceiling tiles held ninety-four perforations each. She had counted the first row in stage one. The water stain in the far corner looked, if she felt generous, like Lake Zurich. Generosity had nothing to do with it. After four hours her mind wanted the stain to be anything it could recognize. Counting holes and enduring the rewrite divided her attention cleanly enough that the split became almost meditative. One part of her tracked the ceiling. Another felt the firmware touch muscles she used every day and had never felt separately: the small bands between her ribs, the flexors of her toes, the deep cuff muscles that held her shoulders steady through the catch.

In the smaller muscles the heat changed character. It felt intimate, almost invasive, as if each piece of her were being lifted and checked on its own.

Twenty minutes into stage four, her vision split.

The ceiling stayed where it was, but a second layer of numbers rose through it: the distance from her eyes to the tile surface, 271.4 centimeters; the luminosity of each fluorescent panel, a value she could read but not name the unit of; the air in the bay, 21.3 degrees Celsius. Every figure refreshed once a second.

It was the raw sensory buffer, all the stripped calculations underneath the finished room. The rewrite had hauled them up and left them on top of everything else.

"Lena." Yusuf's voice reached her as sound and also as readout: decibel level, frequency spread, a minute difference in distance to the right ear and left. "Your heart rate jumped. What's happening?"

"I can see what the system sees." Speech took effort. The motor pattern for talking was being remapped too, and every syllable came with a trace of numbness. "Numbers. Distances. Everything measured."

His typing stopped, then resumed faster. "That's raw-buffer bleed-through. I expected some. Not full overlay. Can you still see the room? The normal room?"

"Both. I can see both."

"Right." Silence for a beat. "It should resolve when stage four completes. Seventy more minutes. Maybe eighty."

Closing her eyes did not help. The data layer remained, floating against the dark inside of her lids: the rig surface temperature, the distance to the nearest wall, Yusuf's thermal signature as a hand-shaped concentration to her right. Open eyes were better. At least then the numbers had a room to sit on.

Time stopped behaving like units and turned into endurance. For the first half hour Yusuf kept talking in the professional register he used during calibrations. Then fatigue started to roughen him.

"Your motor library is massive. I've calibrated dozens of athletes and I've never seen this density of stored patterns. Since you were four, Lena. It's kept everything." He checked another stream of data. "Training sets. Competitions. Drills from the developmental program. I have to rewrite around all of it."

"Is that a problem?"

"It's a lot of data. This is going to run longer than ninety minutes." His accent thickened as he got tired, vowels rounder, t's softer. "Maybe another hour. I'll know more once we're through the competition patterns."

"I can wait."

"Yeah. I know you can." He looked away, then back to the monitor. "I've got tea somewhere if you need it. It'll be cold."

"I'm fine."

The dual view kept feeding her details she had never known the system stored: the rig's vibration frequency moving through her spine, Yusuf's breathing rate a little above baseline, the wavelength spread of the overhead light. As numbers, none of it mattered. As knowledge of how long the system had been studying her, it did.

A memory surfaced from the old Meridian facility in Zug: pediatric rigs sized for smaller bodies, a mural of fish on the ceiling, Latin species names she had learned to sound out before she could read German properly. She had spent childhood under painted fish while the link was tuned and expanded and made invisible. Now the heat was giving it edges.

In the fifth hour the temperature spiked.

Her hands clenched the armrests before she could stop them. The port felt like metal pressed to a heating element. Warmth shot into her jaw and teeth.

"Don't move." Yusuf's voice tightened. "I see it. Forty-two point seven. I'm cutting write speed."

Lake Zurich returned in the ceiling stain, the eastern shore this time. School trips from Zug, when the world outside the program had looked like a place other children belonged to.

"Coming down," he said. "Ten seconds."

Under stress, German numbers came first. *Eins, zwei, drei.* At eight the heat started to break. At twelve it was back to the hard, tolerable burn that had held her all night.

"Sorry," Yusuf said. "Competition patterns are written denser. More data per node, more processing heat. The simulation missed it."

One finger at a time, Lena uncurled her grip from the armrests. Her palms had dented the padding.

"We can stop for a bit," he said.

"No."

"Lena, if we pause, we can reset the curve."

"If we pause, the thermal residue drops and you have to build back to write temperature." The answer arrived whole, either from years of sitting in rigs or from whatever the system was exposing on its way out. "That's twenty more minutes of this before you can do any work. Keep going."

He did.

Stage five remapped the sensory integration layer. Yusuf told her to keep talking through it.

Brown drained out of the water stain in the ceiling corner. The seam where wall met ceiling loosened into angle and brightness. Yusuf leaned over her and his face broke into temperatures and distances, glasses two cool shapes in front of the warmer field of his eyes.

"Tell me what you've got."

"Position. Luminance. Temperature." The words came clipped by a sudden rise in her stomach. "No surfaces."

He lifted a hand into her sightline. She knew it was a hand because five narrow heat signatures moved together.

"Track it."

She tried. The movement made sense as changing numbers. Her body still recoiled too late. Near and far had stopped being instincts and become arithmetic. By the time she understood his hand was close, nausea had already turned hard under her ribs. She twisted her head against the rig and the world flattened further.

"Don't move," Yusuf said.

"I wasn't trying to."

He had an emesis basin under her jaw before the dry heave hit. Nothing came up. The retch still hurt. Her abdominal muscles fired in the wrong order, upper first and lower late, each part joining after the part before it had already failed.

Her eyes watered. The tears entered the visual field as saline values at the edges.

"Again," Yusuf said, quieter now. "Localize me."

"Right side. Thirty-eight centimeters."

"Good. Where am I touching?"

Something brushed her left arm. Pressure arrived first without place. Location dragged after it like a boat coming loose from the dock.

"Forearm," she said.

"Where?"

She guessed wrist. It was elbow. He wrote that down without comment.

For twenty minutes the room would not become a room again. She narrated coordinates and values to Yusuf in short precise sentences because nouns were suddenly expensive. When he moved a penlight across her field, the point of brightness did not cross space. It jumped left to right through a grid and her eyes jerked after it. When he touched a cold metal instrument to the side of her neck, temperature and pressure arrived first. Skin came later, if at all.

Yusuf kept her working: look left, look right, count the lights, tell me which monitor is brighter, tell me if the vibration under your spine changed when he raised the write speed. Every answer had to be dragged up whole. The strain of naming things kept panic from taking the space.

At one point he asked her to reach toward the edge of the rig, not far, just enough to lift two fingers from the padding. She stopped before the command became motion.

"I can't tell where the edge starts."

"You don't have to grab it. Just point."

She pointed too high. Air met her fingertips. Shame came stupidly, like she had failed a child's task in front of a teacher. She brought her hand back to the armrest and pressed until the vinyl seam bit her skin.

"It's fine," Yusuf said. "That's what this stage is."

No, Lena thought. This was what her body was when the hidden work stopped.

The numerical room held and held. A value stream refreshed above her like rain falling in columns. Her own pulse displayed in the corner of her vision as a climbing integer that looked calmer than she felt. The rig's vibration moved through her back in measured frequency. Yusuf's breathing registered from the right, then the left, depending on where he leaned. She began separating the body of him from the data of him by memory alone.

Then color came back wrong. The stain in the ceiling corner turned sickly yellow-brown. Yusuf's shirt went too blue. The red standby light on the monitor struck her eyes like a pin.

She turned toward the water station because the clear bottle on it looked safest, and the movement sent another wave through her stomach.

"Steady," Yusuf said. "Don't chase it. Let the room catch up."

Edges sharpened by degrees. Depth returned as an ache behind the eyes, then settled into something she could use. The basin stayed in Yusuf's hand for another minute before he set it down.

"Color's back," Lena said.

"Normal color?"

"No. Too much."

"That should settle."

It settled only as far as tolerable. The overhead panels still bit. The grain in the rig's plastic side rail showed every fleck and seam under the light.

Stage six arrived without heat, pressure, or warning. The stage began as absence.

At first she thought nothing was happening. Then saliva gathered under her tongue and stayed there.

Usually a swallow would have happened somewhere below notice. This one waited. She had to lift the back of her tongue on purpose, close her throat on purpose, hold the next breath back on purpose. By the time she managed it, panic had already flashed once through her chest.

"What changed?" Yusuf asked.

"It didn't swallow."

He looked at the thermal and motion traces, then back at her face. "Right. Keep telling me."

The low brace in her lower back released next. Then her shoulders. Then the tiny tension in her neck that had always held her head one degree off the padding. Weight poured down through her body as if someone had drained a hidden scaffolding out from under the skin.

Her left hand slid off the armrest, not far, just enough that she had to notice it.

"Lift it," Yusuf said.

She sent the command the way she always would have. Nothing visible happened at first. The intention was there, but the hand did not move.

She had to rebuild the instruction smaller: shoulder first, then upper arm, then elbow, then wrist, then fingers. By the time the hand rose, the motion looked clumsy even from inside it.

"Again," Yusuf said.

She lowered it. Raised it. The second attempt came quicker and still wrong.

"Other side."

The right hand obeyed with the same delay, the same ugly sequence. The motion had to travel the whole route before the hand rose: shoulder, elbow, wrist, fingers.

Yusuf adjusted something on the board. "Try a partial sit-up. Not all the way."

Lena drew breath and told her body to rise.

Her head came first. Her stomach answered late. Her hips stayed heavy. She folded in the middle, lost the line of herself, and dropped back hard enough to jar the port.

Pain flashed white at the base of her skull.

"Stop." Yusuf was beside her before she tried again, one hand braced at her shoulder without pinning her down. "Smaller. Think ribs first."

"I did."

"No, you thought up. That's different."

She hated that he was right. Up was not a usable instruction anymore.

She tried again, thinking ribs first, then abdomen, then neck last. This time she made it halfway before the effort started shaking in her arms. Yusuf pressed two fingers against her sternum and guided her back down.

He looked older when he was tired. The careful warmth he used with athletes had thinned into concentration and sympathy, both exhausted.

"That's restricted mode on the autonomous layer," he said. "It'll still keep you from falling. Injury prevention stays active. But it won't initiate motor patterns anymore." He paused, watching her absorb it. "It won't be swimming for you."

"Good," Lena said, and meant it in the narrowest way possible.

He gave her another task: flex the left foot, then the right, rotate the head slowly to the left and stop before the port pulled, squeeze his fingers, release. Each movement was available. None came cheap. She kept running into missing anticipations: the ankle that did not brace before the foot moved, the shoulder that waited to be told about the arm, the jaw that hung half a second too loose between sentences.

Outside the bay windows the sky had shifted from black to dark grey. Along the eastern ridge a green-white band had formed where dawn was working its way onto the peaks. The green startled her. The valley below stayed dark.

At 6:43 he disconnected the cables from her port with the small exact movements she trusted: loop, tug-test, withdrawal. He had always been the best calibration engineer she worked with. After nine hours inside the architecture of her body, that fact felt closer to trust than to assessment.

He helped her swing her legs off the rig but did not lift her. The patch was in. Whatever her body did next needed to be hers.

The floor found her feet too abruptly. The tile was cold. The slipper foam felt thin. The left knee drifted inward before she caught it. She put a hand on the rig rail and waited for the room to stop rocking.

"Easy," Yusuf said, already moving toward the water station.

He held out a paper cup. She took it too hard, crushing the rim. Water jumped over her knuckles.

"Sorry."

"No need."

She tried again with the next sip and overcorrected, holding the cup so lightly it trembled. Room-temperature water hit her tongue and stayed small. She had to tell her lips to seal around the rim. A thread ran down her chin anyway. She swallowed on purpose and the taste was already over.

"Sit," Yusuf said.

"If I sit, I'll stay there."

"You'll still do the diagnostics."

"I know."

She stayed standing. He did not argue. He only moved the stool closer in case she changed her mind.

***

He started the diagnostics before the patch had fully gone quiet in her nerves.

"I need the first pass now," he said. "Before you compensate."

The reaction panel stood on an articulated arm beside the main monitor, a half-circle of touch targets and light squares she had used a hundred times. She sat because sitting was faster than proving she could stand through it. Yusuf reset the program and the first green square flashed.

She saw it. She moved for it. Her finger landed on plastic an inch to the right.

The error tone was soft and still humiliating.

A second flash came. She hit the square and felt the correction she had not made in time. The finger had traveled on the wrong line all the way to the panel. Nothing underneath had rescued it mid-reach.

A third flash came, and she was too slow.

By the fourth sequence, she understood the new rule: if the first line of movement was bad, the movement stayed bad unless she consciously repaired it. Conscious repair cost time, and time added up.

Yusuf ran the panel four times. The first score was ugly. The second was better. The third settled. The fourth matched the third closely enough that he stopped there.

"Again," Lena said.

"Not yet."

"Again."

He met her eyes, then restarted the sequence. Fatigue had already thickened her fingers, and the fifth run came back slower than the fourth. She hated him a little for being unsurprised.

"Reaction time had slowed from 173 milliseconds to 192," he said after logging it. "That's probably close to real baseline for today."

"Probably."

"You're nine hours deep on no sleep."

"So are you."

"I'm not racing anyone."

The next checks went fast. On the acuity screen the smallest line broke apart too soon. On the speaker test a tone behind her right shoulder landed somewhere between wall and ceiling, nowhere she could point to in time.

He wrote down the misses and brought over the foam balance pad.

With her eyes open she could borrow the room. Without them, the body went blunt.

The first sway came from the ankles and should have corrected there. It rose to the hips instead. By the time she caught it, her right foot had already stepped off the pad.

"Again," she said.

"All right."

He waited until she had reset her feet.

The second attempt lasted longer. Then the left shoulder drifted back, the pelvis answered late, and she lurched sideways hard enough that the foam pad shot out from under her heel. Yusuf caught her by the forearm before she hit the counter.

His grip was careful. Her pulse wasn't.

"This is the one," she said after a moment.

"Yeah," he said. "I know."

"I feel like I'm wearing thick socks. All over my body."

He let go, set the clipboard on the counter, and didn't start another station.

"The desync markers are gone," he said. "Completely. No residual corruption, no anomalous pattern initiation, no autonomous motor firing. Your system isn't going to do things without you anymore."

She looked at the balance pad half under the counter and the red mark Yusuf's hand had left on her forearm.

"And the cost."

"Reaction's down. Vision and spatial hearing are blunt. Proprioception's the worst of it." He rubbed at one eye with thumb and forefinger. "The system's continuous body-position model is gone, and the smoothing that used to cover for it is gone too. Some of it may improve as you relearn. The architecture won't."

"How much slower?"

"In the water? I don't know yet."

"Guess."

He looked unhappy before he answered, which made the answer easier to trust.

"Enough to matter. Not enough to make you ordinary."

Cold from the tile reached her through the thin facility slippers and sat there, a square chill under each foot.

Only then did Lena notice the quiet.

The bay kept making its noises: equipment drone, ventilation hiss, Yusuf turning a page, the tiny crackle of static from the monitor casing when he shifted in the chair. She could hold each one apart without trying. Nothing blurred into the next.

"It's quiet," she said.

Yusuf looked up. "The bay?"

"My head." Her fingers touched her temple. "It's quiet in my head. I didn't know it was loud."

Fatigue had wrecked him. His shirt was wrinkled, his face slack, stubble dark along his jaw. He pushed his glasses higher on his nose.
