"Yeah," he said. "I modeled that. Ambient processing uses bandwidth. When it goes offline, you feel the difference. I just didn't know how it would land."

She listened again. Ventilation. A cable settling against metal. Yusuf breathing through his nose. The sounds stood in the air like separate objects.

"It feels like a vent shut off in a room you've slept in for years," she said. "You only hear it after it stops."

He made a note and did not try to improve the metaphor.

When he offered to walk her to medical observation, she said no. When he suggested sleep before the pool, she said no to that too.

"You need rest."

"I need to know what happened in the water."

"It still happened here."

"Not enough."

He started to argue, then gave up. "If you get dizzy in the pool wing, you sit down. If your vision blurs, you stop. If anything starts feeling like the old desync, you come straight back."

"I know."

"And Lena."

She looked up.

"It's going to feel like you're worse. Everything clumsy, everything off." He pushed his glasses up his nose. "That's just the gap, yeah? The system was filling all of that in. So now you feel it."

She nodded once and took her towel from the shelf by the door.

The corridor to the pool wing gave her concrete floor, overhead lights, chlorine gathering strength as she got closer to the water. Each step ended cleanly. Her heel met cold tile. Texture followed. Then nothing until the next step. Halfway down the corridor she stopped beside the handrail just to stand inside the plainness of that.

Her body did not like standing still anymore. Stillness had to be held from several places at once.

She went on.

The women's locker room was empty except for the dryers running and the smell of detergent baked into tile. On the bench she peeled the last adhesive sensor pads from her ribs and shoulders. The skin underneath stayed pink. There was a perfect round mark around the port where the interface collar had sat all night, the edges darkening toward bruise.

She undressed more slowly than she meant to. Her right hand missed the locker latch the first time. The sports bra stuck at one shoulder because she failed to lower the scapula in the right order. Pulling on the swimsuit should have taken three seconds. Instead she had to watch her own fingers flatten the suit under the straps and tug each side level. The motions were simple. They only refused to disappear into habit.

At the sink she bent to splash water on her face and had to brace a hand on the counter because the angle of her body did not report itself until she was already in it. The mirror showed the same face as yesterday and not the same arrangement inside it. Her eyes looked tired, yes, but more than tired, untuned.

She left before the word could settle.

***

The pool sat empty, her training slot left blank on the board. The overhead lights were on, turning the surface into pale moving sheets. She set her towel on the bench, rolled her shoulders once, and listened.

Water lapped the gutter.

Air moved through the vents.

She heard her own breathing.

That was all.

She crossed to lane three and went in.

Underwater, the entry boom spread through her skull as one blunt rush. The wall, the lane rope, her own kick, the slap of displaced water from the dive all lived inside the same noise until her eyes sorted them back apart. The first stroke hit wrong, too flat at the entry and without enough purchase through the forearm. She corrected on the second and overcorrected on the third, hand angled so sharply that her shoulder complained.

Freestyle at easy pace required work she had never had to name before. Every stroke asked for instructions: reach, catch, pull, roll, breathe. Hand angle, shoulder rotation, the timing of the kick all stayed where she put them, good or bad. Her body did only what she told it to do and did it with the level of skill she actually possessed.

At twelve meters her head had drifted low enough that water rode over the back of her cap and down the line of her neck. She fixed it. Two strokes later the kick lost rhythm because the left ankle had gone slack at the end of the beat. She fixed that too.

Her palm kept waiting for the catch to announce itself cleanly. Nothing answered. Chlorine, warmth, and resistance pressed over her hand in one broad sheet, and she had to choose the angle herself. The choice came a fraction late each time, not disastrously, but late enough to count.

At the first turn she misjudged the wall by half a stroke. Her fingers skimmed tile instead of planting square. She had to shorten, tuck, and flip off a compromised line. Water punched up her nose on the push-off. She broke out angry and swallowed some of it with the next breath.

At the wall after one hundred meters she coughed pool water into her hand and looked at the red arm of the pace clock sweeping upward.

Nothing arrived early. Air came only when her lungs demanded it. Entry angle, catch, roll, kick: each had to be chosen and corrected in real time. The stroke was hers all the way through, messier than before and quieter than she had expected.

She pushed off again.

The second hundred went cleaner. She gave herself fewer tasks at once: hand first, then breath timing, then kick. When she tried to control everything, the stroke broke apart. When she picked one fault and let the others ride for a length, the body started learning the edges of the new rule.

At one wall she reached for air too late and had to take a hurried half-breath, mouth clipping the surface hard enough to sting her front teeth. At another she set the catch right and felt a whole length go by without obvious error. The pleasure of that was strange and almost small, more relief than pride.

Four laps later she held the wall and took a breath she had chosen cleanly this time.

She turned onto her back and floated for a moment in the middle of the lane. The ceiling panels above the pool looked plain and finite, white rectangles breaking in the surface glare. The water held her with the same blunt generosity it would have offered any other body.

Ordinary, she thought, and the word did not feel like insult.

She rolled back over and kept going.

Another eight laps were enough to show the shape of the loss. By the last turn the wall kept arriving a fraction later than her body expected, and the fractions kept stacking. A touch less reach, a touch less force, the recovery dragging on the way through. Over four hundred meters that accumulation became seconds. Seconds put you in another part of the sheet.

At the wall she folded her arms over the lane rope and let the lane line knock lightly against her forearms. The deck windows were still dark. Silence filled her head and kept filling it. She still couldn't tell whether to count it as damage.

The pool door opened.

Dara came in already suited, tightening her braid as she crossed the deck. The red elastic at her wrist had started to fray white at the edges. She set her bottle on the bench behind lane four, laid her towel square, and looked over.

"You're in early."

"I wanted to swim before anyone else got here."

"Did it work?"

"The desync is gone."

Dara nodded once. "And the rest?"

"I'm slower."

"How much?"

"I don't know yet."

Dara looked at the pace clock, then back at Lena. "Come on. One hundred."

"I just did four hundred."

"Do one more."

There was nothing cruel in it. Dara's eyes had already gone to the pace clock; if there was an answer here, she wanted it on the clock face. Lena pushed off when Dara did.

For the first ten meters they held even. Dara's stroke entered clean and disappeared clean. No wasted splash. No visible correction. Then Dara's length started buying distance. At the far wall Dara flipped while Lena still had three strokes left. Lena shortened, turned, and came off the wall knowing before she surfaced that the line out had been crooked.

By the time she found rhythm again, white feet were already leaving the other lane.

Dara touched at the near wall, lifted her goggles, and waited while Lena took the last five strokes in alone.

"Five seconds," Dara said when Lena reached the wall. "Easy pace."

Water ran off Dara's chin. She did not wipe it away.

"Why did you do it?" she asked. "Not the patch. This part."

Lena kept one hand on the wall because the body still wanted the wall there. "I was losing time. In the water. I'd be at one wall and then I'd be halfway down the lane and I didn't — I didn't know how I got there. Minutes just gone."

"But the body kept swimming."

"Yes."

"And it was faster than this."

"Yes."

Dara looked down the lane, then back at Lena. "I don't know how to think about that."

"You don't have to."

"You do."

Lena let water drip from her mouth before she answered. "I do."

Dara pulled at the frayed elastic on her wrist. "If it kept you on your race pace, if it kept you in line, if it kept you from falling apart in the water, I don't know why you'd cut it out."

"Because I wasn't swimming." Lena's grip shifted on the gutter tile. "The stroke was there. I wasn't. I was just — in there while it happened."

"That's five seconds, Lena."

"I know."

Dara's face did not change much. That was one of the things about her. Most of the time she looked composed in the way a well-calibrated body looks composed, everything arriving at the visible surface already corrected. Only her hands gave her away, the small pull at the elastic, the thumb rubbing the inside of her wrist where the skin was pale.

"Did it hurt?" Dara asked.

"The patch?"

"The silence after."

Lena looked across the water at the lane markers disappearing under the surface and at her own wake already flattening.

"Yes," she said. Then, because Dara was waiting for the rest and because the rest was harder to find, she added, "It still does."

"And you'd still pick it?"

"Yes."

"Even if this is the version of you that goes to Doha."

Water lapped the gutter between them.

"I think so," Lena said.

"You think."

"I haven't had breakfast."

That made Dara breathe out something that was almost a laugh and not warm enough to be one.

"All right," she said. "Eat something."

"That's not going to change the answer."

"No," Dara said. "Probably not."

She pushed off the wall to make room for a swimmer entering lane four, though there was no one there yet.

"I hope it holds," she said at last. "The fix."

Then she pulled her goggles back down and left on her interval, first stroke in place before Lena had let go of the wall.

Lena stayed where she was until the red hand reached the top of the pace clock again. Dara was already halfway down the pool, movement neat enough to seem pre-decided.

When Lena pushed off, the lane beside her was empty. Midway through the length the wall came a stroke later than she had judged. She took the extra stroke, turned, and kept going.


# Chapter 14

Tomas counted his steps as his first coach had taught him: *uno, dos, tres*. The rhythm had locked in before engineers ever touched his body, back when counting was the only technology available to a thirteen-year-old on a dirt track outside Quito. Three years of neural integration had not erased it. The enhancement handled stride frequency to the hundredth of a second now. It smoothed his approach pattern into a shape that felt less like running and more like discovering what running was for. But the counting stayed. His one piece of untouched equipment.

It was a mid-morning group session. Four athletes shared the straight and curve, each occupying the part of the room habit had assigned them. In the far lane Kai was doing light sprint work, his stride still favoring the shoulder that had healed wrong after the blackout crash six weeks ago. Two other athletes Tomas knew only by event, a pole vaulter from the Dutch program and a middle-distance runner whose name he kept forgetting and felt bad about, occupied the curve. Tomas liked group sessions. Somebody always noticed a clean clearance.

Tomas had called his mother last night. She'd wanted to know about the weather, so he'd described the snowmelt off the peaks, how the water ran in channels beside the road down to Meiringen, how the Alps looked different now that the ice was pulling back and exposing gray rock. Then his sister Valeria had grabbed the phone to announce she'd made the regional soccer team, and he'd promised to video-call her first match. From the other room his brother Diego, who was nine and not interested in sports, had shouted about a math test. Tomas hadn't caught the words. Before hanging up, he'd told his mother the food was fine and the training was going well and he loved her, and she'd said *cuídate, mi amor*, and he'd taped his lead ankle as he always did, three tight wraps, the tape pulling at the fine hair above the joint.

The ankle tape was still there now, under his compression sock, a small pressure he could feel with every stride.

The fourth hurdle was clean. The lead leg snapped forward and the trail leg swept through with the delayed precision that still surprised him sometimes, the enhancement smoothing his clearance into an aerodynamically exact pattern his body had not been able to make before the grafts. This was the part he loved, not with the senior athletes' resigned tolerance of a body that performed without being asked. Tomas still felt wonder at the meeting point between his effort and the machine's. Three years had not dulled that. The hurdle behind him rattled faintly on its base from the wind of his passage.

The fifth was clean too. The approach was good, his stride frequency settled, his arms pumping in the tight pattern Margot had drilled into him. A small warmth at the base of his skull told him she was there in the feed, a faint sense of being watched from inside. Margot monitored his cardiovascular output and pacing targets in real time through the coaching interface. Thinking about it never seemed necessary. It was like knowing his heart was beating, true and constant and not worth attention.

The sixth hurdle went clean. Twelve meters from the seventh, his body drove forward and the counting continued under everything, *uno, dos, tres*, and his heart rate was climbing, pushed past training range into a zone his body reached for because the coaching signal wanted it there. Nothing in it felt wrong to him. The push felt like effort, like desire.

Seven meters from the seventh hurdle, the world stopped.

It wasn't white or void, and it didn't arrive as a fade or a flicker or a dimming. Kai had described his blackout to the group, and Tomas had prepared himself for a sequence he could recognize and fight. This was not that. One instant the world was present and the next it wasn't. Sight, sound, proprioception, the rubber under his spikes, the temperature of the air in his lungs, the faint chlorine smell drifting from the pool three corridors away, all of it ceased at once. Nothing replaced it, not darkness or anything else. The inputs simply terminated, as if a wire had been cut.

The enhancement did not stop.

Below the blackout, his legs kept driving. His arms kept cutting at his sides in the pattern Margot had drilled into him. Whatever part of him knew the sprint held it at full speed. Momentum carried him toward the seventh hurdle in the same clean shape as before, and he had no way to break it.

The seventh hurdle caught him full-on.

---

Kai saw it happen from forty meters away.

At the edge of the curve, Kai stood toweling sweat off his neck. Mostly he'd been watching Tomas without much attention, just watching a young athlete do something well, appreciating the form, the economy of effort, the eagerness that had not been beaten out of him yet.

Tomas's stride did not break. That was the first wrong thing. From Kai's distance, the sequence looked almost normal for a fraction of a second, an athlete approaching a hurdle, legs driving, and then the geometry bent out of shape. Tomas's lead leg drove forward at full sprint power but did not lift. The hurdle caught him just below the knee. At that speed, close to nine meters per second, the impact was not a stumble. The crossbar bent against his shin and his momentum pitched him forward and over, his upper body rotating around the point of contact hard enough that the Dutch pole vaulter, twenty meters away, dropped her water bottle.

Tomas went over the hurdle and down. No hands came out to break the fall. The arms were still driving the sprint pattern, pistoning at his sides. The track caught his chest first. Momentum drove him along the rubber in a scraping slide, and then the torso buckled. Legs that had not received the instruction to stop kept shoving his hips forward until his upper body folded under him. The compression drove into his spine.

The sound reached Kai forty meters away. It wasn't a crack; it landed wetter than that, a dense impact that made it through the acoustic dampening as a single blunt syllable.

Tomas's legs stopped moving all at once. They froze mid-stride, left leg extended, right leg half-cocked in trail position. The rest of him continued for another half-second, momentum carrying his torso forward in a loose slide, and then he was still.

Kai was running before he understood he had started. Kai's own synthetic grafts fired his legs, the enhancement answering alarm faster than conscious thought, and for one sick instant his body was doing the same thing that had just destroyed Tomas, moving without permission. He had to fight the urge to stop his own legs, to refuse the help, to insist on running at the speed of his own fear.

Six seconds later he reached Tomas.

Tomas's face was turned to the side, his right cheek pressed into the track. Tomas's eyes were open but empty. The pupils moved without landing on anything. His mouth worked around breath with no words behind it. Kai clapped a hand against the track by his head and got no flinch, no turn, no sign that Tomas knew he was there. Six seconds was not enough. The blackout still had him.

"Don't move him," Kai said. The words were out before he knew who he was saying them to. The Dutch vaulter was there, her hands hovering over Tomas's back, and the middle-distance runner was shouting toward the medical bay corridor.

Kai counted without meaning to. Seven. Eight. Nine. Ten. Tomas stayed gone. On the twelfth second his pupils finally steadied. The next breath caught on a Spanish word Kai missed. Then Tomas said, in English that had gone small and careful, "I can't. My legs. I can't feel them."

There was blood on the track from his shin where the hurdle had caught him, and a longer smear of abrasion from his chest slide, visible through his torn training top. But Kai couldn't stop looking at the stillness below Tomas's waist, at the part of the fall that had gone wrong in a way he couldn't yet name.

Petra arrived in under two minutes, moving fast enough that the diagnostic unit knocked against her thigh. She had the portable scanner in one hand and a spinal stabilization kit under the other arm. She dropped to her knees beside Tomas with a practiced efficiency that Kai found both reassuring and horrible. This was work she knew how to do.

"Tomas, can you hear me? Don't try to move."

"I can hear you." His voice stayed quiet and exact, his English pared down to the few words he trusted. "My legs, Dr. Engel. I can't feel them."

Petra's hands moved along his spine without touching while the scanner bar swept the length of his back. Kai watched the readout from where he knelt. Most of the medical data meant nothing to him, but he understood the color. The neural bridge status indicator, which should have been green, was gray. Not red for damaged. Gray for absent.

"The bridge is gone," Petra said. She was talking to the medical staff arriving behind her, not to Tomas. Tomas heard anyway.

"What do you mean, gone?"

Petra looked at his face before she answered. "The neural bridge hardware has been destroyed. We're going to stabilize you and get you to imaging. Don't move."

Tomas lay on the track and looked at the ceiling, white acoustic tile under fluorescent strips, sixty feet up. He heard the hum of the ventilation system and the track rasping against his uninjured cheek. Both hands were flat on the floor beside his head, and feeling stopped at his waist as cleanly as if someone had drawn a line across him and said: *below this, nothing.*

At twenty-one, his legs had been the entire architecture of his life, the thing he was for, the reason Meridian paid for his family's apartment in Quito, the reason his sister could play soccer on a real pitch instead of the street, the reason his brother could fail math tests without it mattering because Tomas was going to the Apex Games and the sponsorship money would cover everything. Thirty seconds ago they had carried him over six hurdles with a grace that still made him feel as if he had been let in on a secret about what a human body could become. Now they lay silent below the waist, and every second they stayed that way widened the panic.

Signing had been the only answer he could afford. Meridian had installed the patch after he gave them everything they asked for, every biometric reading, every neural pattern, every genetic marker. The fix had not fixed this. The virus had taken him mid-stride and thrown him into a hurdle at nine meters per second, and when he told his legs to move, nothing answered.

*Cuídate, mi amor.*

No tears came. He opened his mouth and closed it and opened it again, and what came out was not Spanish or English, not language at all, just the noise a body makes when it thinks the thing it was built for has been taken from it.

---

Margot had felt everything.

She was in the coaching suite, fifteen meters from the track entrance, her feed interface strapped over her pulse point, the neural connection open and streaming. Tomas's sensory world arrived at her nervous system with a half-second delay. It was enough lag that she could not react in real time, not enough that the experience felt secondhand. She was inside his effort. Tomas's hamstrings loaded on the approach, and she felt that loading as phantom tension in her own legs, a ghost of athletic power her body had not produced in years. The numbers climbed, 168, 172, 176, and she'd pushed them there, nudging his pacing targets upward through the coaching interface because the intensity was good, the rush was clean, his cardiovascular output at 178 producing a cascade of sensation through the feed that lit her up in places her own flat, unplugged nervous system could not reach.

She knew what she was doing. She always knew. The knowing had never been enough to stop.

The sixth hurdle looked beautiful through the feed, the explosion at the bar, his body airborne for a fraction of a second, the landing shock traveling up through his legs and into her borrowed proprioception. She pushed the pacing target again, a small nudge. The display hit 182. Cardiovascular strain ran hot through him, blood pressure elevated, the vessels in his spine and legs under more strain than training range called for. She registered it like an addict registers the third drink: noted, not acted on, already reaching for the fourth.

Then the feed went blank.

It cut cleanly, as if someone had lifted a cable. One moment she was inside his effort, the rubber under his feet, the air in his lungs, the rhythmic pulse of his heart at 182, and the next she was alone in her own body, her own unstimulated nervous system, the coaching suite's overhead light flat on her skin. The feed display on the wall showed all channels zeroed: heart rate still transmitting, 184 and climbing, neural input at zero, sensory relay at zero, motor pattern still active. His body was still doing work at full output with no sensory data to guide it.

She should have disconnected. The protocol for feed disruption was immediate severance of the coaching link. She knew this. Her hand moved toward the clasp.

She did not disconnect.

Some channels were coming back. Tomas's senses stayed dark. But the motor data was streaming, and through the coaching interface's relay she could feel his body driving at sprint speed, closing on the hurdle, then taking the impact.

She felt the impact.

Through the feed, at full sensory resolution, her nervous system received the translation of a human body hitting a hurdle at nine meters per second and going down. The shin strike came first, a sharp crack of pain radiating up through her left leg as if she had taken the bar herself. Then the chest impact, the slide, the scraping abrasion that came through as a hot strip across her sternum and ribs. Then the compression.

The spinal compression arrived as a dense total sensation that Margot had no analogy for. It felt like a closing, a compaction of the nerve pathways that carried information between brain and legs, translated through the interface into the feeling of her own lower body folding inward until the space her legs occupied in her proprioceptive map narrowed into a line and then into a point and then disappeared.

Then the channel went dead. Pain vanished with it. That was the worst part, worse than the impact, worse than the compression: the feed channel that had been streaming Tomas's lower-body sensation went flat, and Margot's nervous system received that flatness as a phantom paralysis settling over her own legs like a cold sheet.

She yanked the interface off. The disconnect was brutal, the feed severing mid-stream, the sudden lurch from Tomas's ruined sensory world back into her own. She screamed. The sound came out before she decided to make it, a raw noise with no words in it, the vocal equivalent of ripping a plug from a wall.

She was on the floor of the coaching suite. She did not remember falling. Her legs were fine. She could feel them, move them, press her palms against her thighs and feel the muscle and bone underneath, whole, intact, her own. But the phantom was still there. A layer under the real sensation, a ghost of paralysis the feed had written into her nervous system and her body had not yet overwritten. Her legs worked and they also felt dead. Both things were true at once, and she sat on the floor with her hands clamped on her thighs and her breath coming in short wet sounds that were not quite sobs.

The interface lay beside her, the display still showing Tomas's vitals: heart rate 171 and dropping, neural bridge status gray.

She had him at 182 when the blackout hit. Training range for hurdle work was 155 to 165. She had pushed him twenty beats above threshold because the rush was better at 180, because his cardiovascular effort at maximum output produced the exact flavor of borrowed intensity she could not get anywhere else. When the virus struck and his body crashed and his spine compressed, the elevated blood pressure had been there under the impact. The stressed vessels had been there. She did not know if 160 would have left him with a bridge. She knew she had made sure it was not 160.

She sat on the floor of the coaching suite and looked at her hands, shaking in her lap. Her hands had adjusted the pacing target. Her hands had pushed the slider upward because the feedback at 182 felt better than the feedback at 165. Her hands were still attached to her body, still working, still capable of feeling the cold tile under her and the sweat drying on her forearms while Tomas's legs were gone and she had been inside them when they went.

---

Three hours later, Kai went to the calibration bay.

The alternative was standing in the medical bay corridor outside imaging, and he could not do that anymore. Ninety minutes in the corridor had been enough, his wrist aching, his shoulder aching, his body remembering February with a precision that felt less like memory and more like rehearsal. Meridian's fix had not saved Tomas.

From the corridor he'd heard Tomas once, not words, just the raw animal sound that had come out of him on the track after Petra named the bridge. It kept arriving in Kai's body a second late, as if the building were still throwing it back at him.

The calibration bay looked wrong this empty. Every rig was awake, straps hanging loose, shells open for bodies that weren't there.

The rig nearest the wall was still in emergency audit mode. Post-incident protocol had kicked every station that had shared a mesh link with Tomas's session into read-only trace capture and frozen the raw routing until medical signed it off. An orange banner stretched across the screen: `POST-INCIDENT TRAFFIC REVIEW`. In ordinary athlete view Kai would have seen green status tiles and a one-line health summary. Orange mode peeled that away. Every outbound route sat under the session log until someone cleared it.

He dropped into the chair and pulled up his own logs. Neural bridge status, locomotor performance, sensory integration metrics: all green, all normal. His body continued to function inside parameters that predicted nothing.

No one had cleared this one. Petra was still in imaging with Tomas. The junior tech who'd helped carry the stabilization board had followed her with the portable scanner, leaving the bay collecting evidence nobody had time to read. Kai almost left the screen alone. Then he saw his own name in the session list.

He had no business being in an orange screen. Petra would have said that. Yusuf would have said it more gently. But the reader on the rig flashed green under his wristband because the system still recognized him as the owner of the sessions, and once the file tree opened the line between looking and knowing was only a scroll.

With the band against the reader, only his traffic opened. At first it looked like crash triage: facility server, Meridian sync, firmware verification, packet sizes too small to care about. One line farther down sat a destination with no server name at all, just a red triangle and a device class: `REMOVABLE STORAGE / NON-FACILITY ASSET`. He thought Petra's team might have docked a scanner there. Then he opened the manifest. Neural bridge telemetry. Proprioception logs. Lower-body performance data. Genetic baseline readings. Full raw dumps, gigabytes at a time. Not just today's crash, either. The timestamps rolled backward into February, into ordinary calibrations, shoulder-rehab mornings when he'd closed his eyes in this chair and listened to Yusuf tell him to relax his jaw. Every transfer carried the same operator signature: `qadir_y`. Every destination carried the same unregistered local device.

One February entry carried the note `POST-CRASH SHOULDER RANGE / WRIST LOAD TEST`. Kai remembered that session because Yusuf had made him squeeze foam around the healing wrist until he swore at him. The routing showed the pattern in sequence: facility server, Meridian sync, then the same session ID copied again sixteen seconds later to the local device. Not forwarded. Duplicated. That was the moment suspicion stopped being suspicion.

Ordinary athlete reports boiled routing down into clean checkmarks and no addresses. Only the forensic trace left the routes intact. Petra's team hadn't already flagged it because the capture was pulling everything that had touched Tomas's network path, and nobody handling an acute spinal injury was paging through Kai's February calibrations while Tomas was still in imaging. Kai looked at the device class, the session dates, the file labels that were obviously his body, and knew it wasn't crash traffic.

He checked one more file because one file could still be a glitch. The same pattern came back: the same second copy, the same device. The theft was patient, boring, procedural. It wasn't panic after Tomas or some opportunistic sweep during the crash. Yusuf had built it into ordinary days.

"Yusuf," Kai said to the empty room. Then he stood up.

Yusuf was in the back office, a small glass-walled room overlooking the main calibration floor. He sat at his desk with his glasses pushed up on his forehead and a cup of tea going cold at his elbow. Three monitors. One showed Tomas's crash diagnostic, the neural bridge failure cascade laid out in color bands Kai could not read. The other two showed code comparisons, side-by-side columns with sections highlighted in yellow.

"Yusuf." Kai stopped in the doorway. "I found the transfer history."

Yusuf looked up. A brief flicker crossed his face: professional polish rising and then failing, Birmingham showing through underneath like bone through skin.

"Right," Yusuf said. "Yeah."

"You've been copying my data. During calibration. February, March, all of it. My biometric files, neural logs, performance data. Everything. To a device that isn't in the system."

"That same rehab file. Same endpoint every time." Kai heard his own voice go low. "You were backing me up to something you carried in yourself."

"Yeah."

"How long?"

"Since February." Yusuf's hands were on the desk, his engineer's hands, usually steady and precise. They were not steady now. "Since I got here. Before the virus, Kai. I was doing it before any of this."

The dates kept flashing in Kai harder than the code on Yusuf's monitors. February. March. All those afternoons he'd closed his eyes because calibration went faster when he stopped watching.

Kai stepped into the office. The sliding door stuck half-open behind him as usual. He did not sit. He looked at the monitors. The code on the right meant nothing to him, but the highlighted sections repeated in visible patterns.

"Tell me what I'm looking at."

"Sit down."

"I'm fine standing."

Yusuf took his glasses off his forehead and put them on. Kai had watched him make that same gesture a hundred times before calibration work, the small adjustment that meant technical mode, but now his hands shook against the frames and the whole thing looked like costume.

"Farah's getting worse," Yusuf said. "You know that. The neural data from the athletes — right, the integration telemetry, it maps onto pathways that are relevant to her condition. Not directly, but it's the most detailed neural-interface data in the world and Meridian was about to lock all of it behind their —"

"So you stole from us."

"Off the rig to my own drive, yeah. Raw sessions, all of them." He was talking faster now. "Meridian was going to seal that archive behind the contract and Farah hasn't got — she hasn't got time for me to go through proper channels, Kai. She hasn't got time."

Kai looked at the cold cup of tea. A skin had formed on top, thin and milky. The office smelled like old tea and cable insulation and the metallic ozone of overworked processors.

"Tomas is paralyzed," Kai said.

"I know."

"He signed. He took their fix. He still ended up on Petra's table."

"I know." Yusuf turned one screen farther toward him. "Sit down. Please."

Kai sat. The office chair was a cheap swivel model with one torn armrest, and it creaked under his weight.

"I've been in the virus code since February," Yusuf said. "Trying to work out where it came from. Right, so —" He tapped the left screen. "That column. That's the propagation core, the bit that talks to the calibration network." He tapped the right. "And this is Meridian's patch framework. Their standard architecture."

Kai leaned in. He could read one thing without understanding the rest: the same header on both screens, highlighted in yellow. `MERIDIAN ATHLETE FIRMWARE / INTERNAL`. Under it sat the same certificate ID.

"You don't need to read code," Yusuf said. "Look at the package calls. Same package. The signed fix uses it to check back with their servers, and the virus calls the same — it's the same bloody package, Kai. It came out of their development environment. It's their code, or it was built with their tools, I can't — I don't have an email saying go ahead. But this wasn't built outside."

"Meaning Meridian built it."

"Meaning somebody inside did, yeah." He was rubbing the back of his neck. "Could be one team with access. Could be policy. I don't know how high it goes."

The office seemed to shrink around the two monitors.

"Tomas signed their contract," Kai said. "He gave them everything they wanted. His family's depending on that money and he still —" He looked at the cold tea. "He's twenty-one."

"The patch doesn't fix it," Yusuf said. "It suppresses. And the suppression keeps phoning home to Meridian's servers for authorization, so if they pull that — if they change the terms, you take the new terms, because otherwise your firmware goes back to being compromised. The whole thing only works as long as they keep letting it work."

"You can prove the server part. Not the rest."

"I can show the dependency." Yusuf rubbed his face. "I can show you the structural match. I can't prove someone sat in a room and said do this."

Kai looked at the monitors. He could read the label, the certificate ID, the fact that the same thing sat in both columns. The rest of the code still blurred together, and Yusuf knew exactly where his understanding ended.

"You lied to me," Kai said. "Every session. I'd lie back, close my eyes, and you'd copy my data to your private drive without saying a word."

"Yeah."

"You built an alternative patch that kills Margot's feed access, and you never told anyone your real stake in it was your sister's data."

"I told the group about Farah."

"After you'd already built it. After Lena had already said yes. You told us when it couldn't change the decision you'd pushed us toward."

Yusuf rubbed his eyes with the heel of his hand. The motion was so ordinary it threw Kai off for a second. Yusuf looked wrung out. Just a man in a glass office with cold tea at his elbow and his hands shaking on the desk.

"Tomas's neural bridge is gone," Yusuf said. Quieter now. "The virus hit his sensory firmware, same as yours. The motor system kept running. He hit the hurdle at full speed and the compression —" He stopped. Put his hand flat on the desk. "The bridge hardware's destroyed. The support hardware kept his cord from severing, but without the bridge there's no pathway to his legs. The cord was already — it was degraded from the integration. The bridge was doing that work. And now it's not."

"Is it permanent?"

"They'd have to replace the bridge. That's a full integration procedure, months of rehab, regrowth from scratch." He looked down at the desk, blinked once, hard. "He might walk. He's not going to run."

Kai stood up. The chair rolled backward and bumped the wall.

"You should've told me," he said. "All of it. From the start."

"If I'd told you about the archive, you'd have reported me. The data would've been locked down and Farah —"

"Don't." Kai didn't raise his voice. "Don't make this about Farah right now. A kid is paralyzed and you've been sitting on this." He gestured at the monitors. "All of it."

"I didn't have enough to —"

"Enough for what?" Kai looked at him. "He's down the hall right now finding out he can't walk. What were you waiting for?"

Yusuf did not answer. He kept looking at the monitors, at the yellow blocks lined up beside each other, as if staring hard enough might make them settle into proof. Both hands were on the desk. They were shaking.

"What do you want to do?" Yusuf asked.

Kai looked through the glass wall at the calibration bay beyond, the rigs where athletes lay down and trusted the system to maintain them, the ceiling lights, the processors still running. Three corridors away, Tomas was on an imaging table learning the dimensions of his new life. One floor above, Margot was sitting with the phantom of his paralysis still alive in her legs. Outside, the spring thaw was pulling the last of the winter off the mountains and the meltwater kept running downhill.

"I don't know," Kai said.

Kai left the office with the sliding door stuck half-open behind him. Yusuf's monitor light fell across the calibration bay floor in yellow bars. The rigs sat under the overhead lights with their straps loose, ready for the next athlete to lie down and hand the room his body. A few weeks ago Kai would have done it without thinking. Now the whole place looked rotten.


# Chapter 15

Margot's legs were fine.

In the grey light before the sun cleared the eastern ridge, she sat on the edge of her bed with both feet flat on the cold tile, and her legs were fine. She felt the chill through her soles, the mattress edge against the backs of her thighs, the small adjustments in her calves that kept her upright. Everything worked. It had always worked.

Under the tile and the mattress and the ordinary fact of a body sitting on a bed at six in the morning was Tomas's phantom. Through the feed she had felt his neural bridge break. Full-resolution sensation came down the bidirectional path between her nervous system and his: compression in the lower spine, signals running through his legs, then nothing at all. The stop at the waist had been clean and dead.

On the third step the phantom hit, a skip in the signal, a gap as if her left leg's proprioception blanked for one fraction of a second. Her hand shot to the desk. The gap closed. Another step. Fine. Another. Fine. On the seventh step the right leg vanished the same way and her weight pitched sideways until the wall caught her.

She stayed there until the wave passed. Tomas was in the medical bay paralyzed from the waist down, and she was standing in her own room waiting for borrowed deadness to drain out of working legs.

The feed interface sat on the nightstand where she'd dropped it. Her hand closed around it before she'd decided to move. The sensor pad fit her palm. At once the inside of her right wrist went alert, skin remembering the pressure of the clasp. Her body issued the old sequence: fasten it, wait for the handshake, get out of here. Tomas was in the medical bay unable to feel his legs, and her body still knew the sequence. A scratch crossed the face of the device from a doorframe in Stuttgart, three years ago. The indicator light stayed dark.

In the bathroom she turned on the shower and waited through thirty seconds of cold pipe before the heat reached the coaches' wing. Usually the shower marked the end of a feed. Steam, hot water, the brute fact of her own skin: that was how she burned someone else's body out of hers. Today the pale strap mark on her wrist showed bright against skin gone red from the water. Without the interface there, the bare band looked wrong.

Under the fluorescent strip above the mirror waited her own face, puffy with bad sleep, jaw set hard enough to show a pulse at the hinge. The skin beneath her eyes looked bruised. She dried off and still felt unclean.

Back at the bed, the interface ended up in her hands again. She dropped it into the right pocket of her training pants and pulled them on.

She dressed without looking at the wall display. Grey sweater. Socks. The ordinary uniform of a coach whose day was supposed to start in the suite with athlete schedules and performance notes. When she finally glanced up, the room had cycled to the facility bulletin. VISITING RESTRICTIONS: MEDICAL WING - AUTHORIZED STAFF AND DESIGNATED ATHLETE CONTACTS ONLY. Under it, weather. Under that, breakfast hours. The list of food made her stomach turn, not from nausea exactly but from the stupid persistence of appetite in a world where Tomas Reyes had woken up without legs.

She left the room anyway and walked the corridor toward the medical wing before she'd decided what she was doing. The building smelled of cleaning solution, wet concrete, coffee from the cafeteria below. Night staff were crossing with day staff at the junctions, clipped greetings, badges catching the thin light. Nobody stopped her. Nobody had the authority yet.

Tomas's recovery door stood at the far end of the corridor under a privacy strip that turned the glass panel opaque. A portable monitor sat just inside the threshold, green bars moving across it too far away to read. There was no sound except the air system and, once, the short motorized whine of a bed adjusting position.

She stopped two steps short of the door.

Yesterday she had been in his body when the hurdle came wrong, in his spine when the impact telescoped everything below the waist into pressure and then absence. Now there was a sheet of institutional paper over the window and a sealed door and whatever version of waking he was doing on the other side of it. Knock and say what? I wanted the cleanest version of your legs. I kept taking it. I pushed until the signal got sharp and then I kept pushing.

The phantom took her left leg again. She caught the wall with one palm and waited through the blank fraction while the fluorescent lights buzzed over her head. By the time the floor came back, a nurse in navy scrubs had turned into the corridor from imaging with a tablet tucked against her chest. Margot straightened before the nurse could reach her and walked away without looking up.

She made it as far as the cafeteria after that because bodies still moved on schedule even when everything else had split. Trays knocked against rails. Coffee steamed in thick brown urns. Someone at the back table laughed too hard at a joke that wasn't funny enough, the forced volume of people trying to make ordinary noise in the wrong morning. Tomas's usual chair by the window sat empty under a stripe of pale light.

Margot took coffee because her hands needed something to do. The paper cup burned her fingers. The first swallow tasted of metal and old filter paper. She looked down the length of the room and found athletes looking away one by one with the quickness of people who had already heard one version of the story and didn't yet know whether pity or disgust was the correct response. A junior cyclist's eyes dropped to the strap mark on her wrist and snapped off it again.

She left the cup half full beside the condiment station. By the time she hit the stairs the wanting had already outrun the caffeine. Her palm kept checking for the weight of the dead interface in her pocket as if she might somehow have lost it in the two minutes she'd spent pretending coffee and fluorescent light still belonged to the same world as breakfast.

Back in her room she opened the cached telemetry from yesterday's session, the last one the interface would ever record if Petra did what Petra should do. Twelve beats per minute. Tomas's heart rate had been running twelve beats above target when the blackout hit. She'd put it there. She'd pushed his pacing parameters because the feed sharpened above the safety margin, because his body's exertion at that intensity made the borrowed sensation so clean her own body dropped out, because that disappearance was the thing she kept coming back for.

She opened older files. Tomas two days earlier. Lena last week. Kim on Tuesday. Annika before that. In every overlay the green prescribed zone sat underneath the red trace by exactly the amount she'd learned for that athlete. Enough to sharpen. Not enough to trip the hard-stop alerts. She had spent three years teaching herself to read that gap as craft. Now it looked like handwriting.

Twelve beats per minute. It meant elevated cardiovascular load during a virus-triggered blackout, higher blood pressure, more stress through the tissue when he hit. She didn't know whether those twelve beats made the difference between a recoverable injury and a permanent one. A biomechanics team could give her a model, a confidence interval, a distribution of outcomes. None of it would answer the only question that mattered. Tomas Reyes was twenty-one years old and couldn't feel his legs.

Spring light cut farther into the room than it had a month ago, reaching across the desk and touching the far wall. On the south-facing slopes outside, snow had retreated above the tree line. Brown earth below, mud and scree, the skeleton of the landscape exposed after months of burial. Runoff moved through the drainage channels along the foundation in a continuous murmur.

No tears came. Crying would have been about her own distress, and her distress was not the thing in the medical bay behind the closed door.

The phantom caught her right leg again. This time she stayed sitting and let it pass.

---

At nine Petra sent for her. A blue rectangle appeared on the wall display in her quarters with Dr. Engel's name, a room number, and a time thirty-two minutes ahead. Beneath it the display listed Margot's own biometrics: resting heart rate, sleep quality index, enhancement interface status. The interface status read INACTIVE. For three years it had read ACTIVE.

Four minutes took her through the connecting corridor and past windows that faced the south slope, where snowmelt had turned the access road to mud. A middle-distance runner and a gymnast came the other way. Neither looked at her. Maybe they hadn't heard yet. Maybe they had and were choosing not to look. Either option settled the same in her stomach.

At the junction between the residential wing and the medical wing, her left leg disappeared for that fraction of a second again. The wall caught her palm. A maintenance worker at the far end of the corridor glanced over and then looked away.

Petra's office was glass-walled and adjacent to the medical bay proper. Past Petra's desk, past the interior window on the far wall, ran the corridor to the recovery rooms. Tomas's door was closed. It had been closed since they brought him in yesterday.

Petra was standing when Margot entered. That was wrong. Petra was a deliberate sitter. She positioned herself at eye level, a clinical habit that implied equality even when the power was entirely hers. Standing meant she hadn't settled, or hadn't been able to.

"Close the door."

Margot closed it.

Two screens on Petra's desk held data Margot recognized at once: feed session logs. The format her interface generated automatically every time she connected to an athlete. Heart-rate tracking, respiratory rate, neural feedback markers, session duration, connection quality. Weeks of sessions, laid out as time series.

"Sit down," Petra said.

The chair was standard medical-office issue, padded and adjustable, built for patients receiving news. Margot had sat across from Petra in it twice before for routine coaching certifications. Those meetings had lasted fifteen minutes. This one would not.

Petra stayed standing long enough to brace one hand on the desk. "Yesterday's spinal injury triggered a catastrophic-incident audit. Tomas lost motor function during a virus event, so I asked IT to open cross-system review. Yusuf needed the full pathway map for the forty-eight hours before the crash. I wanted every network, every session, every route into his system."

Margot said nothing.

"Under routine operations medical doesn't receive raw coach-side telemetry," Petra said. "We get your session plans, any variance forms you submit, and automatic alerts when an athlete crosses a hard safety threshold. The live overlays stay in the performance suite unless a coach asks for medical review or I open an audit."

Petra tapped the left screen. "Your athletes stayed below the hard-stop alarms. Above prescription, yes. Above the ceiling, no. So the pattern sat in another system and read as ordinary coaching data unless someone laid the raw trace over the prescribed zone and looked for chronic drift."

She tapped again, harder. "After Kai Brennan's crash we were tracing calibration corruption and blackout events. We were looking for the virus moving through shared calibration. We were not auditing six weeks of coach load decisions inside a firewalled performance feed. Tomas's paralysis changed the scope. The audit changed the access."

The data was already on the screen when Petra turned it toward her. "Your feed session logs with Tomas Reyes. February eighth through yesterday. Thirty-one sessions."

Each session sat on its own track. The target heart-rate zone Margot had filed in her session plans showed in green. The actual heart rate showed in red. The red lines ran above the green lines in every session.

"Average deviation of twelve beats per minute above the prescribed training zone," Petra said. She sounded like someone reading findings. Her gaze stayed fixed on Margot. "The pattern is consistent. Same gap, session after session, across the full six-week period."

"Yes," Margot said.

Petra waited. When nothing else came, she scrolled to a second dataset. Different athlete. Same pattern. Target below, actual above, the gap steady and systematic.

"Lena Morozova. Twenty-three sessions. Average deviation of eight beats per minute." Another scroll. "David Kim. Fourteen sessions. Average deviation of fifteen beats." Another. "Annika Brandt. Nine sessions. Eleven beats."

Four athletes. Weeks of sessions. Systematic elevation. No documented medical justification for any of it.

"I'm not going to ask you why," Petra said. "The telemetry is clear enough."

On the screen, clean colored lines showed the gap between what Margot had promised and what she'd done. The logs had always been there. They generated automatically. She'd never deleted them. Hadn't even considered deleting them. The real concealment had been procedural: keeping the damage in the strip of space between a target zone and a hard-stop alarm, trusting that nobody outside the coaching suite would bother to open the right overlay until somebody broke badly enough to force them to.

"I've also reviewed the network pathway data with Yusuf," Petra said. Her voice lost some of its clinical remove. "The neural coaching feed creates a bidirectional data channel. When you connect to an athlete's system, the pathway opens in both directions."

Margot watched her own hands stay still in her lap.

"Every feed session you ran opened an additional propagation route for the virus through the shared calibration network." Petra wasn't hedging now. The usual qualifiers were gone. "Yusuf has confirmed that the feed pathway is a viable transmission vector. The virus could propagate through a coaching connection the same way it propagates through calibration."

"I know," Margot said.

Petra was quiet for a beat. "When."

"February. After the facility went to restricted operations. After Kai Brennan's crash."

Petra's hand went to her glasses, adjusted them, returned to the desk. "You identified the feed as a transmission risk six weeks ago. And you continued running sessions through yesterday."

"Every one of them."

The silence that followed belonged to a doctor who had heard the answer she expected and was now recalculating the scope of the damage. Petra sat down slowly, as if the act required thought. Her chair creaked. She took off her glasses and cleaned them on the hem of her sweater, a gesture Margot knew well, one Petra used when the rest of her needed to hold still.

"Thirty-one sessions with Tomas. Twenty-three with Lena. Fourteen with Kim. Nine with Brandt. Seventy-seven potential virus exposures beyond the shared calibration network." Petra put the glasses back on. "Seventy-seven sessions where you knew you might be increasing viral load on athletes whose systems were already compromised."

There was nothing to say. The number said it.

"Your coaching privileges are suspended effective immediately," Petra said. "Your feed interface authorization is revoked at the network level. IT will deactivate your connection credentials by end of day. You're not to connect to any athlete's neural system for any purpose. Can you confirm that verbally?"

"I understand. I won't connect."

"You'll retain access to your quarters and common areas. You're not to enter the coaching suite, the calibration bay, or any training environment without my explicit authorization."

"Okay."

Petra did not move on. "By seventeen hundred I want a written accounting of every feed session where the chart and the raw telemetry tell different stories. Any anomaly you normalized. Any symptom you minimized. Any athlete you put above prescription and then documented as acceptable."

"All right."

Petra looked through the layered glass at Tomas's closed recovery-room door. Margot looked too. It was the same institutional white as every other door in the wing and the only one shut in the middle of a weekday morning.

"The heart-rate elevation during yesterday's session," Petra said. Her voice was quieter now. "His cardiovascular load was elevated when the blackout triggered. I've asked the biomechanics team to model whether the elevation contributed to the severity of the spinal outcome."

"It might've," Margot said.

"It might have. Yes." For an instant Petra's face contracted around the eyes before control returned. "I flagged a rejection risk at the Stuttgart facility four years ago. Marius Koch. Decathlete. Twenty-four. I wrote the report and filed it through proper channels. Meridian's medical oversight board reviewed my findings and downgraded the risk classification. He died eight months later from the condition I'd identified."

Margot said nothing.

"I'm not comparing what you did to what they did. The mechanisms are different." Petra stopped and started the sentence again on a different track. "I've spent four years knowing I should've made more noise. Should've refused to let the institution's process overrule my clinical judgment. That's what I'm doing now."

"I know what you're doing," Margot said.

Petra nodded once. Shadows sat under her eyes. Blue ink marked the side of one finger. She looked like someone who'd been awake too long and knew she'd be awake longer.

"You should go," Petra said.

When Margot stood, the phantom erased her left leg for half a beat and her fingertips caught Petra's desk.

Petra's eyes went to the catch, the recovery, the hand on the desk. "What was that?"

"Phantom. From the feed. From yesterday. It'll pass."

"When did it start?"

"When I disconnected from Tomas."

Petra went still. "You're carrying phantom sensation from his injury?"

"His legs going dead. It comes in waves. My nervous system stored it."

"I can order a neural scan. If the phantom is still present tonight, you report to medical. That isn't optional."

Margot almost said no on reflex. The word got as far as her teeth and stopped. "Okay."

Back in the corridor, another wave took her right leg near the junction with the residential wing. She stopped with one hand on the wall until it cleared, then kept walking.

---

Kai found her outside.

The facility's service entrance opened on the north side, onto a loading dock with a rusted safety rail and a clear view of the valley road. From there the switchbacks were visible all the way down through the tree line to where the road leveled toward Meiringen. It was the one place on campus where a person could stand outside and not see any part of the building. Margot sat on an overturned equipment crate near the rail without a jacket, her breath still showing faintly in the cold.

He'd spent most of the morning with Yusuf in the calibration bay, in front of the screens and the code comparisons and the highlighted sections that might prove Meridian wrote it if proof were a thing any of them actually had. Structural signatures in the virus matched Meridian's proprietary development frameworks well enough to convince an engineer and not well enough to survive a legal challenge or a press conference. Suggestive, Yusuf kept saying. Five weeks to the opening ceremony, a paralyzed kid in the medical bay, and a corporate entity that had sold the cure for a disease it had probably made.

Lena had found him when he came out of the bay to rinse the coffee taste out of his mouth. Her hair was still damp at the ends from the pool. Chlorine sat sharp on her sweatshirt.

"Petra suspended Margot," she'd said.

"For what?"

"The feed logs." Lena had held his gaze the way she did when precision mattered. "The raw telemetry. Not the summaries. Tomas. Me. Other athletes. My heart rate was above the prescribed zone in every session Petra reviewed."

"How many sessions?"

"Twenty-three with me. Thirty-one with Tomas." She had looked past him then, toward the window over the stairwell where meltwater streaked the glass. "Petra asked if there were incidents I did not report because I trusted the chart more than what I remembered. I said yes."

He had asked how she was before he could decide whether the question was useful.

Lena's mouth had tightened as if she were testing a word for fit. "I don't know yet," she said. "It's still becoming specific."

That was all she gave him. He knew better than to push harder while she was still arranging the facts into terms her body could live beside. He left the question where it was and went to find Margot.

When she heard him, she looked up. Her face had gone flat. The humor she usually carried like a blade, the sharp self-aware darkness that made people uncomfortable when they realized she wasn't entirely joking, was gone. What remained was stripped down and ugly.

"Lena," he said.

"Yeah."

He'd had the words organized on the walk over, accusation first, then the questions, then whatever came after. Standing three feet from her crate in the cold, the sequence fell apart.

"Petra pulled the feed logs. The heart-rate data."

"I know what's in them."

He stayed standing. Standing over her would have been wrong, but sitting next to her would have been worse. So he took the space between those two bad options and stopped at the edge of the dock, close enough to talk and far enough not to share anything.

"She lost two laps," he said. "In the pool. She was in the water and she wasn't there."

"I was in the feed when it happened-"

"Don't tell me what it felt like."

Her mouth twitched once and went flat. "Fine."

"Tell me what you did."

She swallowed once, hard enough to shift her whole throat. "I ran her hot. Above the prescribed zone, every session. The feed sharpened when her load went up, and the desync made it worse. I told her the numbers were fine. They weren't."

"Did you know she was worse than the charts?"

"Yes."

"Since when?"

"By February." Her eyes went past him to the road and came back. "No. Earlier than that I knew it was wrong. February was when I stopped being able to call it noise."

Below them, a truck engine moved along the valley road and vanished around a switchback. Across the valley the mountains were still mostly white, but the snow had pulled off the rock faces and left the stone dark and wet in the midday sun.

"So you kept her running hot anyway."

"Yeah."

"And Tomas."

"Yeah."

He watched her breathing stay shallow in the cold. "Tell me what the feed is. Not the technical version."

"There isn't another version."

"Try."

She licked dry lips once. "You connect and their signals come through. Legs, lungs, water, impact, all of it. When the load goes up, it gets cleaner."

"Cleaner."

"Sharper." Her breath snagged and came out white. "More of them. Less of me."

He said nothing.

"At a certain point I stop noticing my own body." She dug her fingers into the rough crate edge until the wood bit her palms. "The room thins out. My skin goes dead. Their effort doesn't. That's the part I wanted."

"You needed it that bad."

"Yes."

"And you just — kept taking it."

"Yes."

"Three years."

"I started in Stuttgart. Before I came here. First time was an accident. I set a calibration wrong, the athlete's heart rate spiked, the feed got-" She cut herself off hard enough to show teeth. "It felt good. I did it again on purpose. Then again. I learned the numbers."

"Everybody has a number."

"Not exact. A range." She looked at him squarely. "Tomas was twelve above target."

He pressed his wrist against the cold safety rail until the metal bit into bone. The pain was clean. It helped.

"When I stopped competing, the wanting didn't stop with me," Margot said. "My body did. The wanting didn't. The feed was the closest thing. Someone else's body doing what mine couldn't. After a session the room came back wrong. Flat. Food tasted dead. Water felt like nothing. I'd spend the day waiting for the next one."

He thought of Lena at the pool wall with water running off her shoulders, asking what had happened to the last two laps. Margot had called it acceptable.

"What did you write when she lost the laps?"

Margot looked at him without blinking. "I wrote transient rhythm loss. Acceptable. It should've been a Class III event and a medical stop."

"Did you tell her that?"

"No."

Wind moved through the loading dock hard enough to rattle the loose chain on the side gate. She didn't flinch.

"Why not stop?" he said. "Not in general. Why not stop when it was Lena. When you knew what was happening to her."

Margot's jaw worked once. "Because by then I was already building my days around it. Because I could tell myself I had rules and that was bullshit. Because stopping meant being in my own body full time." She looked down at the pale strap mark without touching it. "My own body wasn't enough for me."

He let that sit between them. There wasn't a cleaner version under it. That was the worst part.

"Petra asked for a statement by five," Margot said after a while. "All the sessions where the chart and the telemetry don't match."

"Put Lena in it."

"I was going to."

"Put the missing laps in it. The days you wrote acceptable when they weren't. Every time you let the chart lie for you."

Her face tightened into a wince and flattened again. "Okay."

"And you stay away from her unless she asks for you."

"Okay."

The apology came after that, too late to redirect anything, too plain to be strategy.

"I'm sorry," Margot said. "About Lena. About Tomas. About all of it."

"Don't give that to me," Kai said. His voice had gone quiet enough that he barely recognized it as his. "That's not — I can't take that for them."

She nodded once. "All right."

He stood. The backs of his thighs were cold. The mountains stayed where they were, patient and indifferent.

He went back inside. Behind him, Margot stayed on the crate in the cold. He didn't look back to see whether she was still sitting there or whether Tomas's phantom had taken her leg again. The loading-dock door shut behind him. By five Petra would have it all in writing, every session where the chart and the body told different stories. Tomas's door would still be closed.


# Chapter 16

By late June the shuttle to Zurich left twice a week. On the Tuesday the relay group went, Kai came in off the indoor track with his shirt stuck to his back and stopped by the front windows while Ursula stacked breakfast cups behind him. A Meridian coordinator in a navy jacket checked names against a tablet. Four relay runners climbed aboard with matching duffel bags and the same new corporate luggage tag looped through each handle. Then the Czech sprinter went, then the two South Korean gymnasts. The shuttle door folded shut. Somebody laughed on the other side of the glass. Then it pulled down the switchback road and was gone.

He cut through the residential wing on his way to the showers. Half the room displays were dark now. The ones still on floated pulse traces and battery diagnostics over empty beds. Most of the institute had made its bargain. By then the holdouts were a handful spread between Kirchberg and Davos: Kai still running nothing, Lena and two others on Yusuf's patch, everyone else signed or already gone. Choices had stopped sounding like arguments. They were just departures.

That morning he'd passed two of the patched holdouts coming back from final diagnostics: a weightlifter from Sarajevo and the Bergen cyclist whose name he always forgot. Both had fresh adhesive shadows at the base of the skull. Both were moving with the careful fraction of delay he'd started to recognize in Lena, not weakness exactly, just people doing their own timing by hand again. Each carried a printed travel packet because their systems no longer synced cleanly with the institute calendar. From ten metres away you could tell who had taken whose version of safety.

The patched ones looked less sponsored now and more like patients carrying instructions.

That afternoon he found Margot at the bottle filler by the side entrance, bent over from the gravel loop below the treeline with one hand braced on the wall. Sweat had soaked the back of her grey sweatshirt. Dust had dried pale on her calves above her socks. Margot drank until the sensor cut off, hit it again, drank more. When she straightened, two fingers went to the pale strap mark the feed interface had left on her wrist. She pressed there once, hard, then seemed to feel herself doing it and shifted her grip to the steel edge of the filler instead, knuckles whitening against the metal.

"You all right?" he asked.

"Not especially." Her voice stayed flat. "Running helps."

"With what?"

Margot looked past him, out through the glass to the path she'd just come up. "Everything else feels like cardboard." The hand on the steel flexed once. "The lungs at least still know how to complain."

Kai nodded.

Margot looked at him properly then, bloodshot-eyed from the climb and something else. "If anything feels wrong in Doha, stop. Don't do the brave thing for the cameras."

He frowned. "You telling me not to race?"

"I'm telling you they clap for stupid." She let go of the metal. "That's different."

Then she pushed off the wall and went back inside.

The night before the team bus left for the Zurich airport, Yusuf made biryani.

---

His mother had sent a care package in March, a padded envelope battered from Birmingham: two bags of basmati, a jar of her own garam masala, saffron threads in a twist of cling film, a bag of fried onions gone slightly soft at the edges, and a note in Urdu that said *Eat properly.* He'd been saving it. The biryani was a production. He'd negotiated use of the cafeteria kitchen from the night cook, a woman named Ursula, after she'd finished labeling boxed breakfasts for the airport bus with room numbers and diet codes. She watched him toast whole spices in a dry pan with visible skepticism and then left him to it. The rice soaked in salted water. The chicken, thighs because his mother had opinions about breast meat and had passed them on through repetition if not genetics, had been marinating since the afternoon in yogurt and her masala and enough green chilis to clear out anyone who wasn't ready.

Kai was the first to arrive. He came in through the side entrance from the track, hair still damp from the shower, and stopped when the smell hit him.

"That's not cafeteria food."

"Nah." Yusuf was standing at the kitchen pass-through, stirring something with a wooden spoon that belonged to Ursula. "My mum's biryani. Or my attempt at it. She'd tell me I've done something wrong with the rice."

"Your mom sent you rice?"

"Rice, spices, guilt. The usual care package." Yusuf wiped his hands on a towel he'd tucked into his belt. "Grab plates, will you? The stack by the serving line."

The extra politeness sat there between them. Yusuf used to just say plates.

Kai got plates. The cafeteria was mostly empty. Two athletes from the French sprint relay were eating protein bowls at the far end beside half-zipped team duffels, and a physiotherapist was reading on her tablet under the noticeboard where tomorrow's bus call, flight codes, and accreditation windows had been taped up in Meridian blue. Late light hit the windows hard enough to bleach the paper. Past eight and still fully bright. The days had gotten very long.

Lena came in from the pool side, her hair wet and carrying the faint chlorine trace it always did. Without ceremony Lena sat down across from Kai, looked at the plates, and said, "Is that Yusuf's?"

"His mom's recipe. Allegedly."

"Good." Then she pulled the plate closer. "The cafeteria chicken's been the same chicken for three days."

"It's the same chicken every day," Kai said. "They just move it to different trays."

"That isn't true."

"I know. But it feels true."

Margot arrived last. Lena had said she could come. At the entrance Margot paused for a moment, taking the room in before she crossed it. Then she sat down next to Lena. Lena moved her water glass half an inch to make room without looking up.

"Biryani," Margot said, looking at the serving dish Yusuf was carrying to the table with both hands.

"Biryani," Yusuf confirmed.

"Your mum's?"

"My mum's spices, my execution. Don't hold her responsible."

"I never do." Margot took the serving spoon and started filling her plate. The rice was golden-brown in layers, the chicken underneath, the fried onions on top. The smell was cumin and saffron and the particular warmth of cardamom that wasn't sweet exactly but made you think of sweetness.

Yusuf sat down. "It needs raita and I haven't got any yogurt, so." He gestured at the table as though the absence of yogurt was a physical object they could all see.

"It's fine," Lena said. She was already eating. "It's really good."

"Don't sound surprised."

"I'm not surprised. I said it's good."

For a while they just ate. The biryani was good. The rice had that texture where each grain held its shape but gave under your teeth. The chicken was tender and spiced in layers that arrived at different points in the bite. The chilis were enough to make Kai's eyes water on the second mouthful but not enough to stop him going back for a third.

"Christ," Kai said, after a particularly loaded bite. He reached for the water.

"The chilis?" Yusuf didn't look up from his plate.

"Yeah."

"My mum goes heavier. This is the mild version."

"This is mild?"

"For Birmingham, yeah."

Margot ate through the heat without comment. On the first really punishing bite her eyes watered and she kept going, faster if anything. It was the first time in weeks Kai had seen her pay attention to what was in her mouth. Earlier in the month she'd sat over untouched lunches until they went cold, jaw working on nothing, as if taste had gone thin on her. Yusuf ate steadily, the way he did everything, focused, economical, not especially social about it. Lena ate with the same precision she brought to most physical activities, small bites, thorough. Margot ate like she was hungry, which she probably was. He hadn't said anything because it wasn't his business and because Margot would have told him to fuck off.

The French relay athletes finished and left. The physiotherapist left. The cafeteria was theirs.

"So," Margot said, setting her fork down. "Big day tomorrow."

"Yeah," Kai said.

"Plane to Zurich. Zurich to Doha. Doha to forty degrees and whatever refrigerated cave Meridian built so television doesn't have to look at the desert."

"You've been reading the briefing packet," Yusuf said.

"I've had time to read." Margot pointed her fork first at Lena, then Kai. "Four-thirty for the swimmers. Six for the track. Extra medical declaration if you're still stupid enough to be running unpatched."

Kai looked up. "Unpatched, you mean."

"I know what I mean."

"That's not how the form words it," Yusuf said. The vowels had flattened into his professional voice. "But yes. There's an additional screen on your file."

"Good to know I'm special."

Yusuf kept his eyes on his plate. "If the body loads before you've heard the gun, or if the second step starts writing itself before you're clear of the blocks, you come up. I don't care if the gun's gone."

Kai set his fork down.

"You don't get to give me instructions like nothing happened," he said.

"I know that."

"Do you?"

Yusuf lifted his head then. "Yeah," he said. "I do."

The silence after that was long enough for somebody in the kitchen to bang a tray into a stack and make them all look up.

"What happens if he reports it?" Lena asked.

"Meridian reruns diagnostics and pulls him if they don't like what they see," Yusuf said.

"Which they won't," Kai said.

"False start's cheaper," Margot said.

Kai let out a breath through his nose. "Everybody's very inspiring tonight."

"That's not what this is," Yusuf said.

"No," Kai said. "It isn't."

Lena put her fork down. "What are you going to do?"

"If they pull him?" Margot asked.

"No. While we're in Doha."

Margot shrugged. "Walk the halls. Eat the chicken. Maybe finally figure out the night cook's name."

"Ursula," Yusuf said.

"See, you've ruined my one project."

Lena's fork stayed in her hand. "And after that?"

Margot rolled a grain of rice flat against the plate with the back of her fork. "Munich, probably. Petra's got a place there that takes feed cases."

"Treatment?" Lena asked.

"That's what the paperwork says."

"And what do you call it?"

Margot's mouth shifted, not quite a smile. "Being somewhere with locked drawers and people who take the hardware before breakfast."

Nobody said anything for a moment.

"When?" Kai asked.

"After you're gone. Maybe the day after." Margot kept looking at the plate. "They're still arguing over paperwork, which feels right somehow."

Kai finished his plate and served himself more. The light outside had thinned enough that the laminated travel sheet by the window started reflecting across the glass: bus call, Zurich transfer, accreditation, extra medical declaration for unpatched athletes. The ridge was still there beyond it, but the glass gave him tomorrow before it gave him the mountains.

"Meridian sent the broadcast reel for approval," he said.

Margot gave a short laugh. "Approval."

"Exactly. They let you catch spelling errors and pretend that counts." Kai scraped rice loose from the side of the dish. "Mine opens on a college final. Then the crash. Then one of those clean surgery renders where they make your spine look like premium plumbing. Then Lausanne. Thirty seconds and apparently that's a life."

"Messy how?" Lena asked.

"Left arm high. Hips late. I spend half the straight fixing it." He looked at the rice crust clinging to the serving dish. "That used to be the job. Now they keep the half after I've sorted it."

"No hospital?" Margot asked.

"No rehab. No six weeks of not being able to turn over in bed without feeling the screws. That bit didn't test well, I guess."

"Recovery is boring on television," Lena said.

"Yeah," Kai said. "It was pretty boring in person too."

"It's a highlight package," Yusuf said, careful again.

"Yeah." Kai shrugged. "That's sort of the problem."

"Did you tell them to put it back in?" Margot asked.

"No." He scraped again at the rice. "I fixed the spelling and sent it back."

Lena was watching him with that particular attention she gave to descriptions of bodies doing things their owners hadn't commanded, the stillness of someone hearing their own condition described in different terms.

Yusuf got up and brought back water for the table, four glasses from the stack by the serving line. He sat down and drank half of his.

"Farah's been on a streak," he said.

"Your sister?" Lena asked.

"Yeah. It's this thing, I don't even know what it is. Some card game her flatmate taught her, like rummy but with rules I can't follow. She wins constantly. Thursday she rang me and spent twenty minutes explaining a hand she'd played. I understood none of it."

"Is she good at cards generally?" Kai asked.

"Farah's good at everything she decides to be good at. It's annoying. It's always been like that." Yusuf's voice was easier now, but not easy. "Farah's got this thing where she pretends she doesn't know the rules so people underestimate her, and then she cleans them out. She did it to my cousin at Eid and he didn't speak to her for a week."

"Good for her," Margot said.

"It's proper evil, is what it is."

"I said good for her."

Yusuf smiled at his plate. The biryani was mostly gone. Remnants of rice clung to the serving dish in a golden-brown crust, and Yusuf scraped at it absently with the edge of the spoon, every grain accounted for.
