For a few minutes Lena had been quiet, looking out the window at the mountains and the last of the light. Her water glass turned between her hands.

"It's getting easier," she said. "The quiet."

Nobody asked what she meant.

"The first week after the patch, I kept checking." The glass turned in her hand. "Like when you think you've left the stove on. Except there's no stove, there never was a stove. Something used to run in the background and now it doesn't, and I keep reaching for where it was." She pressed her thumb hard into the side of the glass. "I'd be thinking something and I'd stop and check — was that me, or was the system still—" She shook her head. "I didn't know those were two things. I never had to ask."

Then she set the glass down and looked at her hands on the table, turning one over slowly.

"Now I can mostly tell. My thoughts are slower, and they stop and start, and there's a gap between them where the system used to be." She flexed her fingers once, watching them close and open. "This morning in the pool I lost count at forty-something. And I knew it was me who lost count. Not a correction. Not a glitch. Just me being inaccurate." She paused. "That sounds small."

"Is it better?" Kai asked.

Lena's thumb went back to the side of the water glass.

"It's mine," she said. Then, quieter: "I don't know if better is what it is."

Margot was looking at the table now, not at Lena. "I still don't know how much of the bad weeks you remember."

The glass stopped turning in Lena's hand.

"Enough," she said.

After a moment she added, "Enough that you should've told me."

Margot nodded once. "Yeah."

Outside, the ridge had gone dark enough that the cafeteria windows chose the room over the view and turned reflective. They gave back plates pushed aside, the fluorescent strip above the serving line, the departure notice taped by the door, and the pale band on Margot's wrist when she moved for her glass. The mountains were still out there somewhere, but the room had become the thing you could actually see.

"Right," Yusuf said eventually. "Dishes."

"I'll do them," Margot said.

"You don't have to-"

"I know I don't have to. Let me."

Margot stood and started stacking plates. Yusuf let her. Kai cleared the serving dish and the glasses, carrying them to the kitchen pass-through where Margot ran water into the industrial sink. Water hit steel. Ceramic knocked against ceramic. It was ordinary.

At the pass-through Yusuf took the serving dish from him and kept his voice low. "I meant it about the blocks."

Kai kept his eyes on the sink. "I heard you."

"That's not the same as doing it."

Kai let go of the dish. "If it loads before the gun, or the second step is already there under me, I stand up."

Yusuf's hand tightened once on the rim. "Right," he said.

After a beat Kai said, "Send me the start traces from March."

Yusuf looked at him.

"The legal ones," Kai said. "Not the archive. The starts where it showed itself early."

"All right."

"I want to look once on the flight. Just the load and the step timing. I want the half-beat before it takes the set."

"All right," Yusuf said again.

Lena stayed at the table, looking at the dark windows where the mountains had been. After a while two fingers brushed the quiet hardware at the base of her skull, a check so brief it barely counted, and dropped again.

Yusuf dried while Margot washed. They didn't talk much. A few words about where things went, Margot asking which shelf the serving dish belonged on, Yusuf reaching past her to put it back because she couldn't quite get to it. The efficiency of two people in a kitchen who knew where everything lived and did not know what to do with each other.

When the dishes were done, Margot dried her hands on the towel Yusuf had been wearing in his belt. Then she folded it and laid it on the counter.

"Fly safe," she said. To the room, not to anyone in particular.

"Yeah," Kai said.

Margot looked at Lena, then at the dish rack. "Swim-" She stopped. "No. Forget it. Goodnight."

"Goodnight," Lena said.

"Night," Yusuf said.

Margot left through the side entrance that led to the residential wing. The door swung shut behind her with the hydraulic hiss that all the facility doors made, a sound they'd heard ten thousand times and that Kai realized he wouldn't hear again for weeks. Then it was three of them.

Yusuf stretched his back and his spine cracked audibly. "I should pack. I haven't packed a thing. I've been making biryani instead of packing."

"Good trade," Kai said.

"Tell that to my bag at four in the morning." He pushed his chair back. "Night, both of you."

"Night."

"Goodnight, Yusuf. And thank you. For the food."

"Thank my mum. I'll tell her someone appreciated it." He raised a hand without turning around and walked out, and the door hissed shut again, and it was Kai and Lena in the empty cafeteria.

The fluorescent strip hummed. The windows were fully black now, holding only the table, the folded towel by the sink, and the white departure packet still clipped to the noticeboard. The smell of cumin and saffron sat on top of the cafeteria's old bleach-and-protein smell and lost ground to it a little more every minute.

"You all right?" Kai asked.

"Yes." Lena looked over the room before answering the rest. "I was thinking about how many meals I've eaten in this room."

"A lot?"

"Since I was sixteen. So." Her eyes moved over the table, the surface wiped clean, the chairs pushed back to where they'd been before any of this. "It won't feel like this again."

Kai looked at Margot's empty chair, then at the folded towel still on the counter by the sink. In the dark window behind Lena there were only the two of them.

"No," Kai said. "Probably not."

Lena did not sit down again. "When Yusuf told you to come up if it starts early, you said yes."

"Eventually."

"Was that real?"

Kai looked at his left hand on the table, the tendons quiet under the skin. "If I feel that March warning in the blocks, yes. Back leg loading before the gun. Second step lining itself up before I've asked for it. I stand up and take the false start." He rubbed the heel of his hand against his jeans. "If it waits until I'm upright, I don't know yet. Then it's not a clean stop. Then it's whether I feel it coming soon enough to break it before it takes the rest of the race."

"And the traces?"

"One look," he said. "March is when it still gave me notice. Not much. Just enough that the body felt half a beat ahead of me. I want that in my head before Doha so I know the difference between race nerves and the bridge reaching."

"You trust him with that?"

Kai shook his head. "Not with me. With the lines."

Lena studied him for a moment. "That is enough for tonight."

Then she came around the table to where he was sitting. At his chair she stopped. He reached for her hand and she gave it to him, his left hand, the one that still complained in the cold, her right hand cool from the water glass. They stayed like that for a while in the empty cafeteria, the fluorescent strip humming above them and the smell of Yusuf's mother's cooking still stubborn against the cafeteria bleach.

Then they let go, turned off the light, and went upstairs to pack.


# Chapter 17

The ready room was underground. The venue sat over Lena like a second pressure system, concrete and cooling ducts and forty thousand seats holding themselves above her head. In Kirchberg the pool had always sounded alive before she entered it: pumps, air handlers, the feed hiss in Margot's wrist unit, all the little system noises braided into one thing. Here the room was cleaner than that, almost ceremonial. Her own skull stayed cleaner still. There were no ambient calculations and no low hum of suggested timing, only silence where there had always been company.

Seven other swimmers waited with her. Lena knew most of them by shoulders, kick timing, and the habits that survived from meet to meet. Adelaïde Moreau stretched in the corner with the saint medallion she always tucked into her suit before the walk out. Kaede Watanabe had her eyes closed and her lips moving through some private rehearsal. Nina Okoro paced the far wall with three fingers taped bright green. The room held that smooth patched readiness Lena could still read before anyone touched the water, bodies already halfway into the race through systems she no longer trusted. Months ago she would have recognized it as ordinary. Tonight it looked slightly indecent, the body being helped in ways nobody in the seats would ever see.

Her towel lay across her knees. The terry cloth kept catching on the dry skin of her fingertips. She pulled the strap on one goggle, then the other, equal pressure, and made herself stop there. The old Lena would have been running invisible checklists without naming them, stroke count windows and turn timing and the fine adjustments a linked system offered so smoothly they had once felt like intuition. This version had to do the rude manual work. She took four controlled breaths, checked that the right shoulder was loose enough and the left calf free of cramp, and gave herself a plain instruction: keep the first hundred clean, no heroics, count to the wall yourself, stay in the water you can actually feel.

One of the volunteers at the door checked the line against a tablet and called, "Morozova. Lane five."

Lena rose. The room changed around her as everyone else rose too, the brief scrape of benches and the soft slap of bare feet on sealed concrete. Moreau touched two fingers to the medallion at her sternum and followed the official. Watanabe opened her eyes and looked not calm but already absent, the way patched athletes sometimes looked just before the body handed certain jobs off. Lena did not want that look anymore. She wanted the ugly human version with all the drag left in it.

The access passage to the pool deck was fluorescent and cold. Chilled air came through the vents hard enough to dry the water on her arms before she reached the light. At the final turn the sound of the crowd stopped being abstract vibration and became one enormous moving thing. She walked into it and the lights found her at once. They came down hard enough to glaze the water and bleach the color out of faces. Blue-green pool, black lane ropes, white splash guards, cameras on their tracks above the far side. Lane five waited in the middle as if the whole building had been built around it.

For one second she looked toward the coaches' section out of old habit. It was only an empty reflex. Margot was in Munich. The place where a coach would have been was just another rectangle of dark seating and people in credentials. That was good. Let the race belong to the people actually in it.

The starter introduced them one by one. Lena stepped onto the block when her name came and the crowd made the broad appreciative noise crowds made for finalists they did not yet care about individually. Moreau stood in four, heavy favorite, French flag painted on one cheek by some child in the stands behind her. Watanabe was in three, still and narrow. Lin in six shook out both arms once and then went expressionless. Lena bent, settled her feet against the rough surface, and let the small useless wish pass through her that the dive would arrive already solved.

The tone sounded and she went.

Water hit her blunt and grainy instead of opening into the old second language of pressure and signal. There was no soft guided feeling of the lane receiving her. Depth was just depth. Cold was just cold. The first pull bit high and clean anyway. She kept the shoulder engaged, the elbow up, and the kick hard enough to hold the line. She broke out under the flags with Moreau half a body to her right and Watanabe's white splash low and efficient to her left. Fifty meters disappeared in ordinary human labor.

On the first turn she counted late on purpose because she still did not fully trust the place where instinct had used to live. She took six strokes from the flags, made the last one longer, tucked, planted, and pushed. The wall gave her a good clean return and for a moment the race simplified into pressure on the soles of her feet and the long narrow relief of streamline. When she came off it she was even with Moreau and a fraction behind Watanabe. That was fine. It was more than fine. It meant she had judged the wall herself and not been wrong.

At a hundred the field was still pretending balance. Moreau's turnover remained quiet, economical, almost rude in how little surface she disturbed for the speed she carried. Lin was coming up outside. Lena turned to breathe and got one brief sliced image of the crowd above the lane rope, mouths open, white panels of advertisement, then water again. The patch had taken away a layer of anticipatory ease she had once mistaken for talent. Every correction now arrived after perception, not before it. She had to feel the hand entering wrong, then fix it. Feel the hips rise too far, then press them down. The race did not forgive the delay, but neither did it take her away from herself.

At a hundred and fifty her deltoids began to burn in the steady nonnegotiable way that meant the body had stopped lying. A different race might have frightened her there. This one did not. Fear would have required room for abstraction, and abstraction had no place in lane five. She hit the wall, pushed, took two underwater kicks, and surfaced. Moreau's feet were still just ahead and then they began, inch by inch, to slide away.

Two hundred brought the part of the evening everyone else would later call decisive. The split numbers flashed at the turn, bright and gone before she could read more than shape. Air came fast on the breath to the right. Lin was there now with a harder, more urgent tempo, the outside lane suddenly level with Lena's shoulder and then not level at all. Watanabe held for a little longer, then her line started to fray. Lena flipped, pushed, and felt the push come back shorter than the one before it, not because the wall was different but because nothing inside her was smoothing out the waste.

That was where the old system used to become seductive. In bad races, in dangerous races, in training sets where the body wanted to quit or drift or blur, something behind her own decisions had always started solving small problems early. The hand rotated a degree before the catch could slip. The kick corrected depth before she noticed she had flattened out. The turn arrived as if a quieter smarter swimmer had done it first and she was merely inheriting the work. For years she had called that integration. Tonight all of it was gone. Her right hand came in slightly wide and she had to pull it back into the line herself. Her feet hit the next wall a shade too high and she had to kick once to settle before the first pull. Every error cost time because every error belonged to her.

The third hundred broke the field open. Moreau and Lin went into their own race. Watanabe came back toward Lena the way Watanabe always did, late suffering written into the Japanese swimmer's whole style. Lena went by on stubbornness and a cleaner line off the wall, but the pass cost her. Her elbow dropped. Water leaked around the heel of her palm. The chemical bite sat at the back of her throat with the hot metallic taste that came when she was breathing too often and still not getting enough air. By three hundred the board was no longer a set of positions. It was just proof that the thing kept moving whether or not anyone in it approved.

The lane narrowed in the last hundred, not physically but morally. Nothing in it softened. Her shoulders filled with acid so thoroughly the burn became a texture rather than a pain. The crowd above the water was a thick muffled weather, loud for somebody, not specifically for lane five. On the breath she could see Moreau's lead only as bubbles and foamed white wake; on the opposite breath Lin's outside splash entered and exited her vision like weather seen through a train window. There was no lane map, no pacing advice, no synthetic whisper that this wall required six strokes instead of seven. There was only counting and getting it wrong by half a hand and still having to live with the result.

At three hundred and fifty her triceps began to tremble on the recovery. Lena felt the tremor all the way into her wrist, ugly and specific. Good. Let it be ugly. Let it be specific. On the final turn she nearly over-rotated because fatigue had shifted her sense of depth by a breath's width. Her heels found the wall crooked, corrected, pushed. The correction cost a fraction and then the final length was underway and there was nothing left to bargain with. She did not need the race to become beautiful. She needed it to remain continuous.

The last fifty meters took place at a speed that would have felt insulting six months earlier. Each stroke had to be built in public, hand in and catch and pull and breathe and hold the line. There was no missing stretch of water, no blank seam stitched across the memory later, no cold astonishment of surfacing with work done by someone else. Her shoulders were close to failure, palms going numb from overworked tendons, legs soft at the end of each kick, and she was there for every centimeter of it. The finish came under her hand like a blunt fact.

She touched and came up hard, mouth open, a sheet of chlorine running off her face. The first breath tore. The second was worse. She got both forearms onto the touchpad and hung there while the whole building resumed shape around her.

The board across the pool gave her 4:08.71. Moreau sat first at 3:49.03. Lin took second at 3:50.88. Lena's name dropped in under them in third, a fraction of a second before the lane number locked beside it. It was bronze, and it was slow by any standard that had governed her old life, slower than the system would once have permitted. But the distance between dive and wall remained intact in her head. There was no gap and no theft, only the full humiliating complete four hundred meters.

A volunteer met her at the edge with a towel and a hand under one elbow because her legs had not yet decided whether land was a reasonable idea. "Warm-down pool that way," the volunteer said in English practiced for international use. "Then ceremony in twelve minutes."

Lena nodded. Speaking took too much breath.

The recovery pool behind the competition lanes was half shadow and half television spill. Swimmers from earlier finals moved through it in slow exhausted circles, each in their own country jacket or flag or private misery. Lena slid in anyway. The water sat differently on the patch than race water did: flatter, less urgent, not the medium where she had once vanished but only warm chlorinated resistance closing around sore skin. She took the lane rope with one hand and let her body lengthen under the surface. Muscles in both shoulders twitched in delayed revolt. Her heart was still trying to outrun the finish.

She could still count the turns backward: six strokes from the flags, Lin going by outside on the third hundred, the slight crooked plant at three fifty. The lane rope left a red braid across her palm where she held it too hard.

She rolled onto her back and looked at the rafters. In the relay of giant venue screens and mirrored glass above, her own race was already running again. Her right hand leaked water at the catch in exactly the place she remembered it, and at the final turn both triceps gave the same ugly little shake they had given her in lane five.

An official in a navy blazer knelt by the recovery pool and said, "Ms. Morozova, medal ceremony in three minutes."

By the time Lena climbed out, the medal finalists' towels and sandals had already been lined up with small bureaucratic care. Someone handed her a jacket in Swiss team colors though she had not felt Swiss on a pool deck since childhood, maybe never. Water cooled on her skin under the fabric. The walk to the podium took place through a service passage lined with screens, and every screen had found the same frame from her finish: head up, mouth open, face not triumphant and not devastated either, just brutally alive.

Moreau stood on the center block with the easy upright posture of someone whose body had given her what it usually gave. Lin took silver with both shoulders drawn back as if she were presenting the medal to the cameras rather than receiving it. Lena climbed onto the third step and felt a brief shiver run through both calves as the legs understood they were done. The bronze ribbon settled cold against the back of her neck before the medal itself found her sternum with a small decisive weight. Metal and body heat negotiated. The medal warmed.

The anthem for Moreau went up into the cooled air. Lena did not sing anything, did not mouth anything, did not look at the cameras when they moved in. The giant screen above them cut among the three medalists and then, for no good reason except that this was the story people wanted, cut to the lower graphic from the lane introductions again with a side note about Meridian's voluntary update. Refused. Declined. Unpatched. The exact wording shifted depending on which feed was active. The meaning did not. Bronze had become an argument before she had even dried her hair.

Applause carried them off the podium. Another volunteer touched Lena's elbow and pointed her toward the mixed zone.

The bronze hung cold and heavy at first, then warm where it rested against her sternum. Cameras waited behind the barrier in a long bright row. Before she had reached the taped mark on the floor, a reporter leaned over the rail with a microphone and asked, "Did refusing Meridian's update cost you the gold tonight?"

The question arrived in English and then again from her left in French. Lena looked past the microphones to the monitor overhead, where the replay was already running her through the middle two hundred with Moreau cleanly opening the gap.

"It cost me time," she said.

"Do you regret it?"

"No."

The first reporter tried again, louder because someone behind him was already talking. "You swam almost nineteen seconds off your season best. Is that a result you can build on, or—"

"It is the result I have."

"—or is it a sign that unpatched athletes can't compete at this level?"

Lena shifted the towel higher on one shoulder. Her deltoids had started to stiffen and the motion cost more than she wanted anyone with a camera to see. "I don't know what other unpatched athletes can do. I swam my race."

Another microphone came in from below, branded with a network she did not recognize. "You said you have no regrets. Can you expand on that?"

"No."

The reporter waited. When she didn't continue, he tried from another angle. "Do you mean the patch prevented another desynchronization event?"

"I didn't desynchronize. I was slow."

"Isn't that the same thing?"

"No."

That got a brief shuffle behind the barrier, reporters recalibrating. The handler to Lena's right shifted closer, wanting to move her along, but another reporter had already found the gap.

"Was this a political decision? A statement against Meridian?"

"It wasn't political."

"Then what was it?"

Lena tightened her grip on the towel because the medal kept knocking against her sternum every time she breathed and her right shoulder was starting to lock. "I wanted to know where I was in the water," she said. "The whole time."

"Is third place enough for that?"

"I'm not comparing them."

The young reporter opened his mouth, but the handler put a hand at the small of Lena's back and said, "One more."

A French reporter with a calm face and a notebook instead of a microphone said, "Mademoiselle Morozova, when you say you wanted to know where you were — are you saying that before this, you did not?"

Lena stopped.

She answered in French because he had asked in French and because it let her be exact in a slightly different way. "Sometimes I lost sequence," she said. "Not consciousness. The order of things. Tonight I didn't."

He wrote it down.

The handler moved her on. By the time she glanced up, the subtitle strip had turned her French into MOROZOVA: STAYED IN CONTROL.

The tunnel out of the mixed zone curved beneath the seating bowl. By the time Lena reached the athletes' passage, two venue staff were already arguing over relay order on their headsets and a floor crew was dragging a cable mat toward the next entrance. Her face kept appearing on the monitor above the stairs, then vanishing under lane assignments and sponsor bugs.

She should have gone to the village transport. She should have showered, eaten something bland, answered the federation message that had come in during warm-down. Instead she took the stairs up toward the athletes' seating with the medal tucked under her jacket so it would stop striking her chest every time she walked.

The track infield opened below her all at once, red and white under harsher light than the pool had carried. Field-event officials moved equipment with efficient indifference. A men's relay semifinal was ending on the far straight. On the giant board above the curve a studio panel in sharp clothes was already replaying her swim from the angles that made the margin look easy. One commentator said the bronze proved the danger of refusing validated updates. Another called it courage. The third talked over a cut that jumped from her dive to the result board and skipped the turn where her heels hit the wall crooked at three fifty.

Lena took a seat high enough to see lane seven on the curve when the assignment board came up. Brennan. Seven. The medal lay warm now beneath the jacket, a second pulse that was not a pulse at all.

---

The warm-up hall under the track smelled of rubber dust, menthol gel, and overworked air-conditioning. Kai was on a stationary bike facing a cinderblock wall when Lena's face came up on the monitor mounted above the stretching lane. No sound reached him from there, only the silent shape of her mouth under a subtitle strip that kept switching between BRONZE AFTER PATCH REFUSAL, MOROZOVA ON CONTROL, and a sponsor bumper before he could catch the next line.

He looked back at the wall and kept pedaling.

The left wrist had started complaining an hour earlier and had not stopped. The ache sat along the outside ridge where the break had sealed too fast, not sharp, just insistent, a remembered failure with good manners. Sweat moved cold under his singlet. The legs felt ready in the narrow technical sense, hamstrings loaded, calves responsive, both grafts answering warm-up commands without lag. Readiness had stopped being reassuring months ago. Readiness only meant the machinery was armed.

Yusuf came through the security curtain with a credential bouncing against his chest and a tablet in one hand. The expression on his face was the one Kai had come to distrust most over the last months: not panic, not honesty, but professional composure polished until it hid everything under it. He stopped at the bike and did not waste time pretending he had come for any reason except one more attempt.

"You can still scratch," Yusuf said.

Kai kept pedaling for another three revolutions before he answered. "No."

"They'd call it precaution. Nobody would make a thing of it until later."

"Later is still a thing."

Yusuf glanced once at the silent monitor where Lena's subtitled face had given way to a replay of some pole vaulter's approach. "I'm not talking about reputation."

"I know what you're talking about."

The bike clicked under Kai's feet, smooth little cycles with none of the violence the actual race would require. Yusuf lowered his voice, though there was nobody close enough to hear.

"At this propagation level the probability of a triggered event in a two-hundred-meter race is significant."

"You told me that two days ago."

"It didn't become less true because you were tired of hearing it."

Fair enough. Kai let the bike coast down and stood carefully, because stepping off too fast sometimes brought a short ugly protest from the left calf even when nothing was actually wrong. "You want the useful answer or the real one?"

"Preferably both."

"Useful answer is I know the model." He wiped his hands on the sides of his shorts and immediately regretted it because the fabric was already damp. "Real answer is I don't want one more layer between me and whatever happens out there."

Yusuf's jaw moved once. In another year, in another relationship, he might have argued. He might have gone technical, or angry, or earnest. The damaged version of trust they were carrying now allowed him only a small exhale and a professional nod. "If it goes," he said, "don't try to salvage the race."

"There won't be much to salvage."

"I mean it."

"Yeah."

The engineer's eyes dropped to Kai's legs, not staring, just checking the hardware he knew too well. "You have maybe a second, maybe less, between trigger and full sprint retention if the blackout hits on the straight. Earlier if you feel the lead-in. Don't wait for clarity. There won't be any."

Kai gave him a look. "Very comforting."

"I'm not here for comfort."

"No," Kai said. "That part I definitely know."

For a beat they stood inside the low ugly room with the bike still ticking as the flywheel lost momentum and the monitor above them showing a replay from the pool. Yusuf shifted the tablet into his other hand.

"Lena's through," he said, because of course he had seen it already.

"Bronze."

"Yes."

Kai nodded. He wanted to ask what she had looked like coming off the water, whether she had seemed relieved or wrecked or both, whether the patch had held the whole way without the small legacy corrections Yusuf had warned might still surface under maximum fatigue. None of those questions belonged in this room. He said, "Good."

Yusuf studied him as if deciding whether that word was enough. "She remembers all of it," he said finally. "That's what she told the press."

The words hit Kai in a place he had been keeping deliberately numb all afternoon. He looked up at the soundless monitor again and saw Lena in freeze frame, wet hair slicked back, medal at her throat, mouth half open on some answer the world was already trimming down to size.

"All right," he said.

The marshal called for finalists to report to the call room.

Kai picked up his spikes bag, though there was nothing in it he still needed. The walk from warm-up to call room ran through a service tunnel under the stands where chilled air moved hard enough to raise gooseflesh on the backs of his arms. Somewhere overhead the crowd changed register for the next event and the sound came through the structure as a dull moving pressure. His body kept trying to interpret that pressure as cue and countdown and opportunity. He kept refusing the help.

The call room itself was smaller than the pool ready area had been, more crowded with muscle and intent. Sprinters always changed the air in a room. Even standing still, they made stillness look temporary. Dekker was on the floor rolling one calf over a hard ball. Okafor stood with his eyes closed and both hands loose at his sides, running whatever sequence his updated system preferred before a major final. Chen bounced once on the balls of his feet and then became completely motionless. Every one of them looked a fraction ahead of the room they were actually in.

Kai sat on the bench under lane seven and pressed his thumb against the sore ridge of the wrist. He had no rehearsal and no inner film of the race. Nothing stood between the original install and the virus except the crude control he had taught himself in ugly private minutes since Kirchberg: fingers stopping halfway to a glass, a calf cramp strangled before the foot could kick, a hand held open on his own thigh while the graft wanted to close it. Those were tiny embarrassing things, not the sort of training anyone put in a biography. They were still what he had.

An official checked bib numbers, shoes, names. Someone on the far bench laughed at a joke Kai didn't hear. Another athlete spat gum into a tissue and folded the tissue with weird ceremonial care. The room held all that economical readiness patched men carried now, preparation looking like the body deciding things early. Kai watched it and kept very still.

The marshal opened the door. They rose together and followed the line through the tunnel.

Doha heat pressed at the sealed glass above the stadium, visible only in the hard white of the light and the fact that everyone coming in from outside looked briefly assaulted by the cold. The track gave under Kai's spikes with that engineered compromise between grip and return he had known for years and never fully trusted. Lane seven waited on the outside curve. Good lane for disappearing if things went badly. Bad lane for winning if they did not.

He knelt at the blocks and set both hands against the track. Rubber granules pressed into his fingertips. A camera floated past on a remote arm low enough for him to see his own face in its polished housing, smaller and flatter than it felt from inside. The crowd made the big generalized noise it made for finalists, louder for Okafor in four, louder still when the stadium screen cut to Kai's name because everybody loved a comeback story and loved catastrophe even more. The screen did not say any of that. It only gave lane, country, season best, sponsor history, the usual clean facts with all the dirt hidden under them.

On your marks.

He put his hands down, tipped his weight forward, and let his hips load. The bridge at the base of his spine hummed low and steady in the familiar preparatory way, running scripts, aligning force, doing the work enhancement had been sold as doing invisibly and well. For the moment it was still on his side. That had to be enough.

Set.

The gun cracked the air and the whole field drove.

The first thirty meters belonged to acceleration, no room for thought, only angle and force and the blunt old argument between track and body. Kai stayed low and pushed. The blocks were gone, the curve was entering, and the outer leg was taking the load. At forty his chest opened a fraction. At sixty the stagger still hid most of the race from him. He could feel only the quality of his own stride: the slight seam between command and execution that had never entirely disappeared after the first blackout, intent landing a fraction late and being caught by practiced muscle before it became visible.

At eighty he was in it. The stagger began to unwind and other bodies resolved from geometry into people. Okafor was inside him, clean and ferocious. Dekker was moving well in six. Chen held form with that severe compact style that always looked as if he resented the event for existing. Kai ran the curve hard and let his arms do what they had been built to do. The wrist lit up once on the back swing, deep and wrong, reminder rather than injury. That was fine. He stayed with it.

The straight opened.

That was usually the part where the race simplified into violence. He had to lift the torso, keep the jaw loose, keep the shoulders from climbing, and trust the legs because there was no longer time not to. Tonight the straight came with its own private weather. Air-conditioning poured over the lane from somewhere high above and dried the sweat at his temples before it could run. The sound of the crowd flattened into one broad moving sheet. At one twenty his scars were tight in the dry air. At one forty his legs were still doing exactly what he told them to do, which would have felt ordinary to anyone else and miraculous to him.

At one fifty something flickered at the edge of the visual field, not darkness but wrong assembly. A lane line drifted half an inch above where it should have been. The far wall arrived too early and then slid back into place. It was the lead-in. There it was.

Kai did not wait for clarity.

At one sixty the world came apart.

This blackout did not empty anything. It shredded adjacency. The red track split into pieces that no longer belonged to the same place. White lane markings hung at separate depths, one near, one impossibly far, the straight and the infield failing to agree on a common world. His own right arm existed twice, once as weight and once as image, out of sync by a beat that would have meant nothing at a dinner table and everything at sprint speed. Then sound thinned into a high interior tone that swallowed crowd, spikes, breath, history.

The legs kept sprinting.

It was not metaphorical and it was not residual momentum. The bridge took the pattern and ran it. His right quad fired on a command he had not sent. His left calf loaded. Hip extension kept time with a race that no longer required him. Somewhere in the broken pieces of the straight his body was still trying to win.

Months of ugly private practice returned not as memory but as grip. The point had never been to regain some pure natural self. That ship was gone. The point had been smaller and meaner than that: to feel the instant when autonomous movement crossed the line from help into theft, and to get there quickly enough to interfere. In Kirchberg he had done it sitting on the edge of a bed with his eyes closed, catching a healing hand as it reached toward nothing. Later he had done it standing barefoot in the bathroom when the left calf wanted to fire early and he forced the foot flat against tile until the whole leg trembled. They were ridiculous secret exercises, nothing anyone would call training unless they knew why.

He caught the right quad first.

Stop.

The contradictory signals met inside the muscle and the leg seized halfway through the stride. Pain tore up the front of the thigh so suddenly and specifically he almost lost the rest of the body to it. Momentum threw his torso left. The track came at him in broken red panels. One spike scraped, found purchase, skidded, held. The left leg tried to complete the sprint cycle on its own and he killed that one too, not smoothly, not cleanly. A hard wrench through the calf. Teeth clacked together. Something bit bloody on the inside of his cheek.

The body wanted the original instruction back. Sprint. Sprint. Sprint.

Kai gave it something smaller and uglier. Step.

The left foot came down because he told it to, not because the bridge had found the next line in the pattern. Weight transferred badly. Another autonomous contraction jumped in the right hamstring and he met it with a stop so hard the whole back of the leg cramped from glute to knee. Fine. Better a cramp than the wall at full speed with no map of where the wall was. Another step. Then another. The straight kept trying to reassemble around him and failing. The high tone in his head did not let up.

By the time the images started agreeing again, the race had gone on without him. Bodies were through the line, arms were up, deceleration phases were underway, and lane officials were already moving. A camera operator in a press vest jogged the edge of the track to stay with him as he covered the last thirty meters in front of forty thousand baffled people, not walking exactly, not running either, dragging a sprint back into something a human nervous system could inhabit.

He crossed in 23.41. His name fell to the bottom of the board, eight of eight.

The noise from the crowd had changed. The cheer broke apart into scattered clapping, a few drawn-out whistles, and people in the lower rows standing to point at lane seven instead of the board. On the screen above the finish, replay footage had already cut the race into neat angles. From the speakers, the English commentary arrived a beat after the picture. "Possible technical failure for Brennan in lane seven."

Kai bent over with both hands on his knees because standing upright asked too much of the legs just then and because if he tried to explain anything immediately he might vomit on the track. Saliva tasted metallic where he had bitten the inside of his cheek. Both calves were still twitching in ugly little aftershocks, autonomous fragments trying to restart a race that had already ended.

A race official and a medic reached him almost together.

"Mr. Brennan, we need you at medical."

"You able to walk?" the medic asked.

Kai straightened slowly. The bridge had gone eerily quiet at the base of his spine, not healed, not safe, just done spending its violence for the moment. On the screen above him he looked like a man malfunctioning. Under the damp fabric of his shorts, the right quad had balled hard where he'd cut the stride in half, and the left calf kept giving small involuntary jumps.

"It wasn't a failure," he said.

The official either did not hear him or filed the sentence under athlete incoherence. "Sir. This way."

The tunnel under the stands felt colder after the track. Fluorescent light caught on the sweat cooling over Kai's shoulders and made the whole underpass look clinical before the actual medical room had even begun. Staff kept moving around him with event-day briskness, headsets, clipboards, practiced concern. One of the television monitors mounted near the entrance still had his stumble on loop with a graphic asking whether unpatched athletes were taking unacceptable risk.

Inside medical, paper crackled on the exam table when he sat down. A nurse clipped a pulse sensor to one finger. Another medic wrapped the blood pressure cuff around his arm. Electrodes found his chest with chilly adhesive precision. The whole room smelled of alcohol swabs and synthetic fabric and the thin sour note of athlete sweat that no amount of venue money ever eliminated.

"Did you lose consciousness?" the first medic asked.

"No."

"Visual disturbance?"

"Yeah."

"Auditory?"

"Yeah."

"Motor control?"

Kai looked at him. "Not in the normal direction."

The medic paused over the tablet. "Can you clarify that?"

"My legs kept going."

"After the sensory event."

"During it."

That earned a quick glance between the medic and the official standing by the door. Neither of them looked interested in the sentence as lived experience. They looked interested in how to categorize it. Heat illness, collision, seizure, hardware error, athlete statement pending. The form on the tablet kept demanding the kind of story institutions preferred: one thing happened, then another thing, then liability traveled neatly to the right place.

Yusuf appeared in the doorway before Kai had to say anything else. Credential, tablet, face too composed. The official moved to intercept him, then saw who he was and stepped aside.

"I just need sixty seconds," Yusuf said.

"He's under assessment," the medic replied.

"So assess him. I need sixty seconds."

They let him in because people always let technicians in when the problem might be technical.

Yusuf stopped at the foot of the bed. "How much sensory loss?"

"Enough."

"Specific, please."

Kai laughed once without humor and immediately regretted it because the laugh tightened the cramp in the right hamstring. "Visual fragmentation first. Then most of the rest dropped out."

"Complete blackout?"

"No. Worse, actually. Pieces."

Yusuf nodded as if he had expected exactly that and hated being right. "Override latency?"

"I caught the lead-in."

"So before full pattern retention."

"Barely."

The engineer's gaze flicked to both legs, reading tremor, involuntary twitch, the way Kai was holding himself a fraction too still around the right thigh. "Any tear?"

"Don't think so."

"That's not the same answer."

"Then no." Kai swallowed. "Feels like I strangled both quads with my brain, but no."

The medic had been listening despite pretending not to. "We need a more standard description for the report."

Kai looked at the ceiling tiles, then back at the medic. "There isn't one."

Silence held for a second. Monitor leads ticked faintly against his skin when he breathed.

The official at the door touched his earpiece and said, "Mixed zone still wants him if he's ambulatory."

Of course they did.

The medic checked the monitor one more time, then peeled the cuff loose. "No collapse, no ongoing arrhythmia, pupils reactive. I want him rechecked in an hour. Hydration, no further exertion."

"No chance of that," Kai said.

The joke landed dead, which suited him fine.

By the time he left medical, the worst shaking had concentrated itself into the hands and the right leg. Not enough to stop walking. Enough to notice with every step. The inside of his cheek had swollen where the teeth caught it. He could taste blood every time his tongue found the cut. The track replay was still everywhere. One screen had slowed the moment of his sideways lurch into something dramatic and wrong enough to keep viewers from changing channels.

The mixed zone after a track final was louder and less orderly than the pool's had been, more adrenaline still floating in the air from the event itself. Barriers leaned under the pressure of cameras. Reporters shouted over one another because that was the economy they worked in. Someone called his name before he had even reached the taped mark.

"Kai, was that a technical failure?"

He stopped because there was no version of this that improved through pretending not to hear.

"No," he said.

"Then what happened in lane seven?"

The question came from three directions at once, English and French and something else under them. Kai looked past the microphones to the overhead screen where his own body was still staggering down the straight under a banner about enhancement risk. The replay caught the sideways hitch and skipped the part where he shut the legs down one by one.

"I lost the track for a second," he said. "Sight, distance, where the wall was. The legs didn't."

"So your system kept firing after you lost orientation?"

"Yeah."

"Was that the virus?"

"Close enough."

A reporter with a federation laminate pushed in from the side. "Medical says you told them your legs were still in sprint retention after the visual event. Did you disclose that risk before the final?"

Kai stared at him. That was new. The question wasn't new. The speed was. The reporter already had one eye on the overhead crawl while he waited for the answer.

"Everybody in this building knew the risk," he said.

"Did they know you could still be moving with no map of the straight?"

"I knew I could stop it."

"Could have, or did?"

"Did."

The overhead monitor cut from replay to studio and back again. A lower bar now asked whether holdouts should be cleared for sprint events at all.

Another reporter took the opening. "Did you endanger the field?"

"I'm in lane seven," Kai said. "Outside. I was trying not to put myself through the wall."

"So you admit you shouldn't have been on the track."

"No. I said what I was trying to stop."

"Should the result stand if you weren't in control for part of the race?"

Kai could feel the handler shifting at his elbow now, wanting to cut it off, but this at least was honest pressure, not the neat version. "I got it back before the wall," he said. "That's why I'm standing here."

The handler touched his elbow. "Last question."

A woman with a public-broadcast microphone and the patient manner of someone who had covered athletes for years asked, "When you say you knew you could stop it, had you practiced that?"

That one deserved an honest answer if he had one.

Kai thought about the bathroom tile in Kirchberg under his bare foot. The healing hand arrested halfway to a glass. Nights on the bed edge with the room dark because he had not wanted to watch his own fingers move early again.

"Yeah," he said. "Badly. In rooms by myself. Stopping stupid little movements before they finished."

Several of them looked down at notebooks or screens.

The handler moved him on.

Beyond the final camera position the service passage widened into a staff thoroughfare with stacks of hurdles against one wall and a row of bottled water on a folding table no one had bothered to make look glamorous. Lena was waiting there in a team jacket with the bronze hidden underneath it badly enough that the shape still showed. Her hair had dried rough from pool water. The skin around her eyes looked tired in the specific post-race way he knew well now, the face still catching up to what the body had already spent.

She looked first at his legs, then at his face. "Did they understand?"

"No."

"All right," she said.

There was no comfort in it and no surprise either, only agreement with the facts.

Kai put one hand on the back of a plastic chair somebody had abandoned by the wall because the right leg had chosen that moment to twitch again from hip to calf. Lena saw the movement and stepped closer without making a production of it.

"Did you stop it in time?" she asked quietly.

"Yeah."

"Did it hurt?"

He laughed once through his nose. "Yeah."

She nodded as if that, too, was exactly what she had expected. Then she moved to his right side and waited for the leg to settle before she started walking with him.

The next event's gun cracked through the concrete above them. Staff started moving again around the edges of the hall. On one of the service monitors the replay ran from the high end-zone angle: hitch, sideways step, pace gone, finish. The lower-third underneath it had already changed to HOLDOUTS TOO RISKY?

Kai looked at the screen once, then kept his eyes on the exit. Lena stayed at his right shoulder as they went down the passage toward the athletes' door, the stadium above them already cheering for what came next.


# Chapter 18

Weeks after Doha, Kai sat at an outside table at a cafe on the Rue du Grand-Pont, his coffee half-finished and the afternoon showing no interest in moving. Lausanne in August: the lake below the old town catching light between buildings, tourists on the steep sidewalks with shopping bags and good shoes, a woman at the next table reading an article on a tablet, her coffee untouched and one finger waking the screen every few seconds before it could go dark. The cathedral's tower was visible above the rooftops, grey stone against a sky that had been blue all day and looked ready to stay that way until evening.

His left wrist had been complaining since lunch. He turned it once. The ache stayed.

The email from SportsVision was still open on his phone, face-down beside the coffee. It was the third reach-out in two weeks. They wanted Kai Brennan, dead last in the 200 meters in Doha, on a commentary team for the next Apex Games cycle, explaining sprinting to viewers who liked the spectacle more than the body inside it.

He hadn't said no. He hadn't said yes. The not-deciding felt fine, which was new. For most of his career, indecision had been one more task to clear out before the next training block, the next race, the next heat. Now it could sit there with the coffee and not spoil the afternoon.

A waiter came. Kai ordered another coffee in English and the waiter answered in French and they understood each other fine. The waiter was maybe twenty, shoulders rounded and his empty hand still curved as if a tray were balanced on it.

His right hand lifted the phone before he meant it to, thumb already waking the screen, and he set it back on the table.

This was the daily version. It wasn't Doha. It wasn't a catastrophe. It was just small corrections arriving early: the phone in his hand, the cup meeting the saucer dead center. His body moved a little ahead of him and then waited.

"Mr. Brennan?"

He looked up. The woman standing by the empty chair across from him was maybe mid-thirties, dark hair pulled back, linen shirt, sunglasses hooked into the collar. She held a phone and a notebook low against her hip and didn't crowd the table.

"I'm Elise Marchand," she said. "SportsVision."

Kai let out a breath through his nose. "You guys are getting creative."

"You weren't answering."

"I was considering not answering forever."

"Can I sit down for five minutes?"

He looked at the chair. "Sure."

She sat. "I'll be quick."

"That's usually a lie."

"Sometimes." She set the notebook on the table but didn't open it. "You're not interested in coaching, and you clearly hate press. Commentary is a different job. Less pretending anybody cares what your recovery breakfast looks like."

"Strong pitch."

"It's not the actual pitch." She glanced at the phone beside his cup. "Doha is the pitch."

Kai stayed still.

"Most former athletes can talk splits and lane discipline," Elise said. "We've got those already. We don't have many people who'll look at a body going wrong on a track and not call it bad luck because that's the easy phrase."

"You say that like your producers are brave."

"Some of them aren't." She didn't smile. "I heard the effects mic after your race. You told the official it wasn't a failure."

Kai looked at the lake through the gap between buildings. "Maybe I was trying to be difficult."

"Were you?"

He tapped one finger once against the table. "No."

Elise waited him out. She was good at it.

"Everybody wants a clean version," Kai said at last. "Hardware issue. Athlete guts it out. Crowd applauds. Then everybody goes home feeling normal."

"And your version?"

He gave a short shrug. "The body got out ahead of me. I was still in it. I just wasn't setting the pace anymore."

Elise watched him. "Would you say that on air?"

"Depends who's paying to hear it."

"SportsVision."

"And Meridian buys ads."

"Meridian buys ads everywhere."

He laughed once, quietly. "Yeah. That's kind of the point."

The waiter returned with the coffee and set it down between them. Kai thanked him. Warmth spread through his palms.

"I'm not asking you to decide right now," Elise said. "If you want, come to Geneva next Thursday and do a screen test. If you hate it, you leave. If you don't hate it, we talk."

"And if I ignore you again?"

"Then I'll assume it's no and stop embarrassing myself in cafes."

"Appreciate that."

She pulled a card from her notebook and slid it across the table. "Tuesday."

Kai didn't touch it.

Elise stood. "Enjoy the afternoon, then."

"That's the plan."

She left him with the card under the sugar jar and the chair still angled a little toward him. A pigeon landed on the back of it a moment later and leaned forward to inspect the table.

"Nothing for you," Kai said.

The pigeon hopped down anyway.

Kai drank half the coffee before he stood to pay. The card was still there. He reached for the cash and his right hand took the card first, folding it into his pocket in one practiced motion he hadn't approved or stopped.

***

Yusuf had rented the room two blocks from Lausanne station because the internet was fast and the desk was large enough for two laptops and three external drives. Everything else about it was temporary: narrow bed, pale walls, one print of a sailing boat, curtains too thin to keep out the afternoon. The room smelled faintly of dust and hot electronics.

On the desk, the public version of the archive waited in three windows.

He had been through the de-identification pass so many times that the files now felt damaged in his hands. Names first. Then dates. Then event IDs. Then the little pieces a lawyer could use to stitch a body back to a person: lane assignments, exact timestamps, location tags, internal athlete numbers. Every pass made the dataset worse science and better defense. He kept making himself take the loss.

The article form on ResearchGate sat open on the right screen. The title was long and flat and ugly in the way he wanted, the kind of engineering title civilians skipped and the right people still clicked. Under his name, the affiliation line read Former Kirchberg Institute.

He had typed Kirchberg Institute out of habit the first time and stared at it until he deleted the word.

The repositories were ready too. One held the virus-analysis files: the structural correspondences between the corruption patterns and Meridian's internal tool signatures, cleaned into a form another engineer could rerun. The other held a de-identified extract from the athlete biometrics in his archive. Not everything. Never everything. Enough to make the point that the biological data did not belong inside Meridian's walls forever.

Every file on the drive had begun in somebody else's body.

He knew that sentence too well by now. It had followed him through the calibration bay, through Doha, through the train back to Switzerland, through three nights of sleeping badly in this room. He knew what Kai would say about it. He knew what he would say back. None of that changed the work on the desk.

Yusuf checked the hashes one last time against the handwritten sheet beside the keyboard. His tea had gone cold. When he swallowed, his mouth still felt dry.

"Right," he said to the empty room. "Fine."

He made the repositories public first. Each one asked if he was sure. Each one made him confirm again. Then he clicked Publish on the article and watched the button grey out under his cursor.

Nothing happened for almost two seconds.

Then the page refreshed with the public URL.

His stomach dropped anyway.

An automated email arrived first. Your article is now live. Another from the code host. Repository visibility changed to public. Then mirror notifications from two other services he had set up the night before in case Meridian tried to bury the links by force or process. His hands were steady, which annoyed him. He would have preferred some visible sign that he'd just set the rest of his professional life on fire.

His phone buzzed on the desk with Farah's name.

He answered on video. Farah was at her kitchen table in Birmingham with a mug in both hands and the article already open on a laptop beside her. Her hair was tied back badly. She had never cared about doing that neatly for family.

"Well," she said. "That title's a hate crime."

Yusuf laughed once. "It's meant to keep the wrong people away."

"It also keeps the right people away if they're tired."

"Then the right people can have a coffee and come back."

Farah looked from the screen to him. Her speech was still clear, but there was that half-beat now before some words, the tiny delay that made him want to climb through the camera and drag time backward with both hands. "Is it up properly?"

"Yeah. Properly public."

"So they can use it."

"They can rerun the signature analysis. They can inspect the archive extract. They can work without asking Meridian for permission first."

"Can they work on mine?"

He looked at the article instead of at her. Technical redirection came to him as easily as breathing. "They can work on Voss-Kessler correlations without Meridian's licensing gate in the middle. They can test across a real comparative set. They can at least start."

Farah let that sit for a second. "That's not the same answer."

Yusuf rubbed the heel of his hand against his mouth. "No."

"Try again."

He looked back at her. "I don't know if it helps you. Not directly. Not now. It might make it possible for somebody else to help later who couldn't even get in the room before."

"So maybe."

"Yeah."

She smiled a little, tired and not fake. "You hate maybe."

"Today I'm charging it rent."

Farah laughed, then coughed into her wrist and took a sip from the mug. When she spoke again her voice was softer. "They'll come after you."

"Yes."

"You didn't put it out anonymously."

"Anonymous would've made it easier to dismiss."

"And easier on you."

"Probably."

She shifted in the chair. For a moment she lost the word she wanted, and he watched her find another route around it the way she always had. "I don't know what to say that isn't either thank you or sorry."

"Then don't do either one."

"All right." She lifted the mug slightly. "Eat, then. You look awful."

"That's very loving."

"I'm being serious."

"I know."

Another call flashed on the screen: Petra Engel.

Farah saw his eyes move. "Take it."

"I can call her back."

"Take it, Yusuf."

He nodded. "I'll ring you later."

"Do that. And tell Lena I'm not dead just because you went full whistleblower."

"Very reassuring message."

"Use your own wording, then."

He ended the call and answered Petra on audio.

"It's public," she said without greeting.

"That was the intention."

"Meridian legal contacted Kirchberg twelve minutes ago."

"Slow of them."

Petra ignored that. "They are alleging theft of medical data, breach of confidentiality, and sabotage of proprietary systems. The Federal Office of Sport has opened a preliminary inquiry and requested the publication materials. The European Athletics Enhancement Authority wants to know whether additional files exist."

Yusuf sat down again. "Of course they do."

"I told them they could review what is already public and make formal requests for anything else."

He closed his eyes once. "Thank you."

"Do not thank me yet." Papers moved on her end; he could hear them. "Is the de-identification conservative?"

"Yes."

"That is not an emotional question."

"I know. Yes. It's conservative. Names, dates, event IDs, internal numbers, location traces. Anything that could directly relink on a first pass is gone."

"On a first pass."

"If someone with internal Meridian access and enough time wants to play clever games, they can argue probabilities. They'd do that anyway."

Petra was quiet for a beat. "Have you preserved the source chain?"

"Three encrypted mirrors. Two public mirrors. Checksums on paper and offline."

"Good. Do not alter the repositories now unless a mirror fails. If you touch timestamps after the inquiry request, they will use that."

"I know."

"Also, you need counsel."

He laughed without humor. "With what money?"

"I am sending a name. Use it."

The line went quieter for a second. When Petra spoke again, some of the institutional metal had gone out of her voice. "Did you tell your sister?"

"Just now."

"And?"

He looked at the open article, the public URL, the download counter already climbing by single digits. "Same answer as before. Maybe."

"That may be the only honest one." Petra paused. "Stay reachable."

"All right."

He ended the call and sat with the phone in his hand until the screen went dark. Then it lit again with alerts stacked one over another.

*NZZ Breaking: Meridian BioSystems falls 11% after published firmware analysis*

*Swiss Federal Office of Sport opens preliminary inquiry into Meridian-linked calibration corruption*

*Comment on your repository: can you provide verification hashes for dataset B?*

The room stayed exactly as cheap and temporary as it had been ten minutes earlier. The sailing boat on the wall kept sailing nowhere.

His phone went on vibrating across the desk, the next call arriving before the last alert had cleared.

***

The treatment facility was in Schwabing, in a converted Jugendstil house on a street lined with linden trees that smelled like August. The house had high ceilings and decorative plaster molding and a garden out back where patients were encouraged to sit in good weather. The art on the walls was bland in three approved ways: watercolors of lakes, a photograph of mountains that could have been any mountains, a print of a Klee hung slightly crooked and left that way for all six weeks Margot had been here.

It had been six weeks without the feed. Forty-two days. There was no accumulating pride in it, no sense that each day was another brick in a wall between her and the substance. She counted as she'd counted laps: because number was information, and information was what she had instead of certainty.

The program was called NeuroBalance, and it was one of three facilities in Germany that specialized in neural-interface addiction. Most of the other patients were feed-dependent, people with compulsive relationships to entertainment-grade sensory interfaces, immersive sports simulations and full-body relaxation feeds marketed as wellness tools and functioning as digital opiates. The feeds they described were crude by comparison. They had plugged into recordings. Margot had plugged into people.

On Thursday she tried to explain this to Helga. Helga was forty-three, Bavarian, precise in her English, grammatically careful and slightly over-enunciated. Good therapist or not, Helga did not understand what Margot was telling her.

"The entertainment feeds are recorded," Margot said. Across from Helga's desk, with its small succulent and framed photo of a dog, Margot sat back in the chair and looked at the plant instead of the woman. "Recorded and processed and cleaned up. Like a movie of a body. What I did was live. I was inside someone's actual nervous system while they were using it."

"Yes, you've said this." Helga's pen rested on her notepad. "Several of our patients describe a similar intensity with the immersive feeds. The sense of being inside another—"

"It's not similar."

Helga waited.

"Those feeds are recordings. They're designed to feel like a body. What I felt was a body." Margot's jaw worked. "There's no version of this where you compare it to Stefan on his entertainment feed eighteen hours a day and I nod along."

Helga wrote a note. Her pen moved across the notepad with steady pressure. "I'm not equating the experiences. I want to discuss your six-week review."

Margot waited.

"Based on our sessions, I'm recommending we move you to the standard craving-management track. Topiramate, daily group, same structure we use for all patients at this stage."

"That's what Stefan's on."

"It's what all patients are on at six weeks."

Margot looked at the succulent on Helga's desk. It was the kind that didn't need much water, which was probably the point of keeping it on a therapist's desk. "Stefan plugged into a sports sim eighteen hours a day. You're giving me the same pill."

"The pharmacological approach targets reward pathways broadly. The specific input—"

"The input was a person." Margot's jaw tightened. "When you plug in, the first thing you feel is their—" She stopped. She'd said this before. Five weeks of sessions and she'd polished it into something that sounded like a sales pitch for the feed. "It doesn't matter how I describe it. You're hearing addiction and filing it in the same fucking box."

"I'm hearing someone in significant distress," Helga said. "The protocol is designed to help with that distress."

"It's designed for people who were watching recordings."

"Margot—"

"I was plugged into a twenty-one-year-old's nervous system when his spine broke. I felt his legs go dead. And I want the part before that back more than I want him to walk." She looked at her hands. Her thumbs were pressed white against each other. "That's not your reward pathway."

Helga set the pen down. "The differentiation you're describing — the insistence that your experience is categorically different from other patients' — is itself a pattern we see at this stage. It can be a form of—"

"Resistance. Yeah." Margot's voice went flat. "I know what that word means."

"It doesn't mean you're wrong about the experience. It means the treatment framework still applies."

Margot was quiet. The wanting was there, same as it was every Thursday in this chair, same size it had been on day one. It just sat there. Like furniture.

Helga wrote the protocol recommendation on the notepad. Margot watched the pen move.

"We'll start the adjusted protocol Monday," Helga said. "If it isn't the right fit, we adjust."

"Sure."

Margot stood up because the session was over and because Helga had just decided to medicate the wrong addiction, and there was nothing more to say about it that wouldn't sound like confirmation.

After group, four people in a circle talking about triggers and coping strategies, during which Margot said reasonable things about mindfulness techniques she didn't believe in and made eye contact with a twenty-six-year-old named Stefan who was trembling through withdrawal from an entertainment feed he'd used eighteen hours a day for two years, Margot walked.

By the time she reached Leopoldstraße, the muscles along her jaw ached from clenching. Stefan's shaking hands had kicked the wanting loose in her. Her own fingers kept closing around the empty air above her right wrist where the feed band had sat for years, ugly little grasping motions she didn't notice until after they happened. Saliva kept flooding her mouth the way it used to in the minute before she plugged in. She spat into the gutter outside a bakery and kept walking.

August in Munich was warm, not hot. The lindens on Leopoldstraße were in full leaf, and the air carried that late-summer smell of heated pavement and green and the distant mineral note of the Isar. South through Schwabing she walked, past the university, past cafes where students sat outside with laptops and beer in the late afternoon, past the Siegestor with its stone horses catching the low light. In jeans and a T-shirt and running shoes, she looked like anyone. That was the thing about it. Nobody on this street could see that the woman walking past the monument was carrying the precise sensory memory of another person's spinal cord going silent, or that she was trying not to run her tongue over her teeth like a dog waiting for a bowl to hit the floor.

By the time she reached the Englischer Garten, she'd ended up at the Eisbach again, though she hadn't meant to come there today. The Eisbach was a channel of the Isar, and at one narrowing the current formed a standing wave that surfers had been riding for decades. It had become her place by the second week, and she kept coming back.

There were four surfers today. Young, most of them. One woman in a wetsuit despite the warm air, her board shorter than standard, shaped for the wave's geometry. The wave was maybe a meter high, clean, glassy in the late light. The surfers dropped in one at a time from the bank, a sideways leap, the board angling to catch the current, then the rapid adjustment of weight and balance on a wave that never moved forward. The whole thing was stationary surfing. The wave held and the surfer held and the river kept moving underneath them.

The woman in the wetsuit was good. On the wave she cut back and forth with efficient fluidity, weight drops, ankle adjustments, the continuous proprioceptive reading of a moving surface. There was nothing enhanced or linked about her. It was just muscle and board and river and the accumulated hours that made the motion look easy. Her weight sank into a bottom turn and her arm swept out for balance and her torso rotated and for a moment the line of her body was a clean functional expression of physics and intention and practice.

Margot could see the biomechanics plainly: quadricep engagement, dorsiflexion, counter-rotation of the hips. Fifteen years of reading bodies had done that to her. The movement almost reached her, dimly, like music from another room, because the neural pathways the feed had built were still there, still listening for input that wasn't coming. Each time the surfer dropped in, Margot's calves tightened in answer and released uselessly. She moved down the rail for a cleaner angle, then another step, as if sightline had anything to do with it.

Her shoe slid on the wet stone at the edge. A man waiting with a board under one arm caught her elbow before she went farther.

"Careful," he said.

"I'm fine," Margot said, too fast.

He let go of her arm. She stepped back onto dry ground and hated the cold little burst of relief in her chest that had come from being touched by someone coming off the wave.

On the bank, Margot watched. The surfers rode the wave and climbed out and rode it again. The woman in the wetsuit finally lost her line and splashed into the current and hauled herself out laughing, water streaming off her suit, her board tucked under one arm. She shook her hair and called to the guy waiting to drop in and he laughed too and the sound carried across the water in the warm air. The laugh tightened Margot's throat. Not concern. Hunger.

Margot watched until the light changed and the surfers climbed out one by one and walked away with their boards under their arms and the standing wave kept going, the water folding over itself as it always did whether anyone was riding it or not. Twice on the walk back she took her phone out of her pocket and stared at the black screen before shoving it away again.

At almost dinner, Margot went up the steps of the old house and through the front hall to the evening nurse's desk. She set the phone on the wood between them.

"Keep this till morning," she said.

The nurse looked up from her paperwork. "You can keep it."

"I know." Margot pushed it an inch farther across the desk. "Keep it anyway."

***

The pool at the Centre Sportif was twenty-five meters, four lanes, and low-ceilinged enough that humidity stained the concrete. The air held chlorine, the slap of flip turns, and bubbles on the exhale. It was not Kirchberg's silent fifty-meter pool. A retired man did breaststroke with his head carefully above water while two teenagers in the far lane tried to splash each other without admitting that was what they were doing.

Lena swam. The stroke was clean but not optimized. Her catch slipped a fraction on entry. The cost was two seconds per hundred meters, and she still timed every session.

This was her sixth two-hundred. Every third stroke she turned to breathe and felt herself choosing it: head, air, back to streamline. The quiet in her head was not silence anymore. It was just her own mind, messy and associative, with the occasional half-second blank where a word should have been. By July she'd stopped calling that frightening. It was ordinary now.

Halfway through the fifth fifty, the left arm smoothed. It wasn't a blackout. It wasn't full desync. It was just the old correction surfacing for less than a second, the stroke going cleaner than she'd asked for. Lena felt it and stopped at the wall.

At the gutter she waited, breathing hard, while water slapped tile in the chopped rhythm of the small pool. The smoothness didn't come back.

This was what the patch had given her: margin. She could tell the difference soon enough to stop. Then she could push off again under her own imperfect catch and take the lost two seconds as the price of being there for it.

At the end of the set, the coach at the Montreux center was still in the bleachers with a book about Mediterranean cooking and a clipboard. Isabelle did not micromanage. At the start of each session she gave Lena a set and looked up only to mark times. The port at the base of Lena's skull had never prompted a question. Lena suspected Isabelle thought it was cosmetic.

"Good set," Isabelle said, not looking up from the book. "One-oh-nine on the last one."

"Thanks."

"You want another?"

"No, I'm done."

Isabelle made a note on her clipboard. Lena climbed out and stood on the deck, water running off her suit onto the concrete. The retired breaststroke man was still going, neck tight with the effort of keeping his head above the surface. The teenagers had given up swimming entirely and were sitting on the edge of the far lane arguing over a video on one of their phones.

In the changing room, Lena sat on the bench in her suit, dripping, and read the article on her phone.

The title was still long and ugly. Yusuf's name sat under it, then the former-affiliation line, then the two public repositories and the alert about Meridian dropping eleven percent. The first link was the firmware analysis. The second was the archive extract. De-identified. Public. Too much now for Meridian to bury under one careful statement.

Lena texted Yusuf. Just: *Saw it.*

His reply came four minutes later: *Yeah. Farah says hi.*

Before Lena could answer, another message arrived.

*Also don't bin any post-patch logs. Petra says if Meridian goes after the archive they'll want athlete-side chain as well.*

Lena looked at the mesh pocket of her bag. The waterproof notebook was still there beside her goggles, swollen a little from damp hands and chlorine.

*Where are you?* she typed.

He sent the hotel name and the street by Lausanne station.

Lena toweled off. The towel caught on the small circular port at the base of her skull. She touched it with two fingers, the absent check of a thing that was part of her body but not her.

Dressed, she walked out into August in Montreux. The lake was enormous and flat and the sun was on it and the air smelled like warm stone and the jasmine that grew along the promenade railing near the station. Her hair was still damp from the pool and the chlorine followed her, same as it had when she was four in a calibration pool in Zug, before she knew the system in her head was a system. The smell was the same. The Lausanne train was already waiting when she reached the platform. Lena climbed in with her bag damp against her hip, pulled the notebook out before the doors shut, and held it in her lap all the way along the lake.

The hotel was two blocks from the station. Kai opened the door on her second knock.

"Hi," he said.

"Hi."

He stepped aside. The room smelled of dust, hot electronics, and station takeaway. A paper bag sat open on the bed beside him. On the desk, Yusuf had three windows open, two drives plugged in, and alerts still repopulating faster than he could clear them. The print of the sailing boat was still on the wall above it all.

"You came," Yusuf said.

"You needed the times."

She took the notebook from her bag and handed it over. "Montreux from June. Dates, splits, when I felt the correction start. I marked it."

Yusuf took the notebook and flipped once through the damp pages. "Right. Good. Thanks."

Kai nudged the paper bag toward her with the back of his hand. "There's food."

Lena set her swim bag on the floor. "What happens now?"

Yusuf put the notebook beside the hash sheet. "Petra's lawyer says Meridian will try to freeze the repositories by morning. If the inquiry's going to treat the release as more than stolen files, they want athlete-side chain too. Post-patch times. Post-Doha statements. Bodies attached to the data."

Lena nodded once. "Use mine."

Kai looked at the floor for a second, then back at Yusuf. "If they ask about Doha, I'll talk."

His phone lit again with the Geneva number Petra had sent. Yusuf cleared a space on the desk between the notebook and the paper checksums, set the phone there, and answered on speaker.
"Yusuf Qadir," he said. He glanced at Kai, then at Lena. "I've got Kai Brennan and Lena Morozova here too."

A woman's voice came through the speaker at once, clipped and professional. Yusuf reached for a pen. Kai stayed by the bed with the paper bag open beside him, his left wrist turning once before he set it still again. Lena stood barefoot on the thin hotel carpet with chlorine drying on her skin and listened to the station announcements drift up from the street below, the notebook open on the desk beside the checksums, all of it ugly and ordinary and public now.
